Defining Ibn al-Sabil: The Stranded Traveler as a Zakat Recipient
Defining ibn al-sabil and answering a conceptual objection

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define ibn al-sabil and distinguish his eligibility for zakat from the absolute poor.

Core Idea
The wayfarer is rich at home but poor in hand — a unique duality for zakat eligibility.

This passage from Al-Bahr al-Raiq clarifies who exactly is meant by 'ibn al-sabil' in the context of zakat recipients. It addresses a common confusion: how can a person be both 'rich' and 'poor'? The answer lies in the distinction between wealth location and immediate need.

Guided Path
1
Identify the key definition
The passage begins by defining ibn al-sabil as one cut off from his wealth due to distance. Recognize the term and its literal meaning - 'son of the road'.
2
Analyze the dual status
He is 'ghani bi makanih' (rich in his place, so zakat is due on his wealth) but 'faqir yadan' (poor in hand, so he can receive zakat). Understand that this is a legal fiction for zakat distribution.
3
Examine the objection and response
The commentator raises a logical objection: if a stranded fighter with no wealth is just poor, how are there seven categories? The response: he is poor, but with an additional quality (qayd) of being stranded in obedience, making him distinct.
4
Note the source authority
The ruling is supported by references to al-Kafi, al-Nihaya, and later works. This shows the Hanafi reliance on earlier authoritative texts.
5
Distinguish from absolute poor
Ibn al-sabil is not simply a poor person; the traveling and being cut off are essential. This distinction affects how zakat is prioritized.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (وابن السبيل) هو المنقطع عن ماله لبعده عنه والسبيل الطريق فكل من يكون مسافراً يسمى ابن السبيل وهو غني بمكانه حتى تجب الزكاة في ماله، ويؤمر بالأداء إذا وصلت إليه يده وهو فقير يداً حتى تصرف إليه الصدقة في الحال لحاجته، كذا في الكافي. فإن قلت : منقطع الغزاة أو الحج إن لم يكن في وطنه مال فهو فقير وإلا فهو ابن السبيل، فكيف تكون الأقسام سبعة؟ قلت هو فقير إلا أنه زاد عليه بالانقطاع في عبادة الله تعالى فكان مغايراً للفقير المطلق الخالي عن هذا القيد كذا في النهاية. وفي الظهيرية : الاستقراض لابن السبيل خير من قبول الصدقة. وفي فتح القدير ولا يحل له أن يأخذ أكثر من حاجته وألحق به كل من هو غائب عن ماله وإن كان في بلده ولا يقدر عليه إلا به . وفي المحيط : وإن كان تاجراً له دين على الناس لا يقدر على أخذه ولا يجد شيئاً يحل له أخذ الزكاة لأنه فقير يداً كابن السبيل ا هـ
His statement: (and the wayfarer) is the one cut off from his wealth due to its distance, and al-sabil is the road. So everyone who is a traveler is called ibn al-sabil. He is rich in his place so that zakat is obligatory on his wealth, and he is commanded to pay it when it reaches his hand, but he is poor in hand so that charity is given to him immediately for his need. Thus in al-Kafi. If you say: the stranded fighter or pilgrim if he has no wealth in his homeland, he is poor, otherwise he is ibn al-sabil; so how are the categories seven? I say: he is poor, except that he has the additional quality of being stranded in obedience to Allah, so he differs from the absolute poor devoid of this restriction. Thus in al-Nihaya. And in al-Zahiriyyah: borrowing for the wayfarer is better than accepting charity. And in Fath al-Qadir: it is not permissible for him to take more than his need. And annexed to him is everyone who is absent from his wealth even if he is in his own town but cannot access it except through it. And in al-Muhit: if he is a merchant who has debts on people that he cannot collect and finds nothing, it is permissible for him to take zakat because he is poor in hand like ibn al-sabil. End.
Source pages p. 422

Commentary
This passage defines ibn al-sabil (wayfarer) as a traveler cut off from his wealth. He is considered rich at home (zakat is due on his wealth) but poor in hand (can receive zakat for immediate need). The commentator then addresses an objection: if a stranded pilgrim or fighter without wealth is just a poor person, how are there seven distinct categories? The answer: such a person is poor but with the additional quality of being stranded in obedience, making him different. Further rulings: it is better for the wayfarer to borrow than to accept charity; he cannot take more than his need; the ruling extends to anyone whose wealth is inaccessible even if in his own town. A merchant unable to collect debts due to the debtor's absence is also like ibn al-sabil.

Key Terms
ابن السبيل (ibn al-sabil): A traveler who is cut off from his wealth and needs financial assistance.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what makes ibn al-sabil distinct from the absolute poor (al-faqir al-mutlaq)?
A) He is traveling
B) He is stranded in obedience to Allah
C) He has no wealth at all
D) He is rich at home

Key Takeaway
The wayfarer is a unique zakat recipient because he is both rich (at home) and poor (in hand), and his being stranded in obedience distinguishes him from the absolute poor.

Rulings for the Wayfarer: Borrowing, Limits, and Analogies
Rulings and analogical extensions for the wayfarer

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the rulings for the wayfarer's receipt of zakat, including the preference for borrowing, the limit of need, and the analogical extension to other cases.

Core Idea
The wayfarer should borrow rather than take charity, and cannot take more than his immediate need.

After defining ibn al-sabil, the passage provides key rulings. The wayfarer is encouraged to seek a loan before accepting zakat, and he cannot take more than his need. The ruling is extended to anyone whose wealth is inaccessible, even if in his own town, and to merchants unable to collect debts. These extensions demonstrate the Hanafi use of analogy (ilhāq) in zakat distribution.

Guided Path
1
Extract the primary ruling on borrowing
The passage from al-Zahiriyya states 'الاستقراض لابن السبيل خير من قبول الصدقة'. Understand that borrowing is better than accepting charity, though charity is allowed.
2
Note the limitation on amount
From Fath al-Qadir, 'لا يحل له أن يأخذ أكثر من حاجته'. The wayfarer can only take what is necessary for his journey back or to his wealth.
3
Analyze the first analogy (ilhāq)
The ruling extends to anyone absent from his wealth, even if in his own town, as long as he cannot access it. Identify the common cause: 'inaccessibility of wealth'.
4
Examine the second analogy (merchant with uncollectible debts)
From al-Muhit, a merchant with debts he cannot collect is considered poor in hand like ibn al-sabil. Note the condition: he cannot find anything and cannot collect.
5
Synthesize the underlying principle
The core principle is 'faqir yadan' (poor in hand). Anyone who is in immediate need despite having wealth elsewhere is eligible for zakat. The analogy works through the shared illa: temporary inability to access wealth.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (وابن السبيل) هو المنقطع عن ماله لبعده عنه والسبيل الطريق فكل من يكون مسافراً يسمى ابن السبيل وهو غني بمكانه حتى تجب الزكاة في ماله، ويؤمر بالأداء إذا وصلت إليه يده وهو فقير يداً حتى تصرف إليه الصدقة في الحال لحاجته، كذا في الكافي. فإن قلت : منقطع الغزاة أو الحج إن لم يكن في وطنه مال فهو فقير وإلا فهو ابن السبيل، فكيف تكون الأقسام سبعة؟ قلت هو فقير إلا أنه زاد عليه بالانقطاع في عبادة الله تعالى فكان مغايراً للفقير المطلق الخالي عن هذا القيد كذا في النهاية. وفي الظهيرية : الاستقراض لابن السبيل خير من قبول الصدقة. وفي فتح القدير ولا يحل له أن يأخذ أكثر من حاجته وألحق به كل من هو غائب عن ماله وإن كان في بلده ولا يقدر عليه إلا به . وفي المحيط : وإن كان تاجراً له دين على الناس لا يقدر على أخذه ولا يجد شيئاً يحل له أخذ الزكاة لأنه فقير يداً كابن السبيل ا هـ
His statement: (and the wayfarer) is the one cut off from his wealth due to its distance, and al-sabil is the road. So everyone who is a traveler is called ibn al-sabil. He is rich in his place so that zakat is obligatory on his wealth, and he is commanded to pay it when it reaches his hand, but he is poor in hand so that charity is given to him immediately for his need. Thus in al-Kafi. If you say: the stranded fighter or pilgrim if he has no wealth in his homeland, he is poor, otherwise he is ibn al-sabil; so how are the categories seven? I say: he is poor, except that he has the additional quality of being stranded in obedience to Allah, so he differs from the absolute poor devoid of this restriction. Thus in al-Nihaya. And in al-Zahiriyyah: borrowing for the wayfarer is better than accepting charity. And in Fath al-Qadir: it is not permissible for him to take more than his need. And annexed to him is everyone who is absent from his wealth even if he is in his own town but cannot access it except through it. And in al-Muhit: if he is a merchant who has debts on people that he cannot collect and finds nothing, it is permissible for him to take zakat because he is poor in hand like ibn al-sabil. End.
Source pages p. 422

Commentary
This passage defines ibn al-sabil (wayfarer) as a traveler cut off from his wealth. He is considered rich at home (zakat is due on his wealth) but poor in hand (can receive zakat for immediate need). The commentator then addresses an objection: if a stranded pilgrim or fighter without wealth is just a poor person, how are there seven distinct categories? The answer: such a person is poor but with the additional quality of being stranded in obedience, making him different. Further rulings: it is better for the wayfarer to borrow than to accept charity; he cannot take more than his need; the ruling extends to anyone whose wealth is inaccessible even if in his own town. A merchant unable to collect debts due to the debtor's absence is also like ibn al-sabil.

Key Terms
ابن السبيل (ibn al-sabil): A traveler who is cut off from his wealth and needs financial assistance.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Al-Zahiriyya, what is better for the wayfarer than accepting zakat?
A) Begging
B) Borrowing
C) Waiting for his wealth
D) Selling his belongings

Key Takeaway
The wayfarer should borrow before taking zakat, take only his need, and the ruling extends to anyone with inaccessible wealth.

Prohibited Recipients of Zakat and the Student of Knowledge Exception
Listing prohibited recipients and discussing the student of knowledge exception

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state which recipients are prohibited from receiving zakat (e.g., mosque, burial, ascendants/descendants) and explain the Hanafi exception for a student of knowledge based on analogy to the zakat worker.

Core Idea
Zakat cannot be given to mosques, for burial, or to ascendants/descendants; but a devoted student of knowledge may take it even if wealthy.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Raiq clarifies two key points: first, an explicit list from al-Mabsut of recipients to whom zakat cannot be paid, and second, a fatwa permitting zakat for a student of knowledge even if he is wealthy but devoted to learning. The reasoning extends the principle that allowed paying zakat to the wealthy zakat worker, because both have devoted themselves to a religious service that prevents earning. Understanding these restrictions ensures zakat is directed to the proper individuals.

Guided Path
1
Identify the prohibited recipients
Locate the list from al-Mabsut: mosque, shrouding the dead, paying the deceased's debt, buying a slave to free, and one's ascendants or descendants (even if low). Note that these are not individual eligible recipients.
The list includes institutions and family members who cannot receive zakat under Hanafi law.
2
Understand the exception for the student of knowledge
The passage cites fatwas that a student of knowledge who devotes himself to learning and teaching may take zakat even if wealthy, by analogy to the zakat worker (who may be paid zakat despite wealth because he is occupied with collection).
The analogy relies on the illa (effective cause) of being occupied with a religious service that prevents earning.
3
Note the verbal dispute and decisive ruling
The commentator notes that the disagreement about makatib is verbal; all agree that eligible recipients must be poor except the worker. The stranded pilgrim is given by consensus. The mufta bihi for the student is permissibility when needy and devoted.
The source Jami' al-Fatawa and al-Waqi'at support this fatwa.
4
Apply the ruling to modern scenarios
Zakat cannot be given to build a mosque or for funeral expenses. It must be given to eligible individuals. A student of knowledge who could work but chooses to study may receive zakat if needy.
This application ensures compliance with Hanafi fiqh.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (وقد قالوا إنه) أي دفع الزكاة قوله : (فحينئذ لا تظهر ثمرته في الزكاة قال في النهر والخلف لفظي للاتفاق على أن الأصناف كلهم سوى العامل يعطون بشرط الفقر فمنقطع الحاج يعطي اتفاقاً ا الغفار بعد ذكره ما مر عن البدائع من تعليل حل الدفع للعامل الغني بأنه فرغ نفسه لهذا العمل فيحتاج إلى الكفاية الخ . قال : وبهذا التعليل يقوي ما نسب إلى بعض الفتاوى أن طالب العلم يجوز له أن يأخذ الزكاة وإن كان غنياً إذا فرغ نفسه لإفادة العلم واستفادته لكونه عاجزاً : عن الكسب والحاجة داعية إلى ما لا بد منه، وهكذا رأيته بخط موثوق و عزاه إلى الواقعات والله تعالى أعلم ا هـ . قلت : وقد رأيته أيضاً في جامع الفتاوى معزياً إلى المبسوط ونصه وفي المبسوط : لا يجوز دفع الزكاة إلى مسجد وتكفين ميت وقضاء دينه وشراء من يعتق وأصله وإن علا وفرعه وإن سفل من النساء يداً كابن السبيل ا هـ . وهو أولى من جعله غارماً كما في فتح القدير، وقد قدمنا في بحث الفقير تفصيلاً له فراجعه
His statement: (and they said that) meaning payment of zakat. His statement: (then its fruit does not appear in zakat) He said in al-Nahr: the dispute is verbal because of agreement that all categories except the worker are given on condition of poverty, so the stranded pilgrim is given by agreement. After he mentioned what passed from al-Bada'i about the reasoning for permitting payment to the wealthy worker because he has devoted himself to this work so he needs sufficiency, etc. He said: by this reasoning is strengthened what is attributed to some fatwas that a student of knowledge is permitted to take zakat even if wealthy, when he devotes himself to teaching and learning, because he is unable to earn and need necessitates what is necessary. And thus I saw it in a trusted handwriting and attributed to al-Waqi'at. Allah knows best. End. I said: And I also saw it in Jami' al-Fatawa attributed to al-Mabsut, and its text: It is not permissible to pay zakat to a mosque, for shrouding a dead person, for paying his debt, for buying a slave to free, and to his ascendant even if high, and his descendant even if low, and from women as a hand like ibn al-sabil. End. And it is more appropriate to consider him as a gharim as in Fath al-Qadir, and we have previously given detail in the discussion of the poor, so refer to it.
Source pages p. 422

Commentary
This passage discusses two main issues. First, the comment on 'وقد قالوا إنه' and 'فحينئذ لا تظهر' relates to the earlier ruling about makatib (slave contracting manumission). The commentator clarifies that the difference of opinion is verbal because all categories require poverty except the worker. The stranded pilgrim is given by consensus. Then he uses the analogy of the worker to support fatwas that a student of knowledge can take zakat even if wealthy, because he devotes himself to learning and cannot earn. Second, the commentator quotes from al-Mabsut that zakat cannot be paid to: a mosque, for shrouding a dead person, for paying the debt of the deceased, for buying a slave to free, or to one's ascendants or descendants (even if low), and to women as a hand like ibn al-sabil? The text is unclear but indicates these are prohibited uses. He then notes that considering such a person as a gharim (debtor) is better, as in Fath al-Qadir.

Key Terms
المكاتب (al-mukatib): A slave who has made a contract of manumission with his master, paying installments.
طالب العلم (talib al-ilm): A student of religious knowledge who devotes himself to learning and teaching.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to al-Mabsut, which of the following is NOT a permissible use of zakat?
A) Giving to a student of knowledge who is needy
B) Giving to a mosque
C) Giving to a stranded pilgrim
D) Giving to a poor person

Key Takeaway
Zakat is strictly for eligible individuals; giving to institutions or for funeral expenses is prohibited, but a student of knowledge devoted to learning may receive zakat by analogy to the zakat worker.

Paying Zakat to One Category or Individual
Proving permissibility of paying zakat to a single category or person

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on paying zakat to one category or individual, cite the Quranic and Sunnah evidence, and explain the linguistic argument based on the definite plural.

Core Idea
Zakat can be given to one category or even one person — the definite plural indicates genus, not universal distribution.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq clarifies a common misconception: that zakat must be distributed among all eight categories. The Hanafi school permits concentrating zakat on one category or even one individual. The author provides Quranic evidence, Sunnah practice, and a linguistic principle about the definite plural to support this ruling.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling
State the Hanafi position: is it permissible to pay zakat to only one category (e.g., the poor) or to only one person? What is the hukm level?
The ruling is mubah (permissible), not mandatory to distribute among all.
2
Locate the textual evidence
Find the Quranic verse (2:271) and the prophetic practice cited. How do these prove the permissibility of giving to one category?
Verse 2:271 mentions only 'al-fuqara', not all eight categories. The Prophet gave to one category at a time.
3
Analyze the linguistic argument
Understand the Hanafi principle that the definite plural (الجمع المعرف باللام) is a metaphor for the genus. Why does this support giving to one person?
Universal distribution (every zakat to every poor person) is impossible; thus the plural indicates the genus, so one individual suffices.
4
Examine the analogy with oaths
The text compares zakat to oaths like 'I will not marry' or 'I will not buy slaves'. How does the same linguistic principle apply?
If one swears not to marry (using the plural), breaking the oath with one marriage shows the plural is for the genus.
5
Apply the conclusion
State the practical outcome: can one pay all zakat to a single poor person? Does this also apply to bequests?
Yes, and the same principle applies to bequests like 'to Zayd and the poor.'

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (فيدفع إلى كلهم أو إلى صنف لأن المراد بالآية بيان الأصناف التي بجوز الدفع إليهم لا تعيين الدفع لهم، ويدل له من الكتاب قوله تعالى ﴿وإن تخفوها وتؤتوها الفقراء فهو خير لكم﴾ [البقرة : ٢٧١] ومن السنة أنه عليه الصلاة والسلام أتاه مال الصدقة فجعله في صنف واحد وهم المؤلفة قلوبهم ثم أتاه مال آخر فجعله في الغارمين، ولم يصرح في الكتاب بجواز الاقتصار على شخص واحد من صنف واحد، ولا شك فيه عندنا لأن الجمع المعرف باللام مجاز عن الجنس ولهذا لو حلف لا يتزوج ولا يشتري العبيد يحنث بالواحد فالمعنى في الآية أن جنس الزكاة لجنس الفقير فيجوز الصرف إلى واحد لأن الاستغراق ليس بمستقيم إذ يصير المعنى أن كل صدقة لكل فقير ولا يرد خالعني على ما في يدي من الدراهم ولا شيء في يدها فإنه يلزمها ثلاثة. ولو حلف لا يكلمه الأيام أو الشهور يقع على العشرة عنده، وعلى الأسبوع والسنة عندهما لأنه أمكن العهد فلا يحمل على الجنس، فالحاصل أن حمل الجمع على الجنس مجاز، وعلى العهد أو الاستغراق حقيقة، ولا مسوغ للخلف إلا عند تعذر الأصل، وعلى هذا تنصف الموصى به لزيد والفقراء كالوصية لزيد وفقير
His statement: (so he may pay to all of them or to one category) because the intended meaning of the verse is the clarification of the categories to which payment is permitted, not the specification of payment to them. Evidence for this from the Book is His statement the Exalted: 'And if you conceal them and give them to the poor, it is better for you' [2:271]. And from the Sunnah: that the Prophet (peace be upon him) was brought charity and he placed it in one category, the reconciled hearts, then another charity came and he placed it in the debtors. And the Book does not explicitly permit limiting to one person from one category, but there is no doubt about it with us because the definite plural is a metaphor for the genus. Hence, if one swears 'I will not marry' or 'I will not buy slaves', he breaks his oath with one. So the meaning of the verse is that the genus of zakat is for the genus of the poor, so paying to one is permissible because universal quantification is not correct since that would mean every charity for every poor person. And it is not refuted by 'she divorced me for what is in my hand of dirhams when she has nothing in her hand', for then three dirhams are obligatory on her. And if one swears 'I will not speak to him for days' or 'months', it applies to ten days according to him (Abu Hanifa) and to a week or a year according to them (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) because specification is possible; so it is not taken as genus. So the conclusion: taking the plural as genus is a metaphor, and as specification or universal quantification is literal, and there is no justification for the metaphor except when the literal is impossible. And upon this, the bequest to Zayd and the poor is divided like the bequest to Zayd and a poor person.
Source pages p. 423

Commentary
This passage proves that zakat can be paid to one category (e.g., only the poor) or even one individual from a category. The verse on zakat only lists categories, not that each must be given. Evidence: Quran verse 2:271 mentions giving to the poor generally, not all categories. The Prophet (peace be upon him) gave to one category (reconciled hearts) at a time. The linguistic analysis: the definite plural (e.g., ‘al-fuqara’) in Arabic indicates the genus, not universal distribution. Analogies: swearing not to marry (breaks with one marriage), swearing not to buy slaves (breaks with one slave). For time expressions like ‘days’, it is contextual. The author concludes that paying to one is permissible unless literal universal meaning is intended, which is impossible here. This applies to bequests as well.

Key Terms
الجمع المعرف باللام (al-jam' al-mu'arraf bi al-lam): A plural noun with the definite article 'al', which in Arabic can indicate the genus, a specific group, or universal distribution.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the primary Quranic evidence cited in this passage for the permissibility of paying zakat to a single category?
A) Quran 9:60 listing eight categories
B) Quran 2:271 which mentions 'the poor' without specifying other categories
C) Quran 2:267 about spending surplus
D) Quran 2:219 about charity

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school permits paying all zakat to a single category (e.g., only the poor) or even a single individual, based on Quranic, Sunnah, and linguistic evidence.

Ruling on Giving Zakat and Other Charities to Dhimmis
Ruling with proof on zakat to dhimmis and other charities

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on giving zakat to dhimmis and distinguish it from other charities, citing the primary evidence and noting the intra-madhhab difference.

Core Idea
Zakat is exclusively for Muslims; other charities may be given to dhimmis.

This passage from Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq addresses a common question: can zakat and other forms of charity be given to non-Muslim citizens (dhimmis)? Using the well-known hadith of Mu'adh and Quran 60:8, the Hanafi school establishes a clear distinction: zakat is prohibited for dhimmis, while voluntary and other obligatory charities (like sadaqat al-fitr and expiations) are permissible. This ruling reflects a careful balance between the specific textual evidence for zakat and the general encouragement to treat dhimmis kindly.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two rulings
The passage contains two distinct rulings: (1) zakat cannot be given to a dhimmi; (2) other charities (like sadaqat al-fitr, expiations, vows) may be given to a dhimmi. Read the Arabic carefully to see how each is introduced.
The phrase 'قوله (لا) إلى ذمي' introduces the first, and 'وصح غيرها' introduces the second.
2
Analyze the evidence for the zakat ruling
The proof is the hadith of Mu'adh: 'خذها من أغنيائهم وردها في فقرائهم' (Take it from their rich and return it to their poor). The commentator explains that the command 'ردها في فقرائهم' specifies the recipients as Muslims, thus excluding dhimmis. Note that this is not a simple 'specification of the thing negates the other' but an active command.
The hadith is mashhur (well-known) and can add to the Quran. The general Quranic command for zakat is specified by this hadith.
3
Examine the evidence for permissibility of other charities
The proof is Quran 60:8: 'Allah does not forbid you from those who do not fight you in religion...' This verse permits kindness and charity to non-fighting non-Muslims. Zakat is excluded from this general permission because of the specific hadith of Mu'adh.
The passage notes that Abu Yusuf differed regarding sadaqat al-fitr, but the majority Hanafi position allows it.
4
Understand the legal reasoning (takhsis)
The commentator defends using a solitary hadith (khabar wahid) to specify the general Quranic command for zakat. He argues that the general is already specified in part by definitive evidence (the poor harbi by verse, relatives by consensus), so the remainder can be specified by a solitary hadith. This is a standard Hanafi usul principle.
This shows the interplay between usul al-fiqh and fiqh rulings.
5
Apply the ruling to a practical scenario
Imagine a Muslim has both zakat and sadaqat al-fitr to distribute. He has a poor dhimmi neighbor. He cannot give zakat to the neighbor, but he may give sadaqat al-fitr and other voluntary charity. This distinction is crucial for correct practice.

Source Text

This passage covers two rulings. First, zakat cannot be given to a dhimmi (a non-Muslim protected citizen). Prohibition of Giving Zakat to Dhimmis
Cites hadith of Mu'adh to prove zakat cannot be given to dhimmis

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on giving zakat to dhimmis, cite its evidence from the hadith of Mu'adh, and respond to the Shafi'i objection using Hanafi usul.

Core Idea
Zakat must go to Muslim poor only — the hadith of Mu'adh specifies that the poor are from your own community.

Why can't zakat be given to a dhimmi, a non-Muslim citizen living under Muslim rule? The Quran says 'the poor' generally, but the Prophet's instruction to Mu'adh narrows it. In this passage, the commentator defends the Hanafi position by showing how a well-known hadith specifies the general Quranic command.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling and its proof
The passage states clearly: do not give zakat to a dhimmi. The proof is the hadith of Mu'adh: 'Take it from their rich and return it to their poor.'
The pronoun 'their' refers to the Muslim community.
2
Understand the objection from the opposing school
The opposing school (Shafi'i) cites the generality of Quran 9:60 which lists 'the poor' without qualification. They argue this includes poor dhimmis.
3
Examine the Hanafi response
The Hanafi response is that the general has already been specified for some groups (Harbi poor by Quran, family of the Prophet by consensus). The remainder can be specified by a khabar wahid like the hadith of Mu'adh.
This illustrates the Hanafi usul principle that ahad hadith can specify the Quran.
4
Note the cross-madhhab dispute
The practical difference (thamarah al-khilaf) is that in the Hanafi school, giving zakat to a dhimmi invalidates the payment, while in the Shafi'i school it is valid but disliked.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله (لا) إلى ذمي) أي لا تدفع إلى ذمي لحديث معاذ خذها من أغنيائهم وردها في فقرائهم لا لأن التنصيص على الشيء ينفي الحكم عماعداه بل للأمر بردها إلى فقراء المسلمين، فالصرف إلى غيرهم ترك للأمر وحديث معاذ مشهور تجوز الزيادة به على الكتاب، ولئن كان خبر واحد فالعام خص منه البعض بالدليل القطعي وهو الفقير الحربي بالآية وأصوله وفروعه بالإجماع فيخص الباقي بخبر الواحد كما عرف في الأصول
His saying: '(not) to a dhimmi' i.e., do not give zakat to a dhimmi because of the hadith of Mu'adh: 'Take it from their rich and return it to their poor.' Not because specification of something negates the ruling from what is other than it, but because of the command to return it to the poor Muslims. So spending on other than them is abandoning the command. And the hadith of Mu'adh is well-known, and it is permissible to increase upon the Quran by it. Even if it is a khabar wahid, the general has been specified from it in part by definitive evidence, namely the poor Harbi by the verse, and his ascendants and descendants by consensus; then the remainder is specified by khabar wahid as is known in usul.
Source pages p. 423

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof prohibiting the payment of zakat to a dhimmi (non-Muslim subject of the Islamic state). The proof is the famous hadith of Mu'adh ibn Jabal (may Allah be pleased with him) where the Prophet (peace be upon him) instructed him to take zakat from the rich of the Muslims and return it to their poor. The commentator argues that this command specifically restricts zakat to the Muslim poor. The passage addresses a potential objection: that the Quranic verse (9:60) lists 'the poor' generally, which might include dhimmis. The Hanafi response is that the general is already specified in some aspects by definitive evidence (the Harbi poor are excluded by consensus, and the close relatives of the Prophet are excluded by explicit prohibition), so specification of dhimmis by a khabar wahid (the hadith of Mu'adh) is methodologically sound. This is an example of how Hanafis use ahad hadith to specify the general of the Quran.

Key Terms
ذمي (dhimmi): A non-Muslim citizen of the Islamic state whose rights and duties are governed by a pact of protection (dhimmah).
خبر واحد (khabar wahid): A hadith narrated by one or a few companions that does not reach tawatur.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Hadith of Abu Dhar
B) Hadith of Mu'adh ibn Jabal
C) Hadith of Anas about categories
D) Hadith of Ibn 'Abbas

Key Takeaway
The hadith of Mu'adh specifies that zakat is for the Muslim poor, and this specification overrides the general Quranic verse in Hanafi methodology.

Permissibility of Non-Zakat Charity to Dhimmis
Ruling with evidence and intra-madhhab dispute.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on giving non-zakat obligatory charities to dhimmis and explain the evidence and intra-madhhab disagreement.

Core Idea
Only zakat is restricted to Muslims; other charities may be given to dhimmis.

This lesson clarifies a key distinction in Hanafi fiqh: while zakat cannot be given to non-Muslims, other forms of charity—both wajib (like sadaqat al-fitr) and voluntary—are permitted for dhimmis. This ruling is based on Quranic permission and is the majority Hanafi view, though Abu Yusuf dissented.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is giving non-zakat charity (wajib or voluntary) to a dhimmi. The ruling according to the Hanafi majority is permissible (mubah).
Zakat is excluded and remains restricted to Muslims.
2
Locate the dalil
The primary evidence is Quran 60:8, which permits kindness to non-hostile non-Muslims.
The verse is general and not abrogated.
3
Understand the exception for zakat
Zakat is specifically excluded by the hadith of Mu'adh, which restricts its distribution to Muslims only.
Thus, the general permission does not extend to zakat.
4
Note the intra-madhhab ikhtilaf
Abu Yusuf held that all obligatory charities (e.g., sadaqat al-fitr) are analogous to zakat and thus restricted to Muslims.
The majority rejects this analogy because zakat has specific textual restriction.
5
Determine the mufta bihi
The majority view is the relied-upon position for fatwa, as stated by al-Ramli: 'And with it we take.'
The practical consequence is that sadaqat al-fitr may be given to dhimmis.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله (وصح غيرها) أي وصح دفع غير الزكاة إلى الذمي واجباً كان أو تطوعاً كصدقة الفطر والكفارات والمنذور لقوله تعالى لا ينهاكم الله عن الذين لم يقاتلوكم في الدين﴾ [الممتحنة : ] الآية . وخصت الزكاة بحديث معاذ وفيه خلاف أبي يوسف ولا يرد عليه العشر لأن مصرفه من يملك نصاباً إلا إلى طالب العلم والغازي والمنقطع لقوله عليه السلام «يجوز دفع الزكاة لطالب العلم وإن كان له نفقة أربعين سنة ا هـ وهذا منافٍ لدعوى النهر تبعاً لفتح القدير الاتفاق تأمل قوله : (ولا يحل له أن يأخذ أكثر من حاجته أقول : تقدم عن شرح المجمع أن ابن السبيل إذا وصل إلى ماله وبقي معه شيء من مال الزكاة الذي أخذه يحل له كما يحل لمولى المكاتب الذي عجز لكن لا منافاة فإن ما هنا معناه أنه يأخذ ما يغلب على ظنه أنه قدر الحاجة لا أكثر ولا يخفى أنه مع غلبة الظن قد يفضل معه شيء فأفاد ما في المجمع أن هذا الفاضل يحل له قوله : (وفيه خلاف أبي يوسف) . أي في جواز دفع غير الزكاة إليه خلاف أبي يوسف قال الرملي في الحاوي القدسي : وبه نأخذ ووقيد بالذمي لأن جميع الصدقات . فرضاً كانت أو واجبه أو تطوعاً لا تجوز للحربي اتفاقاً كما في غاية البيان لقوله تعالى إنما ينهاكم الله عن الذين قاتلوكم في الدين﴾ [الممتحنة : ٩ ] وأطلقه فشمل المستأمن وقد صرح به في النهاية
His saying: '(and valid otherwise)' i.e., it is valid to pay other than zakat to a dhimmi, whether obligatory or voluntary, like sadaqat al-fitr, expiations, and vows, because of His saying 'Allah does not forbid you from those who do not fight you in religion' [Al-Mumtahina]. And zakat is specified by the hadith of Mu'adh. And in this there is disagreement of Abu Yusuf. And the 'ushr is not refuted by it because its recipients are those who own a nisab, except for a student of knowledge, a warrior, and a stranded traveler, because of the hadith 'It is permissible to give zakat to a student of knowledge even if he has expenses for forty years.' This contradicts the claim of an-Nahr following Fath al-Qadir of consensus. Ponder. His saying: 'And it is not lawful for him to take more than his need.' I say: It has preceded from Sharh al-Majma' that if a wayfarer reaches his wealth and something of the zakat money he took remains with him, it is lawful for him just as it is lawful for the master of a mukatib who fails, but there is no contradiction; what is meant here is that he takes what he deems likely to be sufficient for his need, no more, and it is not hidden that with probability something may remain, so what is in al-Majma' indicates that this surplus is lawful for him. His saying: 'And in it is the disagreement of Abu Yusuf.' i.e., regarding the permissibility of paying other than zakat to him (dhimmi) there is disagreement of Abu Yusuf. Al-Ramli said in al-Hawi al-Qudsi: And with it we take. And it is restricted by dhimmi because all charities, whether fard, wajib, or voluntary, are not permissible for a Harbi by agreement as in Ghayat al-Bayan due to His saying 'Allah only forbids you from those who fought you in religion' [9], and he gave it absolutely so it includes the musta'man, and this is explicitly stated in an-Nihayah.
Source pages p. 424

Commentary
This passage discusses the ruling for non-zakat charities given to dhimmis. The commentator states that obligatory charities like sadaqat al-fitr, kaffarat, and nadhr, as well as voluntary sadaqah, may be given to dhimmis, based on the Quranic verse (60:8) which permits kindness to non-fighting non-Muslims. Zakat is excluded due to the hadith of Mu'adh. There is disagreement from Abu Yusuf, who held that all obligatory charities are like zakat in restriction to Muslims; however, the relied-upon position (with al-Ramli) is permissibility for non-zakat. The passage also clarifies that for a harbi (enemy combatant) all charities are impermissible by consensus, and this includes musta'man (a non-Muslim granted safe passage). Additionally, a sub-point about the traveler (ibn al-sabil) is addressed: he may keep surplus zakat money if he later obtains wealth, based on Sharh al-Majma'.

Key Terms
صدقة الفطر (sadaqat al-fitr): Obligatory charity given at the end of Ramadan.
مستأمن (musta'man): A non-Muslim who is given temporary safe passage (aman) into Muslim territory.

Key Terms from Source Passage

C) Only if the dhimmi is a relative.
D) Only voluntary charity is allowed.

Key Takeaway
Non-zakat charities may be given to dhimmis according to the Hanafi majority, based on Quranic permission and the absence of restriction.

The Tamlik Requirement in Zakat Disbursement
Ruling with proof on tamlik requirement

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the Hanafi school prohibits the use of zakat for building mosques and shrouding the dead, and identify the legal stratagem (hila) to achieve a similar result.

Core Idea
Zakat requires tamlik (ownership transfer); using it for communal works invalidates the obligation.

This passage tackles a common misconception: that zakat can be used for building mosques or shrouding the dead. The Hanafi position is clear – such uses are invalid because they lack tamlik. However, a lawful stratagem (hila) allows the payer to achieve the same communal benefit while fulfilling the condition.

Guided Path
1
Identify the prohibited uses
List the four specific purposes mentioned: building a mosque, shrouding a deceased, paying the debt of a deceased, and buying a slave to free. Recognize that the author groups them as invalid.
These are all cases where the zakat is not placed in the ownership of a specific recipient.
2
Locate the ruling and its basis
The ruling is 'la yujzi'' (does not suffice). The basis is the absence of tamlik, stated as the pillar (rukn) for zakat disbursement.
Tamlik is a condition for validity; without it, the zakat is not discharged.
3
Examine the proofs for each case
The text provides specific proofs: (1) Shroud remains donor's property (even if beast eats the body). (2) Paying another's debt does not transfer ownership to the debtor, and if the debt is later denied, the donor recovers from the creditor. (3) Freeing a slave is an iqat (dropping of right), not tamlik.
These proofs illustrate that ownership does not transfer.
4
Distinguish the exception for living debtor
If one pays a living debtor's debt by his order, it is valid because the debtor becomes an agent to receive the zakat. The payment then constitutes tamlik to the debtor.
This shows that tamlik can be achieved indirectly through agency.
5
Understand the hila (legal stratagem)
To still benefit communal projects, give the zakat to a poor person (tamlik achieved) and then ask him to spend it on the desired purpose. The donor gets zakat reward; the poor person gets reward for the deed.
The hila preserves the essence of tamlik while achieving the intended outcome.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله ( وبناء مسجد وتكفين ميت وقضاء دينه وشراء قن يعتق) بالجر بالعطف على «ذمي» والضمير في دينه للميت، وعدم الجواز لانعدام التمليك الذي هو الركن في الأربعة لأن الكفن على ملك المتبرع حتى لو افترس الميت السبع كان الكفن للمتبرع لا لورثة الميت، وقضاء دين الغير لا يقتضي التمليك من ذلك الغير الحي فالميت أولى بدليل أنه لو قضى دين غيره ثم تصادق الدائن والمديون على عدمه رجع المتبرع على الدائن لا على المديون والإعتاق إسقاط لا تمليك . قيد بقضاء دين الميت لأنه لو قضى دين الحي إن قضاه بغير أمره يكون متبرعاً ولا يجزئه عن الزكاة ، وإن قضاه بأمره جاز . يكون القابض كالوكيل له في قبض الصدقة . كذا في غاية البيان . وقيده في النهاية بأن يكون المديون فقيراً ولا بد منه، ويستفاد منه أن رجوع المتبرع بقضاء الدين عند التصادق على الدائن محمول على ما إذا كان بغير أمر المديون، أما إذا كان بأمره فهو تمليك منه فلا رجوع عند التصادق بأنه لا دين على الدائن وإنما يرجع على المديون، وهو بعمومه يتناول ما لو دفعه ناوياً الزكاة، وينبغي أن لا رجوع فيها كما بحثه المحقق في فتح القدير فليراجع. والحيلة في الجواز في هذه الأربعة أن يتصدق بمقدار زكاته على فقير ثم يأمره بعد ذلك بالصرف إلى هذه الوجوه فيكون لصاحب المال ثواب الزكاة وللفقير ثواب هذه القرب. كذا في المحيط . وأشار المصنف إلى أنه لو أطعم يتيماً بنيتها لا يجزئه لعدم التمليك إلا إذا دفع له الطعام كالكسوة إذا كان يعقل القبض وإلا فلا ولو دفع الصغير إلى وليه كذا في الخانية. والمراد بالعقل هنا أن لا يرمى به ولا يخدع عنه
His saying: 'and building a mosque and shrouding a deceased and paying his debt and buying a slave to free' — in the genitive by conjunction with 'dhimmi'. And the pronoun in 'his debt' refers to the deceased. And the impermissibility is due to the absence of tamlik (ownership transfer) which is the pillar in these four, because the shroud is in the ownership of the donor; if a beast of prey devours the deceased, the shroud belongs to the donor, not to the heirs of the deceased. And paying the debt of another does not entail tamlik from that living person, so the deceased is more so; proof: if one pays the debt of another, and then the creditor and debtor agree on its non-existence, the donor can take back from the creditor, not from the debtor. And freeing a slave is iqat (dropping of right), not tamlik. It is restricted to paying the debt of the deceased because if one pays the debt of a living person without his order, he is a volunteer and it does not suffice for zakat; but if he pays with his order, it is permissible; the receiver becomes like his agent in receiving the charity. Thus in Ghayat al-Bayan. And an-Nihayah restricts it to the debtor being poor, which is necessary. And it is understood from it that the donor's taking back in the case of agreement is based on it being without the debtor's order; but if it were with his order, it is tamlik from him, so there is no taking back when they agree that there is no debt on the creditor, but he takes back from the debtor. And its generality includes the case where he paid intending zakat, and it should be that there is no taking back in it, as the muhaqqiq investigated in Fath al-Qadir, so refer to it. The trick (hila) for permissibility in these four is that he gives charity equal to his zakat to a poor person, then commands him to spend it on these purposes, so the owner gets the reward of zakat and the poor person gets the reward of these acts of worship. Thus in al-Muhit. And the author indicated that if he feeds an orphan with the intention (of zakat), it does not suffice due to lack of tamlik, unless he gives him the food like clothing, if the orphan has the capacity to receive; otherwise no; and if he gives to the guardian, thus in al-Khaniyah. And the meaning of 'intellect' here is that he does not throw it or be deceived.
Source pages p. 424

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof that zakat cannot be used for building mosques, shrouding the dead, paying the debts of the deceased, or buying slaves to free them, because these actions do not transfer ownership (tamlik) to a recipient, which is a necessary condition for zakat. The commentator gives evidence: the shroud remains the property of the donor; paying another's debt does not give the debtor ownership; freeing a slave is an iqat (dropping of right). However, paying the debt of a living person with his order is valid because the debtor becomes an agent. There is a subtlety about recovery of the payment if the debt turns out not to exist. The commentator then presents a legal trick (hila): give the zakat to a poor person and then instruct him to spend it on those purposes. Finally, feeding an orphan directly with the intention of zakat is invalid because no tamlik occurs unless the orphan is capable of receiving and actually receives the food. The commentator clarifies that 'capability' means the orphan does not throw it away or is easily tricked.

Key Terms
تمليك (tamlik): Transfer of ownership from the giver to the recipient, which is essential for zakat validity.
حيلة (hila): A lawful stratagem to achieve a permissible result while observing legal restrictions.
إسقاط (iqat): Dropping of a right; freeing a slave is considered iqat, not tamlik.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the primary reason that paying the debt of a deceased person from zakat is invalid?
A) The deceased is not a living recipient who can own the money.
B) Debts are not mentioned in the Quran as a zakat category.
C) The creditors may not be poor.
D) The debt may be disputed by the heirs.

Key Takeaway
Zakat must be given directly to a recipient (tamlik); uses like building mosques or shrouding the dead are invalid, but a hila (giving to a poor person first) can achieve the same outcome.

Zakat — Almsgiving
intermediate
10 min
Zakat Disbursement: The Tamlik Requirement and Its Implications
Ruling on prohibited recipients and uses for zakat with proof and legal stratagem

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why four specific uses of zakat (building mosques, shrouding dead, repaying debt of deceased, freeing a slave) are invalid due to the absence of tamlik, and apply the legal stratagem (hila) to achieve those charitable ends while fulfilling zakat.

Core Idea
Zakat requires tamlik (ownership transfer) to a poor person; direct spending on mosques or shrouds is batil and the obligation remains.

This lesson from Al-Bahr al-Raiq examines the Hanafi position that zakat must be given directly to a poor Muslim, not spent on pious projects that bypass tamlik. We will learn which uses are prohibited, why, and a permissible legal trick (hila) to satisfy both the zakat obligation and the desire for those good deeds.

Guided Path
1
Identify the prohibited recipients and uses
The passage states that zakat is not given to a dhimmi, harbi, or musta'min, and that four specific uses are invalid: building a mosque, shrouding a deceased, repaying the debt of a deceased, and buying a slave to free.
2
Understand the legal reasoning: absence of tamlik
Each prohibited use lacks tamlik (transfer of ownership) to a poor person, which is the rukn of zakat. For example, the shroud remains owned by the donor, and freeing a slave is isqat (cancellation), not tamlik.
Tamlik is a rukn (integral) – without it, the act is batil.
3
Analyze the proof from the Quran
The verse 60:9 is cited to prohibit giving zakat to harbi, showing that alliance (implied by tamlik) is forbidden with enemies.
The dalil is qati' in thubut and dalala.
4
Distinguish between repaying debt of deceased vs. living
Repaying debt of a deceased is invalid because the deceased cannot own; repaying a living person's debt with his order is valid (the creditor acts as agent), but without order it is invalid.
If with order, tamlik is achieved through agency.
5
Apply the hila stratagem
To still perform the good deeds, one can give the zakat amount to a poor person (fulfilling tamlik) and then instruct that person to spend on the mosque, shroud, etc. The giver gets zakat reward, the poor gets charity reward.
This hila is from al-Muhit.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الزكاة باب المصرف
مصرف الزكاة كما قدمناه فلا يدفع إلى ذمي، والصرف في الكل إلى فقراء المسلمين أحب . وقيد بالذمي لأن جميع الصدقات . فرضاً كانت أو واجبه أو تطوعاً لا تجوز للحربي اتفاقاً كما في غاية البيان لقوله تعالى إنما ينهاكم الله عن الذين قاتلوكم في الدين﴾ [الممتحنة : ٩ ] وأطلقه فشمل المستأمن وقد صرح به في النهاية قوله ( وبناء مسجد وتكفين ميت وقضاء دينه وشراء قن يعتق) بالجر بالعطف على «ذمي» والضمير في دينه للميت، وعدم الجواز لانعدام التمليك الذي هو الركن في الأربعة لأن الكفن على ملك المتبرع حتى لو افترس الميت السبع كان الكفن للمتبرع لا لورثة الميت، وقضاء دين الغير لا يقتضي التمليك من ذلك الغير الحي فالميت أولى بدليل أنه لو قضى دين غيره ثم تصادق الدائن والمديون على عدمه رجع المتبرع على الدائن لا على المديون والإعتاق إسقاط لا تمليك . قيد بقضاء دين الميت لأنه لو قضى دين الحي إن قضاه بغير أمره يكون متبرعاً ولا يجزئه عن الزكاة ، وإن قضاه بأمره جاز . يكون القابض كالوكيل له في قبض الصدقة . كذا في غاية البيان . وقيده في النهاية بأن يكون المديون فقيراً ولا بد منه، ويستفاد منه أن رجوع المتبرع بقضاء الدين عند التصادق على الدائن محمول على ما إذا كان بغير أمر المديون، أما إذا كان بأمره فهو تمليك منه فلا رجوع عند التصادق بأنه لا دين على الدائن وإنما يرجع على المديون، وهو بعمومه يتناول ما لو دفعه ناوياً الزكاة، وينبغي أن لا رجوع فيها كما بحثه المحقق في فتح القدير فليراجع. والحيلة في الجواز في هذه الأربعة أن يتصدق بمقدار زكاته على فقير ثم يأمره بعد ذلك بالصرف إلى هذه الوجوه فيكون لصاحب المال ثواب الزكاة وللفقير ثواب هذه القرب. كذا في المحيط . وأشار المصنف إلى أنه لو أطعم يتيماً بنيتها لا يجزئه لعدم التمليك إلا إذا دفع له الطعام كالكسوة إذا كان يعقل القبض وإلا فلا ولو دفع الصغير إلى وليه كذا في الخانية. والمراد بالعقل هنا أن لا يرمى به ولا يخدع عنه .
قوله : (وأطلقه فشمل المستأمن قال الرملي أي أطلق في غاية البيان الحربي فشمل المستأمن ودخوله في الحربي ظاهر لأنه لا يقر في دار الإسلام وإنما الإذن خصه بوصف لا يمنع إطلاق الحربي عليه تأمل قوله : (رجع المتبرع على الدائن لا على المديون الأظهر عبارة الزيلعي وهي: يسترده الدافع وليس للمديون أخذه فقوله وليس للمديون أخذه هو ثمرة قوله قضاء دين الغير لا يقتضي التمليك من ذلك الغير لأنه لو اقتضى تمليكه من المديون كان حق الأخذ عند المصادقة المذكورة للمديون لا للدائن قوله : (ويستفاد منه أن رجوع المتبرع (الخ) أقول : لفظ المتبرع يستفاد منه أنه بغير أمر المديون، وقوله على الدائن متعلق برجوع ، وقوله فهو تمليك منه أي من المدبون أي أنه بمنزلة القرض منه والدائن نائب عن المديون في القبض لأن من قضى دين غيره بأمره لم يكن متبرعاً فله الرجوع على الأمر وإن لم يشترط الرجوع في الصحيح، ولذا قال وإنما يرجع على المديون قوله : (كما بحثه المحقق الخ) وذلك حيث قال : لأنه بالدفع وقع الملك للفقير بالتمليك وقبض النائب عن الفقير وعدم الدين في الواقع إنما يبطل به صيرورته قابضاً لنفسه بعد القبض نيابة لا التمليك الأول لأن غاية الأمر أن يكون ملك فقيراً على ظن أنه مديون وظهور عدمه لا يؤثر عدم الملك بعد وقوعه الله تعالى
Book of Zakat, Chapter of Disbursement. The disbursement of zakat is as we have presented: it is not given to a dhimmi, and disbursing all of it to poor Muslims is preferable. It is restricted to the dhimmi because all charities, whether obligatory or voluntary, are not permissible for a harbi by agreement, as in Ghayat al-Bayan, due to His statement: 'Allah only forbids you from those who fought you in religion' [60:9]. He (the author) made it absolute, so it includes the musta'min, and it is explicitly stated in al-Nihaya. His statement: (and building a mosque, shrouding a deceased, repaying his debt, and purchasing a slave to free) are in the genitive case, conjoined to 'dhimmi'. The pronoun in 'his debt' refers to the deceased. Non-permissibility is due to the absence of tamlik, which is the integral (rukn) in these four. Because the shroud is owned by the donor; even if a wild animal devours the deceased, the shroud belongs to the donor, not the heirs of the deceased. Repaying the debt of another does not entail tamlik from that person, whether living; so the deceased is even more so, evidenced by the fact that if someone repays another's debt and then the creditor and debtor agree it was not owed, the donor recovers from the creditor, not the debtor. Emancipation is an act of cancellation (isqat), not tamlik. He restricted it to repaying the debt of the deceased because if he repays the debt of a living person: if he repays without his order, he is a volunteer and it does not suffice for zakat; if he repays with his order, it is permissible. The recipient becomes like his agent in receiving the charity. This is in Ghayat al-Bayan. He restricted it in al-Nihaya by requiring that the debtor be poor, which is necessary. It is derived from this that the return of the volunteer in repaying the debt when they agree on non-existence is understood to be when it was without the debtor's order. But if it was with his order, it is tamlik from him, so there is no return when they agree that there is no debt; rather, he recovers from the debtor. This, in its generality, includes when he pays intending zakat. It should be that there is no return in it, as the muhaqqiq researched in Fath al-Qadir, so it should be consulted. The legal trick (hila) for permissibility in these four is that he gives charity of the amount of his zakat to a poor person, then after that orders him to spend it on these purposes. Thus the owner gets the reward of zakat, and the poor person gets the reward of these good deeds. This is in al-Muhit. The author indicated that if he feeds an orphan with the intention (of zakat), it does not suffice due to lack of tamlik, unless he gives him the food like clothing, if he is capable of receiving; otherwise no. If he gives to the minor through his guardian, this is in al-Khaniyya. The meaning of 'intellect' here is that he does not discard it and is not cheated out of it. His statement: (He made it absolute, so it includes the musta'min). Al-Ramli said: i.e., he made 'harbi' absolute in Ghayat al-Bayan, so it includes the musta'min, and his inclusion in 'harbi' is apparent because he is not permanently resident in Dar al-Islam; the permission only distinguishes him by an attribute that does not prevent the general application of 'harbi' to him. Consider. His statement: (The volunteer repays the creditor, not the debtor). The clearer expression of al-Zayla'i is: 'The payer takes it back, and the debtor has no right to take it.' So his statement 'and the debtor has no right to take it' is the consequence of his statement that 'repaying the debt of another does not entail tamlik from that person' because if it entailed his tamlik from the debtor, the right to take it upon the aforementioned agreement would belong to the debtor, not the creditor. His statement: (And it is derived from this that the return of the volunteer etc.) I say: the word 'volunteer' indicates that it is without the debtor's order, and his statement 'to the creditor' is attached to 'return'. His statement: 'it is tamlik from him' means from the debtor, i.e., it is in the nature of a loan from him, and the creditor is the deputy of the debtor in receiving. Because whoever repays the debt of another with his order is not a volunteer, so he can recover from the person who ordered, even if he did not stipulate recovery in the correct view. Therefore he said: 'He only recovers from the debtor.' His statement: (As the muhaqqiq researched etc.) That is where he said: 'Because by payment, ownership is established for the poor person through tamlik, and the deputy receives on behalf of the poor person. The non-existence of the debt in reality only nullifies his becoming a recipient for himself after receiving as a deputy, not the first tamlik. Because the utmost is that he made a poor person an owner under the assumption he was in debt, and the appearance of non-existence does not affect the non-existence of ownership after it occurred. Allah knows best.'
Source pages p. 424

Commentary
This passage from Al-Bahr al-Raiq discusses the disbursement of zakat, specifically the requirement of tamlik (transfer of ownership) as a rukn (integral) for zakat to be valid. The author begins by reiterating that zakat is not given to a dhimmi (non-Muslim living under Muslim rule) and that it is preferable to give to poor Muslims. He then cites a verse from Surah al-Mumtahina (60:9) to show that all charities, obligatory or voluntary, are not permissible for a harbi (non-Muslim at war). The text notes that this ruling applies generally, including the musta'min (a non-Muslim given safe passage), because such a person is not a permanent resident of Dar al-Islam. The core ruling of this section is that zakat cannot be paid for four specific purposes: building a mosque, shrouding a deceased, repaying the debt of a deceased, and purchasing a slave to free. The reason is that each of these actions lacks the essential element of tamlik—transferring ownership to a poor person. For example, the shroud remains the property of the donor even after it is used, so the deceased never owns it. Repaying someone's debt does not transfer ownership to the debtor; rather, it settles a liability. Freeing a slave is an act of isqat (cancellation of ownership) not tamlik. The commentary then distinguishes between repaying the debt of a living person with or without his order: if without order, it is voluntary and does not count as zakat; if with order, it is valid because the creditor acts as the debtor's agent. The text also discusses a scenario where the creditor and debtor later agree the debt was non-existent: if it was without order, the donor recovers from the creditor; if with order, he recovers from the debtor. The author notes that when the payment is made with zakat intention, some scholars like the muhaqqiq in Fath al-Qadir argue that there is no recovery at all. As a legal hila (stratagem) to achieve the reward of these four good deeds while fulfilling zakat, one can give the zakat amount to a poor person first (thus achieving tamlik) and then instruct that poor person to spend it on building a mosque, etc. The passage then addresses feeding an orphan with zakat intention: it is not valid unless the orphan is capable of receiving (i.e., has discretion), or the food is given to the guardian. The commentary concludes with a gloss clarifying the term 'volunteer' and the recovery rules.

Key Terms
التمليك (al-tamlik): Transfer of ownership from the giver to the recipient, considered the essential rukn (integral) of zakat in this passage.
المستأمن (al-musta'min): A non-Muslim who enters Dar al-Islam with a safe conduct (aman), treated like a harbi for zakat rulings in this text.
الحيلة (al-hila): A legal stratagem to achieve a permissible result while avoiding a prohibition; here, giving zakat first to a poor person then directing him to spend on prohibited uses.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why is paying zakat for building a mosque not valid?
A) Because building a mosque is a recommended act, not obligatory.
B) Because it lacks the element of tamlik (ownership transfer) to a poor person.
C) Because the mosque is considered a public utility, not private ownership.
D) Because it is makruh to spend zakat on building projects.

Key Takeaway
Zakat is not fulfilled by direct spending on mosques, shrouds, deceased's debts, or freeing slaves because tamlik (ownership transfer) to a poor person is a required rukn; use the hila to achieve both goals.

Zakat — Almsgiving

Zakat to Relatives: Prohibition for Ascendants, Descendants, and Spouses
Ruling with proof via talil of zakat definition

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling that prohibits paying obligatory charity to one's ascendants, descendants, and spouse, and explain the rationale of non-severance of benefit.

Core Idea
Obligatory charity cannot be given to one's own ascendants, descendants, or spouse because the benefit is not fully severed from the giver.

This passage from Al-Bahr al-Raiq clarifies a key restriction in zakat disbursement: you cannot pay your obligatory charity (zakat, kaffara, fitra, etc.) to your own parents, grandparents, children, grandchildren, or spouse. The reason lies in the definition of zakat itself—it requires complete removal of ownership and benefit. Since these relatives share a financial bond with you, the benefit is not truly transferred. However, other relatives like siblings and uncles are not only permissible but preferred recipients. The passage also addresses several nuanced cases involving intra-madhhab differences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the General Rule
The passage states that it is not permissible to give zakat (and all obligatory charity) to one's 'root' (asl: ascending lineage) or 'branch' (far': descending lineage) even if they are distant, or to one's spouse.
This includes parents, grandparents, children, grandchildren, and husband/wife.
2
Understand the Rationale (Illah)
The reason given is that the benefit (manfa'ah) is not completely cut off from the giver. In Hanafi fiqh, zakat requires removing both ownership (raqaba) and benefit. For usul/furu' and spouse, the benefit remains (e.g., a child still benefits from the father's wealth), so the payment is invalid.
This is derived from the definition of zakat mentioned earlier in the text.
3
Analyze the Scope and Exceptions
The rule applies to all obligatory charity (e.g., kaffara, sadqat al-fitr, nadhr). For other relatives (siblings, uncles, etc.), payment is permissible and even preferred. Voluntary charity is better given to usul/furu'.
The passage cites al-Fatawa al-Zahiriyya and other sources.
4
Examine Intra-Madhhab Differences
Two key ikhtilafat are discussed: (1) Giving to a sister who has a mahar owed by a wealthy husband – Abu Hanifa allows, the two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) disallow, and fatwa is with caution (the two). (2) Giving zakat in terminal illness to an inheritor – some deem it valid, others consider it a bequest that heirs can reject; the preferred view is validity.
The passage also covers children born from zina and children of a missing husband.
5
Apply the Rule to Spouses and Correct Misunderstandings
Spouses cannot give obligatory charity to each other. The hadith about the wife of Ibn Mas'ud giving charity to her husband is interpreted as voluntary charity, not zakat. A common mistake is to think the hadith permits zakat to a spouse.
The passage corrects an error in al-Nahr about returning to the debtor.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله (وأصله وإن علا وفرعه وإن سفل) بالجر أي لا يجوز الدفع إلى أبيه وجده وإن علا ولا إلى ولده وولد ولده وإن سفل لأن المنفعة لم تنقطع عن المملك من كل وجه كما قدمه في تعريف الزكاة لأن الواجب عليه الإخراج عن ملكه رقبة ومنفعة ولم يوجد في الأصول والفروع الإخراج عن ملكه منفعة وإن وجد رقبة وفي عبده وجد الإخراج منفعة لا رقبة. كذا في المستصفى. وفيه إشارة إلى أن هذا الحكم لا يخص الزكاة بل كل صدقة واجبة لا عليه . عن هو محمول على الخ. وما وقع في النهر من أنه يرجع على المديون سهو لأن الكلام فيما إذا دفعه ناوياً الزكاة .
His statement: (And his root even if high, and his branch even if low) in the genitive, i.e., it is not permissible to pay to his father and grandfather even if high, nor to his son and grandson even if low, because the benefit has not been cut off from the owner in every respect, as he presented in the definition of zakat. Because it is obligatory upon him to remove from his ownership both the corporeal asset (raqaba) and the benefit (manfa'a). And in ascendants and descendants, the removal of ownership of benefit is not found, although ownership of the corporeal asset is found. And in his slave, the removal of the benefit is found but not the corporeal asset. As in al-Mustasfa. In it there is an indication that this ruling is not specific to zakat, but rather every obligatory charity (like expiations, zakat al-fitr, and vows) is not permissible to give to them, just as for spouses. He restricted it to his root and branch because for other relatives, it is permissible and indeed preferable due to the combination of kinship and charity, like poor brothers, sisters, paternal uncles and aunts, maternal uncles and aunts. That is why it is stated in al-Fatawa al-Zahiriyya: He begins in charities with relatives, then mawlas, then neighbors. And elsewhere, attributed to Abu Hafs al-Kabir: The charity of a man is not accepted while his relatives are in need, and he should satisfy their need. In al-Muhit: If he gives to his sister who has a mahar (dowry) owed by her wealthy husband and she has (other) wealth reaching nisab, it is permissible according to Abu Hanifa; but it is not halal according to the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), and one gives fatwa with caution. If he gives zakat to a relative whose maintenance is obligatory on him, it is permissible provided he does not count it as maintenance. In al-Qunniya: He gave his zakat during his terminal illness to his brother, then he died and the brother inherited it; it took effect. Then he annotated: It is not like one who bequeaths for Hajj—the executor cannot pay it to the deceased's relative because it is a bequest. Similarly here. Then he annotated: It is valid but the heirs have the option to reject considering it a bequest. End. The apparent preference is the first opinion. He made 'branch' absolute, so it includes one whose lineage is established from him and others if created from his sperm. So he does not pay to the one created from his sperm through zina (adultery), nor to the child of his umm walad whom he disowned. And excluded is the child of the woman whose husband was presumed dead: if she married and then gave birth, then the first husband returned alive, then according to Abu Hanifa—may he be pardoned—the child belongs to the first husband. Yet it is permissible for the first to give zakat to them, and their testimony is permissible for him. As in Mi'raj al-Diraya, due to the apparent absence of being a branch. On this basis, it would follow that the second husband may not give zakat to them due to the reality of being a branch, even if lineage is not established from him. However, it is reported in al-Fatawa al-Walwalijiyya that it is permissible for the second to give to them and their testimony is permissible for him according to the Imam's opinion, and his retraction is narrated, and fatwa is given accordingly. On this basis, the first may give to them but not the second. It is understood from the rationale of the issue—the non-cessation of benefit for the owner—that khums (one-fifth) from mines may be spent on ascendants, descendants, and spouses, because the owner may keep the khums for himself if the remaining four-fifths do not suffice him; so it is even more appropriate to allow for others, as mentioned by al-Usbijabi. He restricted it to obligatory charity because voluntary charity is better given to ascendants and descendants. As in al-Bada'i'. His statement: (And his wife and her husband) i.e., it is not permissible for a man to give to his wife nor for a woman to give to her husband, due to what we mentioned about the benefit not being cut off from him in every respect. And regarding a woman giving to her husband, there is disagreement with the two companions due to his (peace be upon him) statement: 'You have two rewards: reward of charity and reward of kinship ties'—which he said to the wife of Ibn Mas'ud when she asked him... We said: Give charity to him... it is interpreted as voluntary. And what appears in al-Nahr that he returns to the debtor is a mistake, because the discussion is when he paid with zakat intention.
Source pages p. 425

Commentary
This passage from Al-Bahr al-Raiq deals with the category of recipients prohibited from receiving zakat due to close blood or marital ties. The ruling is that zakat (and all obligatory charity) cannot be given to one's ascendants (parents, grandparents, etc.) or descendants (children, grandchildren, etc.), nor to one's spouse. The rationale is that the benefit of the giver is not fully severed: for example, a child benefits from the father's wealth anyway, so giving zakat to the father would not truly remove the benefit from oneself. The passage distinguishes this from voluntary charity, which is recommended to be given to these relatives. It then discusses giving to other relatives (siblings, uncles, etc.), which is permissible and even preferable. Detailed issues include: giving to a sister who has a dowry owed by her wealthy husband—Abu Hanifa allows it, but Abu Yusuf and Muhammad do not, and the fatwa is with caution (i.e., following the two). Giving to a relative whose maintenance is obligatory is allowed if not counted as maintenance. Giving zakat in terminal illness to a brother who then inherits: some say it is valid; others consider it a bequest that heirs may reject. The passage then addresses the ruling for children born out of wedlock or from a marriage with a missing husband: the key is whether the child is legally considered a 'branch' (far') of the giver. According to Abu Hanifa's initial view, the child of a woman whose husband was presumed dead belongs to the first husband, so the second husband is not the father; thus the second husband can give zakat to such children. But the later fatwa distinguishes. The passage concludes with the prohibition regarding spouses, noting a hadith where the Prophet gave permission to the wife of Ibn Mas'ud to give charity to her husband (interpreted as voluntary charity). Finally, it corrects a misunderstanding in al-Nahr regarding returning to the debtor when paid with zakat intention.

Key Terms
الأصول والفروع (al-usul wa al-furu'): Ascendants (parents, grandparents up) and descendants (children, grandchildren down) of a person; recipients to whom obligatory charity cannot be given.
المهر (al-mahr): The bridal gift (dowry) owed by the husband to the wife; in this passage, a sister who has a mahar owed by her wealthy husband is considered wealthy enough to not be eligible for zakat according to the two companions.
الفرعية (al-far'iyya): The quality of being a descendant (branch); if a child is legally considered a descendant of the giver, zakat cannot be given to them.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why is it not permissible to give zakat to one's own father?
A) Because the father is not a poor person.
B) Because the benefit of the giver is not fully cut off from the father.
C) Because the father is responsible for the child's maintenance.
D) Because the father is considered a close relative by consensus.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school prohibits paying obligatory charity to one's ascendants, descendants, and spouse because the benefit is not severed, but permits paying to other relatives like siblings, regarding it as virtuous.

Zakat — Almsgiving
intermediate
8 min
The Core Prohibition: Zakat to Ancestors and Descendants
Commentary on the prohibition of paying zakat to one's ancestors and descendants.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on giving zakat to one's ancestors and descendants and explain the legal reasoning based on the requirement of full ownership transfer (tamlik).

Core Idea
Zakat requires full ownership transfer; giving to ancestors/descendants fails because benefit remains with the giver.

In this lesson, we examine the fundamental ruling that zakat cannot be paid to one's own parents, grandparents, children, or grandchildren. The key principle is that zakat must transfer both the corpus and the benefit from the giver's ownership. When the recipient is a close relative, the giver's benefit is not fully severed, making the payment invalid for obligatory charity.

Guided Path
1
Identify the forbidden categories
Read the opening phrase 'وأصله وإن علا وفرعه وإن سفل' to identify that ancestors (upward) and descendants (downward) are prohibited recipients.
The text includes all levels of ancestors and descendants.
2
Understand the condition of tamlik
Zakat requires the giver to fully transfer ownership (tamlik) of both the corpus (raqabah) and the benefit (manfa'ah). The text states that in ancestors and descendants, only the corpus is transferred, not the benefit.
This condition is rooted in the definition of zakat as a transfer of complete ownership.
3
Apply the condition to ancestors and descendants
Since the giver still benefits from these relatives (e.g., through inheritance or familial support), the benefit is not fully severed. Therefore, the condition is not met, and zakat payment is invalid.
4
Note the extension to other obligatory charities
The same ruling applies to all wajib sadaqat (kaffarat, fitr, nadhr) because the condition of tamlik is common to them.
Voluntary charity (tatawwu') is an exception; it is better given to relatives.
5
Distinguish voluntary charity
The text explicitly says that voluntary charity is better given to ancestors and descendants because it combines kinship and charity.
This is mentioned in Al-Bada'i'.
