Purchase of Zakat by the Wealthy: Ownership vs. Permission
Intra-Hanafi dispute on ibaha vs. ownership transfer for zakah consumption by the wealthy.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between ownership transfer (sale/gift) and mere permission (ibaha) regarding the permissibility of a wealthy person benefiting from zakah, and identify the relied-upon Hanafi position.

Core Idea
Mere permission does not change the ruling — ownership transfer is required for a wealthy person to lawfully consume zakah.

You may wonder whether zakah, once given to a poor person, can ever return to a wealthy person. This lesson explores a subtle intra-Hanafi dispute: does the poor person's mere permission (ibaha) suffice to make the zakah lawful for a wealthy person, or must ownership be formally transferred? The answer hinges on the principle that change of ownership changes the legal status (tabaddul al-milk tabaddul al-'ayn) and on the lesson from the hadith of Barirah.

Guided Path
1
State the general Hanafi ruling
Identify that a wealthy person may buy or receive as a gift the obligatory charity after the poor has owned it. This is based on the principle that ownership transfer changes the ruling.
This is the agreed-upon basis.
2
Identify the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf
Recognize that a dispute arises when the poor merely permits (ibaha) the wealthy to use the charity without transferring ownership. Al-Nasafi prohibits it; Khuwahrzadah permits it.
Both are Hanafi scholars.
3
Follow the proof for each opinion
Al-Nasafi uses the hadith of Barirah: the Prophet said 'It is charity for her and a gift for us,' showing that transfer of ownership (gift) is needed. Khuwahrzadah argues that ibaha suffices because the poor owns the item.
The hadith is the key evidence.
4
Analyze the tarjih (preference)
The commentator and al-Fawa'id al-Tajiyyah prefer al-Nasafi's view because if ibaha were sufficient, the Prophet's distinction between charity and gift would be pointless.
This is the mufta bihi.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical consequence: a wealthy person may accept zakah from a poor person only through formal sale or gift, not by mere permission. This affects everyday transactions like sharing food.
The difference is directly actionable.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وللغني أن يشتري الصدقة الواجبة من الفقير ويأكلها، وكذا لو وهبها له لما علم أن تبدل الملك تبدل العين فلو أباحها له ولم يملكها منه ذكر أبو المعين النسفي أنه لا يحل تناوله للغني. وقال خواهر زاده : يحل. كذا في الفوائد التاجية. والذي يظهر ترجيح الأول لأن الإباحة لو كانت كافية لما قال عليه الصلاة والسلام في واقعة بريرة هو لها صدقة ولنا هدية كما لا يخفى إلا أن يقال بالفرق بين الهاشمي والغني، وإن قيل به نصحيح لما تقدم أن الشبهة في حق الهاشمي كالحقيقة بدليل منع . قوله : (قيدنا به أي بقوا . إذا كان قيمته أي قيمة ما دون النصاب لا تساوي نصاباً .
And it is permissible for the wealthy person to buy the obligatory charity from the poor person and eat it, and likewise if the poor person gifted it to him, because it is known that change of ownership is like change of substance. If the poor person merely permitted him to use it without transferring ownership, Abu al-Ma'in al-Nasafi mentioned that it is not permissible for the wealthy to take it. And Khuwahrzadah said: it is permissible. Thus in al-Fawa'id al-Tajiyyah. What is apparent is the preference of the first opinion, because if mere permission were sufficient, the Prophet (peace be upon him) would not have said in the incident of Barirah: 'It is charity for her and a gift for us,' as is not hidden. However, it may be said that there is a distinction between the Hashimi and the wealthy, and if it is said it is correct because the doubt for the Hashimi is like reality, due to the prohibition... His statement: (We qualified it with) meaning 'with his daily food'. If his value, i.e., the value of what is less than the nisab, does not amount to a nisab.
Source pages p. 427

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute presented in the super-commentary. The issue is whether a wealthy person may consume obligatory charity (like zakah) after it has been transferred to a poor person by means of sale or gift. The general principle is that once the poor person owns the zakah, it becomes his lawful property, and he may sell it or gift it to anyone, including a wealthy person. This is because ownership transfer changes the status of the item (like a change of substance). However, if the poor person merely allows the wealthy to use it without transferring ownership (ibaha), there is a dispute: Abu al-Ma'in al-Nasafi says it is not permissible, while Khuwahrzadah says it is permissible. The text prefers al-Nasafi's view, reasoning that if mere permission were enough, the Prophet (peace be upon him) would not have distinguished between zakah as charity for Barirah and as a gift for the Prophet's family. The hadith of Barirah shows that even though the Prophet's household could not take zakah, they could take it as a gift after ownership transferred. By analogy, a wealthy person cannot take by mere permission but can by ownership transfer. There is also a discussion about whether the Hashimi (descendant of the Prophet) is treated differently; the text suggests that the doubt (shubha) for a Hashimi is like certainty, so the ruling for them is stricter. The last sentence is a separate gloss explaining the earlier phrase about 'qayyadna bihi' (we qualified it) referring to the daily food exception.

Key Terms
الإباحة (al-ibaha): Permission granted by the owner to another to use or consume the item without transferring ownership.
تبدل الملك تبدل العين (tabaddul al-milk tabaddul al-'ayn): The principle that change of ownership changes the legal status of an object, like a new substance.
صدقة واجبة (sadaqah wajibah): Obligatory charity, such as zakah and 'ushr.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the preferred Hanafi view, can a wealthy person eat food that a poor person has received as zakah if the poor merely allows it?
A) Yes, because the poor owns it.
B) No, because mere permission does not change the ruling.
C) Yes, because the poor can do whatever he wants.
D) No, because the wealthy cannot take any charity.

Key Takeaway
For a wealthy person to lawfully benefit from zakah, the poor must transfer ownership through sale or gift, not merely permit use.

Prohibition of Zakat for the Rich: Conditions and the Case of 19 Dinars
Ruling and conditions for zakat prohibition with a disputed case.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions that make it haram for a person to take zakat and distinguish the two Hanafi narrations regarding the 19-dinar case.

Core Idea
Owning a nisab beyond essential needs makes taking zakat haram; a disputed case is 19 dinars worth 300 dirhams.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq expands on the ruling that taking zakat is haram for the rich. It details several exceptions and conditions, and presents an intra-Hanafi difference on whether a person with 19 dinars (worth 300 dirhams) is considered rich. Understanding these nuances helps you apply the ruling correctly in various situations.

Guided Path
1
Identify the general prohibition
The passage begins by stating the original prohibition (sual) of taking zakat for the rich. Note that the key condition is ownership of a nisab (200 dirhams or equivalent) beyond essential needs and debts.
2
List the exceptions
Next, the author lists cases where a person with nisab may still take zakat: e.g., a scholar with books needed for learning, a debtor with overwhelming debt, a farmer with only two oxen. Categorize these under 'need' or 'debt'.
These exceptions show that the prohibition is not absolute but conditional.
3
Analyze the condition of being free of essential needs
The phrase 'being free of essential needs' is crucial. If one's wealth is tied up in necessities (like a house for dwelling, work tools), he may take zakat even if the items are worth nisab.
The jurist's books are considered essential for his profession, so he is not considered rich by them.
4
Focus on the 19-dinar case
The passage presents two narrations from Imam Muhammad: in al-Muhit, a person with 19 dinars worth 300 dirhams is not allowed zakat; in al-Fatawa al-Zahiriyya, he is allowed and fitr is not obligatory. Identify which view is mufta bihi.
The author seems to prefer al-Muhit's view, making it the relied-upon position.
5
Determine the practical consequence
Thus, a person with 19 dinars at modern gold prices (approx. 300 dirhams) is considered rich and prohibited from taking zakat. But note the existence of a weaker contrary view.
This difference is purely within the Hanafi school; both narrations are from Imam Muhammad.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
درهم هو الظاهر أصل تحريم السؤال وقيدنا بكونه فارغاً عن الحوائج الأصلية لأنه لو كان مستغرقاً بها حلت فتحل لمن ملك كتباً تساوي نصاباً وهو من أهلها للحاجة لا إن زادت على قدرها أو كان جاهلاً، والفقيه غني بكتبه ولو كان محتاجاً إليها لقضاء دينه فيجب بيعها كما في القنية من باب الحبس من القضاء، ويحل لمن له دور وحوانيت تساوي نصباً وهو محتاج لغلتها لنفقته ونفقة عياله على خلاف فيه ولمن عنده طعام سنة تساوي نصاباً لعياله على ما بخلاف قضاء الدين فإنه يجب عليه بيع قوته إلا قوت يومه كما في القنية من الحبس. وحلت لمن له نصاب وعليه دين مستغرق أو منقص للنصاب، وحلت لمن له كسوة الشتاء لا يحتاج إليها في الصيف وللمزارع إذا كان له ثوران لا إن زاد وبلغ نصاباً، ولا تحل لمن له دار تساوي نصاباً والفاضل عن سكناه يبلغ نصاباً. وقيد بملك النصاب لأن من ملك ما دونه يح له أخذها إذا كان قيمته لا تبلغ نصاباً ولو كان صحيحاً مكتسباً، قيدنا به لأنه لو كان تسعة عشر ديناراً تساوي ثلاث مائة لا تحل له الزكاة. كذا في المحيط عن محمد. وفي الفتاوى الظهيرية خلافه قال : وقال هشام سألت محمداً عن رجل له تسعة عشر ديناراً تساوي ثلاث مائة درهم هل يسعه أن يأخذ؟ قال : نعم ولا يجب عليه صدقة فطره .
The dirham is apparent [condition]*. The original prohibition of begging [taking zakat]. And we restricted it to being free of essential needs because if he was engrossed in them, it becomes permissible. So it becomes permissible for one who owns books worth a nisab and he is of its people (scholar) due to need, not if it exceeds his need or if he is ignorant. The jurist is rich with his books even if he needs them to repay his debt, so he must sell them as in al-Qunyah from the chapter of confinement from judiciary. And it becomes permissible for one who has houses and shops worth nisab and he needs their income for his own expenses and his family's expenses; there is a difference of opinion on this. And for one who has a year's supply of food worth nisab for his family, contrary to debt repayment, he must sell his food except his day's food, as in al-Qunyah from the chapter of confinement. And it became permissible for one who has nisab and has a debt that engrosses or reduces the nisab. And it became permissible for one who has winter clothing that he does not need in summer, and for a farmer if he has two oxen, not if it exceeds and reaches nisab. And it is not permissible for one who has a house worth nisab and the surplus over his dwelling reaches nisab. And it is restricted by possession of nisab because one who owns less than that may take it if its value does not reach nisab, even if he is healthy and earning; we restricted it because if he had nineteen dinars worth three hundred [dirhams], zakat is not permissible for him. Thus in al-Muhit from Muhammad. And in al-Fatawa al-Zahiriyya there is contrary: Hisham said: I asked Muhammad about a man who has nineteen dinars worth three hundred dirhams; is it permissible for him to take? He said: Yes, and fitr charity is not obligatory on him.
Source pages p. 427

Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq is an expansion on the ruling of who is prohibited from taking zakat. It opens by stating the original prohibition of asking for zakat or taking it as a rich person. The condition for prohibition is being free of essential needs; if one is engrossed in essential needs, it becomes permissible to take zakat. The author then lists various cases: a scholar who owns books worth nisab may still take zakat if he needs the books for learning, but not if they exceed his need. A jurist is considered rich with his books even if he must sell them to repay debt. The owner of houses or shops worth nisab who needs their income is a subject of disagreement. One with a year's food supply is considered rich, but for debt repayment he must sell even his food except for one day. A person with a nisab but an overwhelming debt may take zakat. Winter clothing not needed in summer, and a farmer with just two oxen, are exceptions. Having a house worth nisab with surplus reaching nisab makes one rich. The key condition is ownership of a complete nisab; one who owns less than nisab may take zakat even if healthy and earning. A specific case is given: nineteen dinars worth three hundred dirhams—according to Muhammad in al-Muhit, zakat is not permissible for him, but in al-Fatawa al-Zahiriyya, a different narration from Muhammad allows it and says fitr charity is not obligatory. This shows an intra-Hanafi transmission difference.

Key Terms
غني (ghani): One who possesses a complete nisab (200 dirhams or equivalent) beyond essential needs and debt.
نصاب (nisab): The minimum amount of wealth that obligates zakat or prohibits taking zakat; here, 200 dirhams or equivalent.
سؤال (su'al): Begging or asking for zakat or charity; prohibited for the rich.

Key Takeaway
A person with 19 dinars worth 300 dirhams is considered rich (has nisab) and cannot take zakat, according to the stronger view.

Zakat vs Other Sadaqat: The Rich May Receive Optional Charity
Ruling distinguishing zakat from other sadaqat for the rich

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between obligatory and voluntary sadaqah regarding permissibility for the rich, and state the evidence and exceptions.

Core Idea
Obligatory sadaqah is haram for the rich; voluntary charity is a permissible gift.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq expands on a matn phrase by clarifying that the prohibition of giving sadaqah to the rich applies only to obligatory and necessary forms of charity (zakat, ushr, kaffarat, vows, fitr), not to voluntary charity (nafl). The proof is the hadith 'لا تحل صدقة لغني', from which voluntary charity is excluded because giving to the rich voluntarily is considered a gift, not charity. The passage also addresses the ruling for endowment sadaqah, which is treated as obligatory unless the waqif specifies otherwise.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is giving sadaqah to a wealthy person. The ruling is that it is haram for obligatory sadaqat (zakat, ushr, kaffarat, vows, fitr), but permissible for voluntary charity (nafl).
2
Locate the dalil
The proof is the hadith 'لا تحل صدقة لغني' – 'Sadaqah is not permissible for the rich.' This is cited as the general evidence.
Hadith is ahad, but its meaning is considered qati' in this context.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
The hadith uses the general term 'sadaqah' which includes all obligatory charity. The prohibition (la tahillu) indicates haram. The exception for nafl is based on the principle that sadaqah upon the rich is a gift (hibah), not charity.
The exclusion of nafl is not from the hadith itself but from a legal reasoning (istiḥsan) that treats voluntary giving as a gift.
4
Note the ikhtilaf
The passage does not mention any intra- or inter-madhhab dispute; it presents the ruling as uniformly accepted (muttafaq alayh).
5
Evaluate the mufta bihi
The ruling is established in the Hanafi school, with al-Bada'i (al-Kasani) cited as authority. The operative fatwa is that obligatory sadaqat are haram for the rich, while voluntary charity is permissible as a gift.
For waqf sadaqah, the ruling depends on the waqif's specification.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقيد بالزكاة لأن النفل يجوز للغني كما للهاشمي، وأما بقية الصدقات المفروضة والواجبة كالعشر والكفارات والنذور وصدقة الفطر فلا يجوز صرفها للغني لعموم قوله عليه الصلاة والسلام لا تحل صدقة لغني خرج النفل منها لأن الصدقة على الغني هبة . كذا في البدائع . وأما صدقة الوقف فيجوز صرفها إلى الأغنياء إن سماهم الواقف وإلا فلا لأنها من الواجبة. كذا في البدائع أيضاً.
And it is restricted to zakat because supererogatory (nafl) is permissible for the rich as for the Hashimi. As for the rest of the obligatory and necessary sadaqat like ushr, kaffarat, vows, and fitr charity, it is not permissible to spend them on the rich due to the generality of his saying, peace be upon him: 'Sadaqah is not permissible for the rich.' The supererogatory is excluded from this because sadaqah upon the rich is a gift. Thus in al-Bada'i. As for endowment sadaqah, it is permissible to spend it on the rich if the waqif designated them, otherwise not because it is from the obligatory. Thus in al-Bada'i also.
Source pages p. 427

Commentary
This passage expands on the matn's phrase 'restricted to zakat' by explaining that the prohibition of giving to the rich applies specifically to zakat and other obligatory sadaqat (like ushr, kaffarat, vows, and fitr charity), not to voluntary charity (nafl). The proof is the hadith: 'Sadaqah is not permissible for the rich.' From this general prohibition, voluntary sadaqah is excluded because giving to a rich person voluntarily is considered a gift, not charity. Endowment sadaqah is also treated as obligatory if the waqif did not specify the rich; if he did, it is permissible.

Key Terms
نفل (nafl): Voluntary or supererogatory charity not legally obligatory.
هبة (hibah): Gift; when given to a rich person, voluntary sadaqah is considered a gift, not charity.

Key Takeaway

Ruling on a Zakat Collector Who Accumulates Over 200 Dirhams
Derived ruling on zakat collector's accumulation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions under which zakat payments to a collector become invalid and distinguish between agency of the poor and agency of the payers.

Core Idea
Zakat paid to a collector becomes invalid once the collector owns 200 dirhams—but only if he is an agent of the poor.

The prohibition of giving zakat to the rich has a subtle branch: what happens when people pay zakat to a collector for a poor person, and the collector accumulates 200 dirhams? The Hanafi jurists derive conditions based on whether the collector was authorized by the poor person or by the payers. This lesson examines the ruling and its practical consequences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the parties and their roles
Determine the payers (who pay zakat), the collector (who gathers it), and the intended recipient (a poor person). The collector may be an agent of the poor person or of the payers.
2
Analyze the agency
If the collector was appointed by the poor person (by his order), he is an agent of the poor and owns what he collects on his behalf. If not, he is an agent of the payers and does not own the money.
3
Apply the 200 dirham threshold
If the collector is an agent of the poor, once he accumulates 200 dirhams, his ownership makes him rich, so any further payments collected after that are invalid—unless the poor person is in debt, in which case the threshold is considered after deducting debt.
4
Determine the ruling for each scenario
If the collector is an agent of the payers (without the poor person's order), then all payments are valid regardless of the amount, because the collector never owns the money.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفرعوا على منع دفع الزكاة للغني ما لو دفع قوم زكاتهم إلى من يجمعها لفقير فاجتمع عند الآخذ أكثر من مائتين فإن كان جمعة له بأمره قالوا كل من دفع قبل أن يبلغ ما في يد الجابي مائتين جازت زكاته ومن دفع بعده لا يجوز إلا أن يكون الفقير مديوناً فيعتبر هذا التفصيل في مائتين تفضل بعد دينه، فإن كان بغير أمره جاز الكل مطلقاً لأنه في الأول هو وكيل عن الفقير فما اجتمع عنده يملكه وفي الثاني وكيل الدافعين فما اجتمع عنده ملكهم كذا في فتح القدير.
And they branched from the prohibition of paying zakat to the rich: if a group of people pay their zakat to someone who collects it for a poor person, and more than two hundred [dirhams] accumulates with the receiver; then if he collected it by [the poor person's] order, they said: everyone who paid before the collector's hand reached two hundred, his zakat is valid; whoever paid after that, it is not valid, unless the poor person is in debt, then this detail is considered regarding two hundred surplus after his debt. But if it was without his order, then all is valid absolutely, because in the first case he is an agent of the poor person, so what accumulates with him he owns; in the second he is an agent of the payers, so what accumulates with him is owned by them. Thus in Fath al-Qadir.
Source pages p. 427

Commentary
This passage derives a ruling from the general prohibition of giving zakat to the rich. It considers the case where people pay their zakat to a person who collects it for a poor person. If that collector (agent) accumulates more than 200 dirhams, the validity of subsequent payments depends on whether he was authorized by the poor person or not. If authorized by the poor person, then once the collector has 200 dirhams, he becomes rich, so subsequent payments are invalid unless the poor person is in debt (in which case the 200 threshold is after debt). If the collector is acting on behalf of the payers (not authorized by the poor person), then all payments are valid because the collector does not own the money; the payers are still the owners. This is from Fath al-Qadir (Ibn al-Humam).

Key Terms
جامع الزكاة (jami' al-zakat): A person appointed to collect zakat funds for distribution to the poor.
وكيل (wakil): Agent; the collector can be an agent of the poor person (making him owner) or of the payers (not owner).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
The validity of zakat paid to a collector depends on whether the collector is an agent of the poor (ownership triggers invalidity after 200) or of the payers (no invalidity).

Can a Rich Person Consume Zakat from the Poor?
Ruling on a rich person buying zakat from the poor with a scholarly dispute

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on a rich person obtaining zakat from a poor person through purchase, gift, or ibahah, and explain why ibahah is not sufficient according to the preferred view.

Core Idea
Ownership transfer is required for the rich to benefit from zakat; mere permission (ibahah) is insufficient.

A common question is whether a rich person can benefit from zakat that a poor person has received. The Hanafi school allows the rich to buy zakat from the poor or receive it as a gift, but a dispute arises when the poor merely permits the rich to use it without transferring ownership. This lesson examines the ruling, the dispute, and the proof from the hadith of Barirah.

Guided Path
1
Identify the central ruling
State the initial ruling: a rich person may buy zakat from the poor or receive it as a gift. Understand the underlying principle: change of ownership (tabaddul al-milk) is like change of substance (tabaddul al-ayn).
2
Recognize the contested scenario
Identify the case of ibahah (mere permission to use). Note the dispute between Abu al-Mu'in al-Nasafi (not permissible) and Khawahrizadeh (permissible). Determine which view is preferred.
The author prefers al-Nasafi's view.
3
Analyze the proof
Examine the hadith of Barirah: 'It is sadaqah for her and a gift for us.' Understand how this distinguishes between the poor's ownership and others' benefit, showing that ownership transfer is necessary for the rich.
The hadith is ahad, but its meaning (dalala) is clear.
4
Address the distinction of Hashimi
Consider the exception for Hashimis: they may take sadaqah as a gift. Understand why this does not apply to the non-Hashimi rich—ambiguity (shubha) in the case of Hashimi is treated as reality (haqiqah).
This distinction reinforces the general prohibition for the non-Hashimi rich.
5
Apply the ruling
Conclude that a rich person may only consume zakat from a poor person through purchase or gift, not through ibahah. For fatwa, al-Nasafi's view is relied upon.
Always ensure ownership transfer occurs.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وللغني أن يشتري الصدقة الواجبة من الفقير ويأكلها، وكذا لو وهبها له لما علم أن تبدل الملك كتبدل العين فلو أباحها له ولم يملكها منه ذكر أبو المعين النسفي أنه لا يحل تناوله للغني. وقال خواهر زاده : يحل. كذا في الفوائد التاجية. والذي يظهر ترجيح الأول لأن الإباحة لو كانت كافية لما قال عليه الصلاة والسلام في واقعة بريرة هو لها صدقة ولنا هدية كما لا يخفى إلا أن يقال بالفرق بين الهاشمي والغني، وإن قيل به نصحيح لما تقدم أن الشبهة في حق الهاشمي كالحقيقة بدليل منع
And for the rich person, he may buy the obligatory charity from the poor and consume it. Likewise, if he gave it to him as a gift, because it is known that change of ownership is like change of substance. So if he permitted him (the rich) to use it without transferring ownership, Abu al-Mu'in al-Nasafi mentioned that it is not permissible for the rich to consume it. And Khawahrizadeh said: it is permissible. Thus in al-Fawa'id al-Tajiyyah. And what appears is the preference of the first because if permission were sufficient, the Prophet, peace be upon him, would not have said in the case of Barirah: 'It is sadaqah for her and a gift for us,' as is not hidden, unless it is said that there is a difference between the Hashimi and the rich. If it is said, it is considered correct because it has been established that the ambiguity in the case of the Hashimi is like reality, due to the prohibition...
Source pages p. 427

Commentary
This passage addresses whether a rich person can obtain zakat from the poor through purchase or gift. The initial ruling is that it is permissible because ownership changes. However, if the poor merely permits (ibahah) the rich to use the zakat without transferring ownership, there is a dispute. Abu al-Mu'in al-Nasafi says it is not permissible for the rich to consume it, while Khawahrizadeh says it is permissible. The author prefers al-Nasafi's view, arguing that if pure permission were sufficient, the Prophet would not have distinguished between sadaqah and gift in the case of Barirah. There is a possible distinction between the Hashimi (who is allowed to take from sadaqah as a gift) and the ordinary rich person, but the text seems to argue that the prohibition holds for the rich.

Key Terms
إباحة (ibahah): Permission to use without transfer of ownership; the rich may not consume zakat through mere ibahah.
هاشمي (Hashimi): A descendant of Hashim (the Prophet's clan); they are allowed to take sadaqah as a gift but not as charity.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the preferred view in the passage, a rich person may consume zakat from a poor person if:
A) The poor person grants him permission to use it (ibahah).
B) He buys it from the poor person or receives it as a gift.
C) He is a Hashimi.
D) He gives it to the poor as a gift first.

Key Takeaway
Ownership transfer is essential for a rich person to benefit from zakat; purchase or gift is permissible, but mere permission (ibahah) is not.

The Condition of Being Free of Essential Needs for Zakat Eligibility
Commentary defining the condition 'free of essential needs'

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the condition that makes a person eligible to receive zakat and apply it to identify eligible recipients.

Core Idea
One may take zakat if the wealth remaining after essential needs is less than the nisab.

This passage from Bahrain al-Ra'iq clarifies a key condition for a zakat recipient: being 'free of essential needs' (فارغاً عن الحوائج الأصلية). It restricts the permissibility of taking zakat to those whose disposable surplus after covering basic necessities does not reach the nisab threshold. Understanding this condition is crucial for correctly identifying who may lawfully receive zakat.

Guided Path
1
Identify the key term
The phrase 'قيدنا به' (we restricted it by) introduces the condition being defined. Recognize that this is a restriction on the general ruling about taking zakat.
The restriction limits permissibility to those whose surplus < nisab.
2
Interpret the gloss 'أي بقوا'
The parenthetical 'أي بقوا' likely means 'i.e. remaining' — referring to the wealth that remains after essential needs. The condition is about the surplus, not total wealth.
OCR may have corrupted 'بقاء' or 'بقية'; the meaning is 'remaining'.
3
Apply the condition to determine eligibility
Check if the value of what remains after essentials (the surplus) is less than the nisab. If yes, taking zakat is permissible; if no, it is prohibited.
The nisab for zakat is the minimum threshold (e.g., 85g gold or 595g silver).
4
Derive the legal outcome
The condition yields a clear ruling: a person whose surplus after essential needs does not reach nisab may receive zakat as a poor or needy person.
Essential needs include food, clothing, shelter, and basic household items.
5
Test with examples
Consider scenarios: Person A has 2000 dirhams after needs (above nisab) → cannot receive zakat. Person B has 150 dirhams after needs (below nisab) → may receive zakat.
The condition is only one of several; others include not being a Hashimi, not being a zakat payer oneself, etc.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (قيدنا به أي بقوا . إذا كان قيمته أي قيمة ما دون النصاب لا تساوي نصاباً .
His saying: (We restricted it by [being free of essential needs]) i.e. [we restricted it by] remaining [of wealth?]. If its value, i.e. the value of what is less than nisab, does not equal a nisab.
Source pages p. 427

Commentary
This very short commentary explains the phrase 'قيدنا به' (we restricted it by) from the earlier text. It clarifies that the condition of being free of essential needs means that one's disposable wealth after covering essential needs does not reach the nisab. In other words, a person who has less than nisab after fulfilling his essential needs is allowed to take zakat. The parenthetical note 'أي بقوا' is likely a gloss meaning 'remaining' (the surplus). The second part reiterates that for one who owns less than nisab, the value of that wealth does not reach the nisab threshold, so zakat is permissible.

Key Terms
قيدنا به (qayyadna bihi): We restricted it by [the condition of being free of essential needs]

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Based on the passage, what does the condition 'free of essential needs' (فارغاً عن الحوائج الأصلية) mean for zakat eligibility?
A) The person has no wealth at all.
B) The surplus wealth after essential needs is less than the nisab.
C) The person's total wealth is less than the nisab.
D) The person is not required to pay zakat.

Key Takeaway
A person is eligible to receive zakat if the wealth remaining after covering essential needs is less than the nisab threshold.

Criterion for Being Rich: Number or Value for Livestock?
Intra-Hanafi dispute: criterion for being rich regarding livestock nisab.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the two intra-Hanafi positions on whether owning five camels worth less than 200 dirhams makes one rich, and apply the primary view to a real case.

Core Idea
For zakat, the livestock nisab is counted by number, not value—even if camels are cheap, they still make you rich.

In this passage, the author of al-Bahr al-Raiq clarifies a critical point: does owning five camels always prohibit taking zakat, even if they are not worth 200 dirhams? This question leads to an intra-Hanafi dispute with practical consequences for the poor. We will examine the primary view and the dissenting narration, and how later scholars reconciled them.

Guided Path
1
Identify the issue
The issue is whether ownership of five camels (nisab) worth less than 200 dirhams makes one rich (prohibited from taking zakat).
2
State the primary Hanafi position
According to the commentators of al-Hidayah, yes, owning a nisab of livestock makes one rich regardless of value.
This is the explicit position in al-Muhit and other core texts.
3
State the opposing view within the school
Some Hanafi scholars, including al-Marghinani in al-Jawharah, and a narration in al-Fatawa al-Zahiriyya, hold that the value (200 dirhams) is the criterion.
They cite the hadith about 200 dirhams or their equivalent.
4
Examine the proofs
The primary view relies on the fact that livestock nisab is defined by number in zakat obligation; the opposing view cites the hadith that defines sufficiency as 200 dirhams or its equivalent.
The hadith: 'Whoever asks people while he has what suffices him... 200 dirhams or their equivalent.'
5
Understand the reconciliation
Later scholars reconciled by saying the value criterion applies to weighed items, while number criterion applies to counted items like livestock.
Thus, for livestock, the primary view based on number remains the mufta bihi.

Source Text

What is in al-Muhit is the second, and what is in al-Zahiriyya is the first. And it appears that considering weight is specific to weighed items because it is consistent, while counted items like grazing livestock consider number instead of weight. So what is in al-Bahr, al-Nahr, and al-Manh proceeds on what is in al-Zahiriyya, and what is in al-Sharnabaliyya on what is in al-Muhit. Thus the contradiction between the statements of the people is resolved. End of summary. This is possible, but if it were stated in what the author said, contradiction would occur, but since it is not, as al-Sharnabali claimed, then... their speech what is explicit what he transmitted from.

Commentary
This passage addresses a key dispute: is the criterion for being rich the ownership of a nisab of any type (like five camels) regardless of its value, or is it the value equal to 200 dirhams? The first paragraph states the primary view that owning five camels or a nisab of grazing livestock makes one rich, even if the value is less than 200 dirhams. This is the explicit position of the commentators of al-Hidayah. The next part refutes a claim from Mi'raj al-Dirayah that permissibility to pay does not imply permissibility to take, citing Ghayat al-Bayan that one who owns less than nisab may take. Then the text discusses the slave and child of a rich person, noting that zakat cannot be given to them. A major dispute arises: some (like al-Sharnabali) claim that the value (200 dirhams) is the criterion even for livestock, citing al-`Inayah and a hadith about 200 dirhams. Others, like al-Marghinani in al-Jawharah, say that if one has five camels worth less than 200 dirhams, zakat is permissible for him. The reconciliation is attempted: some say the difference is due to conflicting narrations from Muhammad regarding weight vs. value as the criterion. Weight is for weighed items, number for counted items like camels. Thus the view from al-Zahiriyya (considering value) applies to weighed items, while al-Muhit (considering number) applies to livestock. The passage ends abruptly but indicates that this reconciliation resolves the apparent contradiction.

Key Terms
سائمة (sa'imah): Grazing livestock (camels, cattle, sheep/goats) kept for milk, breeding, or trade.
نصاب نامي (nisab nami): Productive nisab; wealth that has growth potential, like livestock or trade goods.

Key Terms from Source Passage

D) May take zakat only if he is a Hashimi.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school primarily holds that for livestock, the nisab is determined by number, not value, making even low-value herds sufficient to prohibit taking zakat.

Nisaab for Zakat Recipients: Counting Camels or Their Value?
Intra-Hanafi dispute on nisaab for livestock

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the nisaab that prohibits receiving zakat and identify the intra-madhhab dispute regarding value vs. count for grazing livestock.

Core Idea
To receive zakat, a person must own less than the nisaab; for livestock, the majority says it's by count, not value.

This lesson addresses a key question in zakat distribution: when does a person's wealth disqualify them from receiving zakat? The source text from al-Bahr al-Raiq discusses the nisaab threshold, particularly for grazing livestock, and reveals an important disagreement within the Hanafi school. By understanding this dispute, you will learn how jurists reconcile textual evidence with practical application.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Identify the general ruling: a person who owns a complete nisaab (the minimum wealth subject to zakat) cannot receive zakat. For livestock, the nisaab is defined by number (e.g., 5 camels).
This is the majority view.
2
Identify the opponent and their proof
Some Hanafi scholars, like al-Marghinani, argue that the nisaab should be measured by value (200 dirhams) based on the hadith defining sufficiency. Understand their dalil and logic.
The hadith does not specifically mention livestock.
3
Analyze the Hanafi response
The majority Hanafi view holds that the hadith applies to liquid assets, not livestock. Livestock have their own fixed nisaab by count from the Sunnah. Evaluate how the school uses tahsis (specification) to limit the generality of the hadith.
The response relies on the principle that livestock nisaab is established by non-textual evidence.
4
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical difference: a person with 5 camels worth only 100 dirhams can receive zakat according to the value view but cannot according to the count view. Assess which ruling applies today.
This shows the real-world impact of the dispute.
5
Identify the mufta bihi
The majority opinion (count for livestock) is the relied-upon position in later Hanafi fatwa works. Understand why it prevails despite al-Marghinani's authority.
Tarjih mechanisms include consideration of practice and analogy.

Yes, it is better not to [give] because the Hashemite is from the working class unlike the rich. And included under the mentioned growing nisab first is five of grazing camels. So if he owns it or a nisab of grazing livestock from any wealth, it is not permissible to give zakat to him, whether it equals two hundred dirhams or not. The commentators of al-Hidayah stated this at his saying: 'from any wealth.' And in Miraj al-Dirayah: his saying: 'and it is permissible to give it to someone who owns less than that, but it is not permissible for the taker to consume it because the permissibility of giving does not necessitate the permissibility of taking, like the case of thinking a rich person is poor.' This is incorrect. What is clearly stated in Ghayat al-Bayan and others is that it is permissible for the one who owns less than the nisab to take it just as it is permissible to give it, and to take for the one who has sufficient sustenance, as stated in al-Bada'i. His saying: 'and his slave and his child' i.e., it is not permissible to give zakat and what is attached to it to the slave of a rich person and his young child because ownership in the slave belongs to his master and he is not a recipient. This is in al-Kafi. So it indicates that what is meant by 'slave' is other than his saying: 'whether it equals two hundred dirhams or not.' His brother and student followed him in al-Munyah and affirmed in al-Shurnubalaliyah that it is a mistake. He said: and he mentioned the opposite in al-Ashbah wa al-Naza'ir in the art of conundrums, so he contradicted himself. I have not seen any of the commentators of al-Hidayah clearly state what he claimed; rather their expressions indicate the opposite, except that he said in al-Inayah: 'and it is not permissible to give zakat to one who owns a nisab, whether from cash or grazing livestock or trade goods.' So he gave the illusion of what he said, and it is refuted because the wording of al-Inayah 'whether it is from etc.' indicates interpreting the nisab as value absolutely, since trade goods have no nisab except what reaches the value of two hundred dirhams. And he clearly stated that the measure of the nisab is what matters in al-Tabyin and others. And he adduced for this in al-Kafi by his saying, peace be upon him: 'Whoever asks while he has what suffices him, then he has asked people importunately.' It was asked: 'And what suffices him?' He said: 'Two hundred dirhams or their equivalent.' And similarly in al-Muhit. So the hadith included the consideration of grazing livestock by value due to its generality. And the consideration of the value of grazing livestock has been stated in several books with no disagreement in al-Ashbah, al-Siraj, al-Wahbaniyah and its commentary by the author and by Ibn al-Shahnah and al-Dhakha'ir al-Ashrafiyyah. And in al-Jawharah, al-Marghinani said: 'If he has five camels whose value is less than two hundred dirhams, zakat is lawful for him and obligatory upon him.' By this it is clear that the nisab of cash is what is considered from any wealth, whether it reaches a nisab i.e., from its kind or not. End of al-Marghinani. End of what is in al-Shurnubalaliyah. And some of the commentators of al-Durr al-Mukhtar reconciled by carrying what is reported from al-Muhit and al-Zahiriyyah on a difference in narration from Muhammad regarding whether the measure in the prohibitive nisab is weight or value. So what is in al-Muhit is the second, and what is in al-Zahiriyyah is the first. And the apparent is that consideration of weight is specific to items weighed because it is applicable there. As for counted items like grazing livestock, number is considered instead of weight. So what is in al-Bahr, al-Nahr, and al-Munyah is in accordance with al-Zahiriyyah, and what is in al-Shurnubalaliyah is in accordance with al-Muhit. By this the conflict between the statements of the people is resolved. End of summary. This is possible, but if it were stated in what the author said, then conflict would occur. In the Musnad.
Source pages p. 428

Commentary
This passage addresses the definition of nisaab (wealth threshold) that disqualifies a person from receiving zakat. The source text is from al-Bahr al-Raiq, a Hanafi commentary on Kanz al-Daqa'iq. It begins by affirming that a Hashemite (descendant of the Prophet) is disallowed from receiving zakat due to his honorable lineage, unlike a wealthy person who is disallowed because of his wealth. The key issue is the ownership of nisaab: if a person owns five grazing camels (or any nisaab of grazing livestock), zakat cannot be given to him, regardless of whether the value equals 200 dirhams. The commentator then discusses a dispute within the Hanafi school. Some scholars, relying on a hadith that defines sufficiency as 200 dirhams or their equivalent, argue that the nisaab is measured by value (200 dirhams) for all types of wealth. Others, like al-Shurnubalali, claim that for counted items like livestock, the nisaab is by count (e.g., 5 camels), not value. This passage reconciles these views by attributing the difference to two narrations from Imam Muhammad al-Shaybani: one considering weight/value and the other considering count for livestock. The relied-upon position (mufta bihi) is that the nisaab is determined by value for monetary items, but for livestock, the count is substituted for weight, so owning five camels of any value disallows receiving zakat. The hadith from Abu Dawud and others supports the consideration of value, but it is interpreted to apply to monetary wealth. This intra-madhhab dispute is resolved by noting that the prohibition is not absolute; rather, it depends on the type of wealth and the effective cause (illa). The passage also clarifies that it is permissible to give zakat to someone who owns less than the nisaab, and such a person may take it if his earnings are insufficient.

Key Terms
النصاب (al-niṣāb): The minimum amount of wealth that makes zakat obligatory and also disqualifies a person from receiving zakat.
السائمة (al-sā'imah): Grazing livestock (camels, cattle, sheep) that are left to pasture for most of the year.
المعبر (al-mu'tabar): The measure considered (value or count) in determining the nisaab.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the hadith cited in the passage, what is the measure of sufficiency that prohibits asking?
A) Five camels
B) Two hundred dirhams or their equivalent
C) One hundred dirhams
D) A full nisaab of any kind

Key Takeaway
The nisaab that prohibits receiving zakat for grazing livestock is determined by count (e.g., 5 camels) in the majority Hanafi view, not by value.

Zakat — Almsgiving
beginner

Prohibition of Giving Zakat to the Slave or Minor Child of a Wealthy Person
States the prohibition and explains the reasoning

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on giving zakat to the slave or minor child of a wealthy person and explain the legal reasoning based on ownership and dependency.

Core Idea
A slave or minor child of a wealthy person is considered wealthy through their master/father, so zakat is prohibited.

This lesson addresses a specific prohibition in zakat distribution: you cannot give zakat to a slave whose master is wealthy, nor to the minor child of a wealthy father. The fundamental reason is that in both cases, the individual is not considered independent in wealth; their sustenance and property are legally attached to the wealthy person. Understanding this ruling helps you correctly identify who is genuinely poor and eligible for zakat.

Guided Path
1
Identify the prohibited recipients
From the text, note that 'his slave' (ʿabd al-ghanī) and 'his young child' (ṭifl al-ghanī) are explicitly prohibited as zakat recipients. The phrase 'his' refers to a wealthy person (ghanī).
The prohibition extends to any zakat-like obligatory charity (e.g., sadaqat al-fitr) as implied by 'what is attached to it'.
2
Explain the legal reasoning (ʿillah)
For the slave: all ownership and earnings belong to the master, who is wealthy. Thus the slave is 'wealthy through his master' and does not meet the need criterion. For the minor child: the father is obligated to provide support, so the child is considered wealthy through the father.
This reasoning is based on the principle that need (faqr) is negated when an individual's necessities are covered by a wealthy party.
3
Recognize the scope and exceptions
The ruling applies to slaves owned by a wealthy master (not a poor one) and to minor children. Once a child reaches puberty and is independent, they can receive zakat if poor. Similarly, a disabled slave not supported by the master may be eligible according to Abu Hanifa.
This highlights the importance of 'actual need' in zakat distribution, which is the underlying criterion.

Source Text

Commentary
This passage is a parsing exercise that clarifies the ruling on giving zakat to the slave and minor child of a wealthy person. The base text from Kanz al-Daqa'iq states that it is not permissible to give zakat to 'his slave' and 'his child'. The commentator explains the reasoning: for the slave, ownership belongs to the master, who is not a recipient of zakat because he is wealthy. The minor child is considered wealthy through the father's wealth, as the father is obligated to support him. The passage then delves into a related dispute about whether the prohibition applies only if the slave/child own nisaab themselves. It cites al-Shurnubalali who claims that some earlier commentators made a mistake by suggesting that the prohibition depends on the value of the slave's property. The commentator refutes this by quoting al-Inayah and other sources, arguing that the issue is about the master's wealth, not the slave's. The passage also discusses the nisaab threshold for livestock, but that is secondary here. The main ruling is straightforward: zakat cannot be given to the slave or minor child of a wealthy person because they are legally considered wealthy through their connection to the master/father. Exceptions are noted in later parts: if the slave is a disabled person not cared for by the master, or if the child is an adult, the ruling differs. The passage also mentions that giving zakat to the rich person's wife or father is permissible, as they are not considered wealthy through him.

Key Terms
عبد الغني (ʿabd al-ghanī): A slave whose master is wealthy; the slave's wealth belongs to the master, so he is considered wealthy through his master.
طفل الغني (ṭifl al-ghanī): The minor child of a wealthy father; the child is considered wealthy because the father provides for him.

Key Takeaway
Zakat cannot be given to a slave or minor child of a wealthy person because they are legally considered wealthy through their master/father, unless actual need overrides that attribution.

The Prohibition of Zakat for Banu Hashim and Their Clients
States the prohibition with hadith proof

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling prohibiting zakat for Banu Hashim and their mawali and cite the primary hadith evidence.

Core Idea
Zakat is forbidden for Banu Hashim and their clients based on authentic hadith.

This lesson explains a well-established ruling in Hanafi fiqh: the prohibition of giving obligatory charity (zakat) to the clan of the Prophet (Banu Hashim) and their freed slaves. The ruling is based on explicit hadith texts that clarify the special status of the Prophet's household. Understanding who is included and why is essential for proper zakat distribution.

Guided Path
1
Identify the subject and ruling
Recognise that the passage states it is not permissible to give zakat to Banu Hashim and their mawali (clients). The ruling is a prohibition.
The ruling applies to all obligatory charity, including zakat and sadaqat al-fitr.
2
Recite the hadith proof
The commentator cites two hadiths: the Prophet said 'We, the people of the household, charity is not lawful for us' (Bukhari) and 'The mawla of a people is from themselves, and charity is not lawful for us' (Abu Dawud).
These hadiths establish the prohibition definitively.
3
Note the scope includes all Hashimis regardless of support
The prohibition is general for all Banu Hashim, whether they supported the Prophet or opposed him, like the son of Abu Lahab. The basis is lineage, not personal loyalty.
Thus, even non-practicing Hashimis are prohibited from receiving zakat.
4
Understand extension to mawali
The second hadith equates the mawla (client) to the family itself, so the prohibition extends to freed slaves of Banu Hashim.
This is a matter of ikhtilaf between schools, but Hanafi school holds it.
5
Confirm the basis is lineage, not need
The prohibition is tied to kinship with the Prophet, not to poverty or wealth. Even poor Hashimis cannot receive zakat.
They may receive other forms of charity (e.g., voluntary sadaqah).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله (وبنى هاشم ومواليهم أي لا يجوز الدفع لهم لحديث البخاري «نحن أهل بيت لا تحل لنا الصدقة (۱) والحديث أبي داود مولى القوم من أنفسهم وإنا لا تحل لنا الصدقة . أطلق في بني هاشم فشمل من كان ناصراً للنبي الا الله ومن لم يكن ناصراً له منهم كولد أبي لهب، فيدخل من أسلم منهم في حرمة الصدقة لكونه هاشمياً فإن تحريم الصدقة حكم يختص بالقرابة من بني هاشم لا بالنصرة. كذا في غاية البيان وقيده المصنف في الكافي تبعاً حاجة إليه لعدم التنافي تأمل قوله : (خلافاً لما روي عن أبي يوسف في الأخير) أي الزمن الذي ليس في عيال ،مولاه، وقوله واختاره في «الذخيرة وفيه نظر فإنه في الذخيرة حكاه بقوله «وعن أبي يوسف ولم أر في كلامه ما يقتضي اختياره ومجرد الحكاية لقول لا يفيد اختياره تأمل قوله : (وقد يقال الخ) قال العلامة المقدسي أقول : إن أريد أن المولى ليس بمصرف لغناه فابن السبيبل غني ولا صدقة لغني، أو يقال : العبد المذكور لا ينزل حالة عن مأذون مديون وهو لا يملك المولى كسبه عند أبي حنيفة فجاز الصرف إليه فليجز ههنا للضرورة المذكورة، ويجوز أن يخالف أبو يوسف أصله فيه للضرورة اهـ.
His saying: (and Banu Hashim and their mawali) i.e., it is not permissible to give to them due to the hadith of al-Bukhari: 'We are the people of the household; charity is not lawful for us.' And the hadith of Abu Dawud: 'The mawla of a people is from themselves, and charity is not lawful for us.' It is general in Banu Hashim, so it includes those who were supporters of the Prophet, peace be upon him, and those who were not supporters of him, like the son of Abu Lahab. So whoever among them accepts Islam is included in the prohibition of sadaqah because he is a Hashimi. Ponder his saying: (and it could be said etc.) al-Allamah al-Maqdisi said: I say: If it is intended that the master is not a recipient because of his wealth, then ibn al-sabil is wealthy and no sadaqah is given to a wealthy person. Or it could be said: the mentioned slave does not descend to the level of a permitted debtor, and he does not own his earnings according to Abu Hanifa, so it is permissible to give to him. So let it be permissible here for the mentioned necessity. And it is possible that Abu Yusuf differs from his principle in this case due to necessity. End.
Source pages p. 429

Commentary
This passage is an expansion probe that explains the prohibition of giving zakat to Banu Hashim (the clan of the Prophet) and their mawali (clients). The source text from Kanz al-Daqa'iq states the prohibition, and the commentator provides the proof from authentic hadiths: al-Bukhari's narration that the Prophet said, 'We, the people of the household, charity is not lawful for us,' and Abu Dawud's narration that the mawla of a people is considered from them, so the same prohibition applies. The commentator clarifies that the prohibition is based on lineage (being a Hashimi), not on whether the person supported the Prophet or not. Thus, even descendants of Abu Lahab (who opposed the Prophet) are included if they are Hashimi. The prohibition applies to all types of obligatory charity (zakat and sadaqah fitr). The passage also addresses a related intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the disabled slave (zaman) who is not under the care of his master. According to a narration from Abu Yusuf, such a slave cannot receive zakat because his property belongs to the master. However, Abu Hanifa permits it by analogy to a ma'dhun (slave permitted to trade) who owes debt, where the master does not own his earnings. The commentator leans towards Abu Hanifa's view, noting that the necessity of the slave's need justifies the permissibility. The passage also mentions that the prohibition is specific to zakat; other forms of charity like voluntary sadaqah may be given to Banu Hashim.

Key Terms
بني هاشم (Banī Hāshim): The clan of the Prophet Muhammad, including his descendants and relatives. They are prohibited from receiving zakat.
مواليهم (mawālīhim): Clients (freed slaves) of Banu Hashim; they are considered part of the clan in this ruling.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) Because they are not Muslims.
B) Because they are considered part of the family based on the hadith 'the mawla of a people is from them.'
C) Because they are wealthy.
D) Because they have their own lineage.

Key Takeaway
The prohibition of zakat for Banu Hashim is based on lineage, not poverty or support of Islam, and extends to their mawali.

Zakat — Almsgiving

The Dispute Regarding the Disabled Slave of a Hashimi Master
Discusses intra-Hanafi dispute about disabled slave of Hashimi

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the intra-Hanafi dispute concerning giving zakat to a disabled slave owned by a Hashimi master and state the mufta bihi.

Core Idea
Abu Hanifa permits zakat for a disabled Hashimi slave by qiyas, differing from a narration of Abu Yusuf.

The previous lesson established that Banu Hashim and their mawali cannot receive zakat. A nuanced question arises: can a disabled slave (zaman) who is owned by a Hashimi master receive zakat? The slave is not a Hashimi descendant, but his earning capacity belongs to his master (who is Hashimi). This lesson examines the opinions of Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf on this case, highlighting the use of qiyas and consideration of necessity.

Guided Path
1
Identify the case under dispute
The case concerns a disabled slave (zaman) who is owned by a Hashimi master and is not under the master's care. Can zakat be given to this slave?
The slave himself is not Hashimi, but his property (including what he earns) belongs to the master.
2
State Abu Yusuf's position (transmitted view)
According to a narration from Abu Yusuf, zakat cannot be given to such a slave because the master is a Hashimi (prohibited) and the slave's earnings ultimately belong to the master, so the benefit would reach the Hashimi.
This view is based on the principle of blocking the means (sadd al-dhara'i).
3
State Abu Hanifa's position
Abu Hanifa permits giving zakat to this slave. He analogizes (qiyas) to the case of a slave permitted to trade (ma'dhun) who is indebted: in that case, the master does not own the slave's earnings, so giving zakat to the slave does not benefit the master.
The key similarity: in both cases, the master does not have ownership of the slave's earnings.
4
Understand the illa (effective cause)
The illa for permissibility is that the master does not own the earnings of the slave. For the disabled slave, since he is unable to work, his earnings are non-existent, so the master does not own them. Therefore, giving zakat to him does not reach the Hashimi master.
This is a qiyas khafi (subtle analogy).
5
Determine the mufta bihi (relied-upon position)
The later Hanafi jurists, including the author of al-Dhakhirah and al-Maqdisi, lean towards Abu Hanifa's position. The necessity of the slave's need also supports this view.
The commentator in the passage notes that the narration from Abu Yusuf may have been for a specific context, and the general principle favours Abu Hanifa.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله (وبنى هاشم ومواليهم أي لا يجوز الدفع لهم لحديث البخاري «نحن أهل بيت لا تحل لنا الصدقة (۱) والحديث أبي داود مولى القوم من أنفسهم وإنا لا تحل لنا الصدقة . أطلق في بني هاشم فشمل من كان ناصراً للنبي الا الله ومن لم يكن ناصراً له منهم كولد أبي لهب، فيدخل من أسلم منهم في حرمة الصدقة لكونه هاشمياً فإن تحريم الصدقة حكم يختص بالقرابة من بني هاشم لا بالنصرة. كذا في غاية البيان وقيده المصنف في الكافي تبعاً حاجة إليه لعدم التنافي تأمل قوله : (خلافاً لما روي عن أبي يوسف في الأخير) أي الزمن الذي ليس في عيال ،مولاه، وقوله واختاره في «الذخيرة وفيه نظر فإنه في الذخيرة حكاه بقوله «وعن أبي يوسف ولم أر في كلامه ما يقتضي اختياره ومجرد الحكاية لقول لا يفيد اختياره تأمل قوله : (وقد يقال الخ) قال العلامة المقدسي أقول : إن أريد أن المولى ليس بمصرف لغناه فابن السبيبل غني ولا صدقة لغني، أو يقال : العبد المذكور لا ينزل حالة عن مأذون مديون وهو لا يملك المولى كسبه عند أبي حنيفة فجاز الصرف إليه فليجز ههنا للضرورة المذكورة، ويجوز أن يخالف أبو يوسف أصله فيه للضرورة اهـ.
His saying: (and Banu Hashim and their mawali) i.e., it is not permissible to give to them due to the hadith of al-Bukhari: 'We are the people of the household; charity is not lawful for us.' And the hadith of Abu Dawud: 'The mawla of a people is from themselves, and charity is not lawful for us.' It is general in Banu Hashim, so it includes those who were supporters of the Prophet, peace be upon him, and those who were not supporters of him, like the son of Abu Lahab. So whoever among them accepts Islam is included in the prohibition of sadaqah because he is a Hashimi. For the prohibition of sadaqah is a ruling specific to the kinship of Banu Hashim, not to support. This is in Ghayat al-Bayan. And the author restricted it in al-Kafi following [something] due to need to avoid contradiction. Ponder his saying: (contrary to what is narrated from Abu Yusuf in the last [case]) i.e., the disabled person who is not under the care of his master. And his saying: 'and he chose it in al-Dhakhirah' but there is consideration in it, because in al-Dhakhirah he narrated it by saying 'and from Abu Yusuf' and I did not see in his words anything that indicates his choosing it, and mere narration of a saying does not indicate choosing it. Ponder his saying: (and it could be said etc.) al-Allamah al-Maqdisi said: I say: If it is intended that the master is not a recipient because of his wealth, then ibn al-sabil is wealthy and no sadaqah is given to a wealthy person. Or it could be said: the mentioned slave does not descend to the level of a permitted debtor, and he does not own his earnings according to Abu Hanifa, so it is permissible to give to him. So let it be permissible here for the mentioned necessity. And it is possible that Abu Yusuf differs from his principle in this case due to necessity. End.
Source pages p. 429

Commentary
This passage is an expansion probe that explains the prohibition of giving zakat to Banu Hashim (the clan of the Prophet) and their mawali (clients). The source text from Kanz al-Daqa'iq states the prohibition, and the commentator provides the proof from authentic hadiths: al-Bukhari's narration that the Prophet said, 'We, the people of the household, charity is not lawful for us,' and Abu Dawud's narration that the mawla of a people is considered from them, so the same prohibition applies. The commentator clarifies that the prohibition is based on lineage (being a Hashimi), not on whether the person supported the Prophet or not. Thus, even descendants of Abu Lahab (who opposed the Prophet) are included if they are Hashimi. The prohibition applies to all types of obligatory charity (zakat and sadaqah fitr). The passage also addresses a related intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the disabled slave (zaman) who is not under the care of his master. According to a narration from Abu Yusuf, such a slave cannot receive zakat because his property belongs to the master. However, Abu Hanifa permits it by analogy to a ma'dhun (slave permitted to trade) who owes debt, where the master does not own his earnings. The commentator leans towards Abu Hanifa's view, noting that the necessity of the slave's need justifies the permissibility. The passage also mentions that the prohibition is specific to zakat; other forms of charity like voluntary sadaqah may be given to Banu Hashim.

Key Terms
زمن (zaman): A disabled or chronically ill slave who is unable to work.
مأذون (ma'dhūn): A slave permitted by his master to engage in trade; he may incur debts but his earnings belong to the master unless debts exceed value.

B) Because the master does not own the slave's earnings, so giving zakat does not benefit the Hashimi.
C) Because all slaves are eligible for zakat regardless of master.
D) Because the prohibition for Hashimi is only for direct descendants, not their property.

Key Takeaway
Despite the general prohibition, giving zakat to a disabled slave owned by a Hashimi is permissible according to the mufta bihi, based on qiyas and the absence of benefit to the master.

Zakat to a Slave of a Wealthy Absent Master
Ruling on zakat to a slave of a wealthy absent master with no earning capacity

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on giving zakat to a slave whose wealthy master is absent and who cannot earn, explain the Sahibayn's objection, and identify the mufta bihi.

Core Idea
Abu Hanifa permits zakat to a slave of an absent wealthy master who cannot earn, analogizing to ibn al-sabil.

The general rule prohibits giving zakat to a slave of a wealthy master because the master benefits. However, Abu Hanifa identified an exception when the master is absent and the slave cannot earn. This lesson unpacks the reasoning and the intra-Hanafi dispute between Abu Hanifa and his two companions, Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its default ruling
The matn lists 'his slave' among those prohibited from receiving zakat. Determine the default ruling and the condition that creates an exception.
The exception is when the master is wealthy, absent, and the slave has no earning capacity.
2
Analyze the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf
Abu Hanifa permits giving zakat to such a slave. The Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) prohibit it. Identify the key difference in their reasoning: ownership of the slave's earnings.
Abu Hanifa argues the master does not own the earnings in this situation; Sahibayn hold ownership is not negated.
3
Trace the istidlal: analogy to ibn al-sabil
Abu Hanifa's justification is that the slave, when unable to earn and the master absent, is like a wayfarer (ibn al-sabil) who is a valid zakat recipient. Understand this analogy.
The text critiques this analogy but upholds the permissibility.
4
Determine the mufta bihi
The preferred view in the school is Abu Hanifa's permissibility. Note the tarjih authority from Fath al-Qadir and the text's author.
Thus the fatwa permits giving zakat to such a slave.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وعبده وطفله وبني هاشم ومواليهم ولو دفع بتحر فبان أنه غني أو هاشمي أو كافر أو المديون المستغرق لما في يده ورقبته، أما هو فيجوز دفعها له لعدم ملك المولى إكسابه في هذه الحالة عند الإمام لما عرف خلافاً لهما وأطلق العبد فشمل القن والمدبر وأم الولد والزمن الذي ليس في عيال مولاه ولم يجد شيئاً أو كان مولاه غائباً خلافاً لما روي عن أبي يوسف في الأخير واختاره في الذخيرة لأنه لا ينفي وقوع الملك لمولاه بهذا العارض. وقد يجاب بأنه عند غيبة مولاه الغني وعدم قدرته على الكسب لا ينزل عن حال ابن السبيل. كذا في فتح القدير. وقد يقال : إن الملك هنا يقع للمولى وهو ليس بمصرف وأما ابن السبيل فمصرف فالأولى الإطلاق كما هو المذهب، وقد تقدم أن الدفع إلى مكاتب الغني جائز، وإنما منع من الدفع لطفل الغني لأنه يعد غنياً بغناء أبيه. كذا قالوا وهو يفيد أن الدفع لولد الغنية جائز إذ لا يعد غنياً بغناء أمه ولو لم يكن له أب، وقد صرح به في القنية. وأطلق الطفل فشمل الذكر والأنثى ومن هو عيال الأب أو لا على الصحيح لوجود العلة، وقيد بالطفل لأن الدفع لولد الغني إذا كان كبيراً جائز مطلقاً، وقيد بعبده وطفله لأن الدفع إلى أب الغني وزوجته جائز سواء فرض لها نفقة أو لا.
And his slave and his child and Banu Hashim and their clients, and if he paid after searching and it became clear that he is rich or a Hashimi or a disbeliever or a debtor whose debt consumes what is in his hand and his neck, as for him (the slave), it is permissible to give to him because the master does not own his earning in this situation according to Imam (Abu Hanifa), as known, contrary to them (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad). And the word 'slave' is unrestricted, including the full slave, the mudabbar, the umm walad, and the disabled one who is not under his master's care and finds nothing, or if his master is absent, contrary to what is narrated from Abu Yusuf regarding the last case, and he (the commentator) chose it in al-Dhakhirah because this does not negate the occurrence of ownership for the master by this obstacle. And it can be answered that when his wealthy master is absent and he is unable to earn, he does not descend from the status of a wayfarer (ibn al-sabil). Thus in Fath al-Qadir. And it may be said that ownership here occurs for the master, and he is not a recipient (of zakat), whereas the wayfarer is a recipient, so the better view is the unrestricted (permissibility) as is the school. And it has preceded that giving to a mukatib of a wealthy person is permissible. And the prohibition of giving to a child of a wealthy person is only because he is considered wealthy through the wealth of his father. Thus they said, and it indicates that giving to a child of a wealthy mother is permissible, since he is not considered wealthy through the wealth of his mother, even if he has no father, and this is explicitly stated in al-Qunyah. And the word 'child' is unrestricted, including male and female, and whether he is under the father's care or not, according to the correct view due to the existence of the cause. And it is restricted to a child because giving to an adult son of a wealthy person is absolutely permissible. And it is restricted to 'his slave and his child' because giving to the father or wife of a wealthy person is permissible, whether maintenance is prescribed for her or not.
Source pages p. 429

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on a matn (likely Kanz al-Daqaiq) that lists those to whom zakat cannot be given. It clarifies two categories: the slave and the child of a wealthy person. The ruling on the slave: According to Abu Hanifa, if a slave's master is wealthy but absent, and the slave cannot earn, it is permissible to give zakat to the slave because the master does not own the slave's earnings in this situation. Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (the Sahibayn) disagree, holding that the master's ownership is not negated. The preferred view (mufta bihi) is that of Abu Hanifa, comparing the slave to a wayfarer (ibn al-sabil). The ruling on the child of a wealthy person: Zakat cannot be given to a minor child of a wealthy father because the child is considered wealthy through the father's wealth. However, if the mother is wealthy and the father is dead or absent, giving zakat to the child is permissible because the child is not considered wealthy through the mother's wealth. For an adult child of a wealthy parent, regardless of gender, it is permissible to give zakat. Additionally, giving zakat to the father or wife of a wealthy person is permissible.

Key Terms
عبد (ʿabd): A slave; in this context, any type of slave (full, mudabbar, umm walad) whose master is wealthy and absent, and who cannot earn.
ابن السبيل (ibn al-sabīl): A wayfarer; a traveler in need, who is a valid recipient of zakat even if he has wealth back home. Used as analogy for the slave without earning capacity.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school (mufta bihi), when is it permissible to give zakat to a slave?
A) When the slave is a mukatib.
B) When the master is wealthy and absent, and the slave cannot earn.
C) When the slave is owned by a poor master.
D) Always, because slaves are poor.

Key Takeaway
Zakat to a slave of a wealthy absent master who cannot earn is permissible by Abu Hanifa's view, which is the relied-upon position.

Zakat to the Child of a Wealthy Person
Ruling on giving zakat to a minor child of a wealthy person, with distinction between father and mother

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on giving zakat to a minor child of a wealthy person and distinguish between the effects of the father's wealth and the mother's wealth.

Core Idea
A minor child is considered wealthy through the father's wealth, not through the mother's.

The general rule prohibits giving zakat to a minor child of a wealthy father because the child is deemed wealthy by virtue of the father's wealth. However, if the father is not wealthy (e.g., poor or absent), even if the mother is wealthy, the child is eligible for zakat. This lesson explains the 'illa (cause) and its scope.

Guided Path
1
Identify the prohibition and its basis
The matn prohibits giving zakat to 'his child' (the wealthy person's child). Understand that this applies to minor children because they are considered wealthy through their father's wealth.
The text specifies 'minor child' (طفل) and not adult child.
2
Distinguish between father's and mother's wealth
The prohibition is specifically tied to the father's wealth. The child is not considered wealthy through the mother's wealth, so if the father is not wealthy, the child can receive zakat even if the mother is wealthy.
This is explicitly stated in al-Qunyah.
3
Note the scope: minor vs. adult
The ruling applies only to minor children. Adult children of a wealthy person may receive zakat without restriction because they are not automatically deemed wealthy through their parents.
The text says 'restricted to a child' (قيد بالطفل).

Source Text

Zakat — Almsgiving
