Prohibition of Zakat for Banu Hashim: Dalil and Ruling
Ruling on prohibition of obligatory charity to Banu Hashim and clients.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling that giving obligatory zakat to Banu Hashim and their clients is prohibited, and identify the primary hadith evidence and its legal implication.

Core Idea
Obligatory charity is haram for Banu Hashim and their clients, proven by explicit hadith.

This lesson presents the foundational ruling that it is impermissible to give obligatory zakat (and similar obligatory charities) to the clan of Banu Hashim, including the Prophet's family, and to their clients (mawali). The evidence comes from authentic hadith, and the ruling is a specific honor for the Prophet's lineage. We will explore the proof and the exact scope of the prohibition.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
Determine what act is being ruled upon and what the hukm (legal classification) is. The act is giving obligatory charity (zakat, etc.) to Banu Hashim and their clients. The ruling is prohibition (haram).
Obligatory charity includes zakat, nadhr (vowed charity), 'ushr (tithe), and kaffarah (expiation).
2
Locate the dalil (evidence)
Find the primary proof cited. The text quotes the hadith from Sahih al-Bukhari: 'We are the people of the household; charity is not lawful for us.' Also the hadith from Sunan Abi Dawud extends this to clients.
The hadith is ahad (single-transmitter) but its meaning is clear and accepted by the Hanafi school.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah (indication of proof)
Analyze how the hadith yields the ruling. The phrase 'la tahillu' (is not lawful) is a negation of permissibility, which indicates prohibition in Hanafi usul. The text explicitly states the prohibition.
The ruling is based on the explicit wording (dalala qat'iyya) even though the transmission is zanni.
4
Note the extension to clients
Understand that the prohibition applies not only to Banu Hashim but also to their clients (freed slaves). The hadith 'the client of a people is from among themselves' establishes this extension.
This is based on the principle of legal identity between a person and their mawla.
5
Determine the practical consequence (thamarah)
Recognize that this prohibition applies to all obligatory charities, not just zakat. The commentator explicitly mentions zakat, nadhr, 'ushr, and kaffarah. This is a significant practical scope.
It does not apply to voluntary charity, which is a separate topic.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله (وبنى هاشم ومواليهم أي لا يجوز الدفع لهم لحديث البخاري «نحن أهل بيت لا تحل لنا الصدقة» والحديث أبي داود مولى القوم من أنفسهم وإنا لا تحل لنا الصدقة . أطلق في بني هاشم فشمل من كان ناصراً للنبي صلى الله عليه وسلم ومن لم يكن ناصراً له منهم كولد أبي لهب، فيدخل من أسلم منهم في حرمة الصدقة لكونه هاشمياً فإن تحريم الصدقة حكم يختص بالقرابة من بني هاشم لا بالنصرة. كذا في غاية البيان وقيده المصنف في الكافي تبعاً لما في الهداية وشروحها بآل علي وعباس وجعفر وعقيل وحرث بن عبد المطلب، ومشى عليه الشارح الزيلعي والمحقق في فتح القدير وصرحا بإخراج أبي لهب وأولاده الحكم لأن حرمة الصدقة لبني هاشم كرامة من الله تعالى لهم ولذريتهم حيث نصروه عليه الصلاة والسلام في جاهليتهم وإسلامهم، وأبو لهب كان حريصاً على أذى النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم فلم يستحقها بنوه، واختاره المصنف في المستصفى وروى حديثاً لا قرابة بيني وبين أبي لهب». ونص في البدائع على أن الكرخي قيد بني هاشم بالخمسة من بني هاشم فكان المذهب التقييد لأن الإمام الكرخي ممن هو أعلم بمذهب أصحابنا وقيد ببني هاشم لأن بني المطلب تحل لهم الصدقة وليسوا كبني هاشم وإن استووا في القرابة لأن عبد النبي لأنه محمد بن عبد الله بن عبد المطلب بن هاشم بن عبد مناف، ولعبد مناف أربعة بنين : هاشم والمطلب ونوفل وعبد شمس والخمسة المذكورون من بني هاشم لأن العباس والحرث عمان للنبي ، وجعفر وعقيل أخوان لعلي بن أبي طالب وهو عم النبي ، وكان لأبي طالب أربعة من الأولاد ولد له طالب فمات ولم يعقب وكان بينه وبين عقيل عشر سنين وبين عقيل وجعفر عشر سنين وبين جعفر وعلي عشر سنين، وأمهم فاطمة أسد بن هاشم بن عبد مناف. كذا في غاية البيان وجمهرة النسب. وقال المصنف في الكافي وهذا في الواجبات كالزكاة والنذر والعشر والكفارة، أما التطوع والوقف فيجوز الصرف إليهم لأن المؤدي في الواجب يطهر نفسه بإسقاط الفرض فيتدنس المؤدى كالماء المستعمل، وفي النفل تبرع بما ليس عليه فلا يتدنس به المؤدى كمن تبرد بالماء اهـ. وإنما لم تلحق صدقة التطوع لهم بالوضوء على الوضوء فيتدنس به المؤدى لأن الأصل يقتضي عدمه وإنما قلتا به في الماء للنص الوارد «الوضوء على نور» إذ ازدياد النور يقتضي زوال الظلمة بقدره لا محالة. كذا في النهاية مختصراً. وفيها عن العتابي : إن النفل جائز لهم بالإجماع كالنفل للغني وتبعه صاحب المعراج واختاره في المحيط مقتصراً عليه وعزاه إلى النوادر ومشى عليه الأقطع في شرح القدوري، واختاره في غاية البيان ولم ينقل غيره شارح المجمع فكان هو المذهب.
His statement (and Banu Hashim and their clients) means it is not permissible to give to them due to the hadith of al-Bukhari: 'We are the people of the household; charity is not lawful for us' and the hadith of Abu Dawud: 'The client of a people is from among themselves, and charity is not lawful for us.' It is unrestricted in Banu Hashim, so it includes those who supported the Prophet (peace be upon him) and those who did not support him, like the child of Abu Lahab. So whoever among them accepts Islam enters into the prohibition of charity because he is a Hashimi; the prohibition of charity is a ruling specific to kinship to Banu Hashim, not to support. Thus in Ghayat al-Bayan. And the author in al-Kafi restricted it, following what is in al-Hidayah and its commentaries, to the families of Ali, Abbas, Ja'far, Aqil, and Harith ibn Abd al-Muttalib. And al-Zayla'i and the muhaqqiq in Fath al-Qadir walked upon this and explicitly stated the exclusion of Abu Lahab and his children from the ruling, because the prohibition of charity for Banu Hashim is an honor from Allah to them and their descendants for supporting him (peace be upon him) in their Jahiliyyah and Islam, and Abu Lahab was eager to harm the Prophet (peace be upon him), so his children did not deserve it. And the author chose it in al-Mustasfa and narrated a hadith: 'There is no kinship between me and Abu Lahab.' And it is stated in al-Bada'i that al-Karkhi restricted Banu Hashim to the five from Banu Hashim, so the school became the restriction because Imam al-Karkhi is among those most knowledgeable of the school of our companions. And it is restricted to Banu Hashim because Banu al-Muttalib charity is lawful for them, and they are not like Banu Hashim even if they are equal in kinship, because 'Abd of the Prophet is Muhammad ibn Abdullah ibn Abd al-Muttalib ibn Hashim ibn Abd Manaf. And Abd Manaf had four sons: Hashim, al-Muttalib, Nawfal, and Abd Shams. And the five mentioned are from Banu Hashim because al-Abbas and al-Harith are uncles of the Prophet, and Ja'far and Aqil are brothers of Ali ibn Abi Talib, and he (Ali) is the cousin of the Prophet. And Abu Talib had four children: Talib died without offspring, and between him and Aqil was ten years, between Aqil and Ja'far ten years, and between Ja'far and Ali ten years, and their mother was Fatima bint Asad ibn Hashim ibn Abd Manaf. Thus in Ghayat al-Bayan and Jamharat al-Nasab. And the author said in al-Kafi: This applies to obligatory charities like zakat, nadhr, 'ushr, and kaffarah. As for voluntary charity and waqf, it is permissible to give to them because the payer in the obligatory charity purifies himself by fulfilling the obligation, so the recipient becomes defiled like used water; whereas in supererogatory charity, he gives a gift out of his own accord, so the recipient is not defiled like one who cools himself with water. And the analogy of voluntary charity for them (being allowed) is not like wudu upon wudu where the recipient becomes defiled, because the principle requires its absence, and we only said so in water due to the text 'wudu upon wudu' (wudu upon light) for the increase of light necessarily removes darkness to its extent. Thus in al-Nihayah briefly. And in it from al-Utabi: Supererogatory charity is permissible for them by consensus, like supererogatory charity for the rich. And the author of al-Mi'raj followed him, and al-Muhit chose it, confining to it, and attributed it to al-Nawadir. And al-Aqta' in Sharh al-Quduri followed it, and Ghayat al-Bayan chose it, and no other (view) was transmitted by Sharh al-Majma', so it is the school.
Source pages p. 429

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the ruling that zakat is forbidden for Banu Hashim (the clan of the Prophet) and their clients (mawali). The proof is the hadith: 'We are the people of the household; charity is not lawful for us,' and 'The client of a people is from among themselves, and charity is not lawful for us.' The text discusses whether the prohibition includes all descendants of Hashim or only specific families. It states that the apparently unrestricted text includes even those who did not support the Prophet, like the children of Abu Lahab. However, many scholars, following the author of al-Kafi and al-Hidayah, restrict the prohibition to the five families: the descendants of Ali, Abbas, Ja'far, Aqil, and Harith ibn Abd al-Muttalib. This is because the prohibition is an honor for those who supported the Prophet, and Abu Lahab was an enemy. The relied-upon view is this restriction, following Imam al-Karkhi. The passage then distinguishes between obligatory charity (zakat, nadhr, 'ushr, kaffarah) and voluntary charity (sadaqat al-tatawwu') and waqf. Obligatory charity is forbidden for Banu Hashim because the payer is purifying himself, so the recipient is like used water. Voluntary charity is permissible because it is a gift, not an obligation. This is supported by consensus and is the chosen school of the Hanafis.

Key Terms
بني هاشم (Banū Hāshim): The clan of the Prophet Muhammad; zakat is forbidden for them due to honor and specific hadith. In this text, the prohibition is restricted to the five specific families.
مولى القوم (mawlā al-qawm): A client or freed slave of Banu Hashim; they are treated like their patrons in the prohibition.
صدقة التطوع (ṣadaqat al-taṭawwuʿ): Voluntary charity, as opposed to obligatory zakat; it is permissible for Banu Hashim.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following is NOT covered by the prohibition of giving obligatory charity to Banu Hashim?
A) Zakat al-Fitr
B) Sadaqat al-Tatawwu'
C) Kaffarah
D) Nadhr

Key Takeaway
Obligatory charity is haram for Banu Hashim and their clients; voluntary charity is halal.

Zakat — Almsgiving
intermediate

Scope of Banu Hashim and Exception for Voluntary Charity
Resolution of intra-Hanafi dispute on scope and exception for voluntary charity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the intra-Hanafi dispute on which families are included in Banu Hashim for the prohibition of zakat, and state the rationale for allowing voluntary charity.

Core Idea
The prohibition is limited to five families; voluntary charity is universally permitted.

While the prohibition of obligatory charity for Banu Hashim is clear, the exact scope of 'Banu Hashim' is debated within the Hanafi school. This lesson explores the restriction to the five families of Ali, Abbas, Ja'far, Aqil, and Harith, and the exclusion of Abu Lahab's line. It also explains the distinction between obligatory and voluntary charity using a water analogy.

Guided Path
1
Identify the conflicting views on scope
The matn originally says 'Banu Hashim' unrestrictedly, but many scholars restrict it to specific families. Identify the two positions: a) all descendants of Hashim, b) only the five families.
The restriction is based on the idea of honor for supporters of the Prophet.
2
Present the evidence for restriction
The passage argues that the prohibition is an honor for those who supported the Prophet. Abu Lahab was an enemy, so his children are excluded. A hadith is cited: 'There is no kinship between me and Abu Lahab.'
This is not a rejection of lineage but a ruling specific to the prohibition.
3
Note the role of Imam al-Karkhi
Imam al-Karkhi is cited as the authority who restricted the scope to the five families. His knowledge of the Hanafi school is used to settle the matter.
Al-Karkhi was a leading Hanafi jurist; his view is considered authoritative.
4
Identify the exception for voluntary charity
The passage explicitly states that voluntary charity (tatawwu') and waqf are permissible for Banu Hashim. This is supported by consensus (ijma).
The rationale is that in obligatory charity, the payer is purifying himself, so the recipient is 'defiled'; in voluntary charity, the payer is giving a gift, so no defilement occurs.
5
Confirm the mufta bihi position
The relied-upon view in the Hanafi school is the restriction to the five families and the permissibility of voluntary charity. This is the position followed in fatwa.
Other views are acknowledged but not acted upon.

Definition and Prohibition of Obligatory Charity for Banī Hāshim
Defining the five members of Banī Hāshim and ruling on obligatory charity

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the five members of Banī Hāshim, explain the prohibition of obligatory charity for them, and apply the exclusion of Abu Lahab's lineage.

Core Idea
Obligatory charity is haram for the five specified Hashimites as an honor, excluding Abu Lahab.

This passage from Bahr al-Ra’iq defines exactly who among the Prophet’s family is prohibited from receiving obligatory charity (zakat, kaffara, etc.). The Hanafi school restricts this honor to five individuals and their descendants, based on genealogical reasoning and a hadith severing Abu Lahab’s kinship. Understanding this definition is essential for correctly distributing zakat and avoiding unlawful consumption.

Guided Path
1
Identify the legal question
What is the ruling regarding obligatory charity for the Prophet's family, and who exactly is included?
2
Examine the definition in al-Hidayah and commentaries
The passage states that Banī Hāshim consists of the families of Ali, Abbas, Ja'far, Aqeel, and Harith ibn Abd al-Muttalib.
These are the five specified individuals.
3
Understand the genealogical reasoning
Trace the lineage: Abbas and Harith are the Prophet’s uncles; Ali, Ja'far, and Aqeel are descendants of Abu Talib. Banī al-Muttalib are excluded because they are from a different branch.
The Prophet’s grandfather is Hashim ibn Abd Manaf; Banī al-Muttalib descend from Muttalib, not Hashim.
4
Apply the dalil for excluding Abu Lahab
The hadith 'There is no kinship between me and Abu Lahab' severs ties, so his descendants are not included in the honor and can receive obligatory charity.
This hadith is zanni al-thubut but its dalala is qati.
5
Derive the mufta bihi ruling
The relied-upon position: obligatory charity is haram for the five and their progeny; permissible for Abu Lahab's line and Banī al-Muttalib.
This follows Imam al-Karkhi and is endorsed by al-Marghinani.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لما في الهداية وشروحها بآل علي وعباس وجعفر وعقيل وحرث بن عبد المطلب، ومشى عليه الشارح الزيلعي والمحقق في فتح القدير وصرحا بإخراج أبي لهب وأولاده الحكم لأن حرمة الصدقة لبني هاشم كرامة من الله تعالى لهم ولذريتهم حيث نصروه عليه الصلاة والسلام في جاهليتهم وإسلامهم، وأبو لهب كان حريصاً على أذى النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم فلم يستحقها بنوه، واختاره المصنف في المستصفى وروى حديثاً لا قرابة بيني وبين أبي لهب». ونص في البدائع على أن الكرخي قيد بني هاشم بالخمسة من بني هاشم فكان المذهب التقييد لأن الإمام الكرخي ممن هو أعلم بمذهب أصحابنا وقيد ببني هاشم لأن بني المطلب تحل لهم الصدقة وليسوا كبني هاشم وإن استووا في القرابة لأن عبد النبي لأنه محمد بن عبد الله بن عبد المطلب بن هاشم بن عبد مناف، ولعبد مناف أربعة بنين : هاشم والمطلب ونوفل وعبد شمس والخمسة المذكورون من بني هاشم لأن العباس والحرث عمان للنبي ، وجعفر وعقيل أخوان لعلي بن أبي طالب وهو عم النبي ، وكان لأبي طالب أربعة من الأولاد ولد له طالب فمات ولم يعقب وكان بينه بنت مناف جد وبين عقيل عشر سنين وبين عقيل وجعفر عشر سنين وبين جعفر وعلي عشر سنين، وأمهم فاطمة أسد بن هاشم بن عبد مناف. كذا في غاية البيان وجمهرة النسب.
For in al-Hidayah and its commentaries: the family of Ali, Abbas, Ja'far, Aqeel, and Harith ibn Abd al-Muttalib. Al-Zayla'i and the editor of Fath al-Qadir followed this and explicitly excluded Abu Lahab and his children from the ruling because the prohibition of charity for Banī Hāshim is an honor from Allah for them and their descendants, since they supported the Prophet (peace be upon him) in their jahiliyyah and Islam. Abu Lahab was eager to harm the Prophet (peace be upon him), so his children do not deserve it. The author chose this in al-Mustasfa and narrated a hadith: 'There is no kinship between me and Abu Lahab.' And it is stated in al-Bada'i' that al-Karkhi restricted Banī Hāshim to the five from Banī Hāshim, so the madhhab is the restriction, because Imam al-Karkhi is among those most knowledgeable of the madhhab of our companions. He restricted to Banī Hāshim because Banī al-Muttalib are permissible for charity and are not like Banī Hāshim even if they are equal in kinship, because the grandfather of the Prophet is Muhammad ibn Abdullah ibn Abd al-Muttalib ibn Hashim ibn Abd Manaf. Abd Manaf had four sons: Hashim, al-Muttalib, Nawfal, and Abd Shams. The five mentioned are from Banī Hāshim: Abbas and Harith are the Prophet's uncles; Ja'far and Aqeel are brothers of Ali ibn Abi Talib, who is the Prophet's uncle. Abu Talib had four children; he had a son Talib who died without issue, and between him [؟] and Aqeel was ten years, between Aqeel and Ja'far ten years, between Ja'far and Ali ten years. Their mother was Fatima bint Asad ibn Hashim ibn Abd Manaf. Thus in Ghayat al-Bayan and Jamharat al-Nasab.
Source pages p. 430

Commentary
This passage from Bahr al-Raiq defines who exactly is considered Banī Hāshim for the purpose of the prohibition of receiving obligatory charity (zakat, kaffara, etc.). The prohibition is an honor for the Prophet's family. The author rules that only five named individuals constitute Banī Hāshim: Ali, Abbas, Ja'far, Aqeel, and Harith ibn Abd al-Muttalib. Abu Lahab and his descendants are excluded because of his enmity. The madhhab follows al-Karkhi's restriction. The genealogy is explained to show why Banī al-Muttalib are not included.

Key Terms
بني هاشم (Banī Hāshim): The five specified individuals (Ali, Abbas, Ja'far, Aqeel, Harith) and their descendants, for whom obligatory charity is prohibited.
الصدقة الواجبة (al-sadaqah al-wājibah): Obligatory charity such as zakat, nathr (vowed charity), 'ushr (tithe), and kaffara (expiation).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Bahr al-Raiq, which of the following is NOT considered a member of Banī Hāshim for the prohibition of receiving obligatory charity?
A) Abbas
B) Ja'far
C) Abu Lahab
D) Harith ibn Abd al-Muttalib

Key Takeaway
Obligatory charity is prohibited for the specified five descendants of Hashim (Ali, Abbas, Ja'far, Aqeel, Harith) and their progeny, excluding Abu Lahab's line.

Voluntary Charity and Waqf for Banī Hāshim: The Hanafi Ruling and Intra-Madhhab Dispute
Ruling on voluntary charity and waqf for Banī Hāshim

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on voluntary charity and waqf for Banī Hāshim and explain the intra-madhhab dispute.

Core Idea
Voluntary charity and waqf for Banī Hāshim are permissible; only obligatory charity is prohibited due to taint.

This lesson examines the distinction between obligatory and voluntary charity in the Hanafi school regarding Banī Hāshim. While obligatory charity (zakat, vows, etc.) is prohibited for them, voluntary charity and waqf are permitted. However, an intra-Hanafi dispute exists on whether voluntary charity is also prohibited. We will explore the evidence and the relied-upon position.

Guided Path
1
State the general Hanafi position
The general rule is that obligatory charity (like zakat) is prohibited for Banī Hāshim because it taints the recipient, while voluntary charity and waqf are permissible.
This is based on the analogy of used water.
2
Identify the intra-madhhab variant
Some Hanafi scholars (al-Zayla'i and the editor of Fath al-Qadir) held that even voluntary charity is prohibited, citing the absolute language of the hadith forbidding charity to Banī Hāshim.
They analogize voluntary charity to wudu upon wudu, which also taints.
3
Follow the proof of the opposing view
The opposing view relies on the general prohibition without distinguishing between obligatory and voluntary charity, and they use the analogy of wudu upon wudu.
They argue that any charity given to Banī Hāshim is tainted, regardless of obligation.
4
Analyse the Hanafi response
The majority response is that the prohibition is limited to obligatory charity because the giver purifies himself from duty, tainting the object. Voluntary charity has no such taint, as it is purely a gift. The analogy to wudu upon wudu is rejected because it relies on a specific text (hadith about light).
Additionally, there is consensus that voluntary charity for the rich is permissible, so it is for Banī Hāshim.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical consequence: a Hanafi mufti will allow voluntary charity and waqf for Banī Hāshim, while a minority would prohibit it.
The relied-upon position is permissibility, as transmitted from al-Nawadir and adopted by later scholars.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقال المصنف في الكافي وهذا في الواجبات كالزكاة والنذر والعشر والكفارة، أما التطوع والوقف فيجوز الصرف إليهم لأن المؤدي في الواجب يطهر نفسه بإسقاط الفرض فيتدنس المؤدى كالماء المستعمل، وفي النفل تبرع بما ليس عليه فلا يتدنس به المؤدى كمن تبرد بالماء اهـ. وإنما لم تلحق صدقة التطوع لهم بالوضوء على الوضوء فيتدنس به المؤدى لأن الأصل يقتضي عدمه وإنما قلتا به في الماء للنص الوارد «الوضوء على نور» إذ ازدياد النور يقتضي زوال الظلمة بقدره لا محالة. كذا في النهاية مختصراً. وفيها عن العتابي : إن النفل جائز لهم بالإجماع كالنفل للغني وتبعه صاحب المعراج واختاره في المحيط مقتصراً عليه وعزاه إلى النوادر ومشى عليه الأقطع في شرح القدوري، واختاره في غاية البيان ولم ينقل غيره شارح المجمع فكان هو المذهب. وأثبت الشارح الزيلعي الخلاف في التطوع على وجه يشعر بترجيح الحرمة، وقواه المحقق في فتح القدير من جهة الدليل لإطلاقه. وقد سوى المصنف في الكافي بين التطوع والوقف كما سمعت، وهكذا في المحيط. وفي شرح الطحاوى وغيره : إن الحل مقيد بما إذا سماهم، أما ذا لم يسمهم فلا ، لأنها صدقة واجبة. ورده المحقق في فتح القدير بأن صدقة الوقف كالنفل لأنه متبرع بتصدقه بالوقف إذ لا إيقاف واجب، وكان منشأ الغلط دفعها على الناظر وبذلك لم تصر صدقة واجبة على المالك، بل غاية الأمر أنه وجوب اتباع شرط الواقف على الناظر .اهـ. وفيه نظر إذ الإيقاف قد يكون واجباً كما إذا كان منذوراً كان وجوب
And the author said in al-Kafi: This applies to obligatory acts like zakat, vows, tithe, and expiation. As for voluntary charity and waqf, it is permissible to give to them because the payer of an obligatory act purifies himself by fulfilling the duty, so the object given becomes tainted, like used water. In voluntary charity, he gives what he is not obligated to, so the object is not tainted, like one who cools himself with water. And voluntary charity for them was not likened to wudu upon wudu, which taints the object, because the principle requires that it does not, and we only said that regarding water due to the text 'wudu upon light' as the increase of light necessarily removes darkness. Thus in al-Nihayah briefly. And in it from al-Attabi: Voluntary charity is unanimously permissible for them like for a rich person. The author of al-Mi'raj followed him, and al-Muhit chose it exclusively, attributing it to al-Nawadir. Al-Aqta' followed it in his commentary on al-Quduri, and it was chosen in Ghayat al-Bayan, and no other was transmitted by the commentator of al-Majma', so that is the madhhab. And al-Zayla'i affirmed the disagreement regarding voluntary charity in a way that suggests preference for prohibition, and the editor of Fath al-Qadir strengthened it from the perspective of evidence due to its absoluteness. And the author in al-Kafi equated voluntary charity and waqf, as you heard, and so in al-Muhit. And in Sharh al-Tahawi and others: Permissibility is conditional upon specifying them, otherwise not, because it would be obligatory charity. But the editor of Fath al-Qadir refuted this, saying that waqf charity is like voluntary charity because the waqf donor gives voluntarily, as there is no obligatory waqf. The source of the error is giving it to the overseer, thereby it did not become obligatory charity on the owner; rather at most it is a duty to follow the waqif's condition on the overseer. End. But this is questionable because waqf can be obligatory, such as when vowed, and it becomes obligatory.
Source pages p. 430

Commentary
This passage addresses the difference between obligatory and voluntary charity for Banī Hāshim. The author of al-Kafi rules that obligatory charity (zakat, vows, tithe, expiation) is prohibited for them because it taints the recipient, similar to used water. However, voluntary charity and waqf are permissible because the giver is not obliged to give, so no taint. Some scholars held that voluntary charity is also prohibited, but the relied-upon position in the madhhab is permissibility, based on the analogy that voluntary charity for a rich person is permissible by consensus. The passage discusses a refutation of the view that permissibility is conditional on naming the recipients.

Key Terms
التطوع (al-tatawwu'): Voluntary charity, which is not obligatory.
الوقف (al-waqf): Endowment, considered a charitable act that is like voluntary charity in this context.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the relied-upon Hanafi position, what is the ruling on giving voluntary charity (nafl) to Banī Hāshim?
A) Haram
B) Makruh
C) Mubah (permissible)
D) Wajib

Key Takeaway
Voluntary charity and waqf are permissible for Banī Hāshim by the relied-upon Hanafi position, while obligatory charity is prohibited due to taint.

Ruling on Paying Zakat with Taharrī and to Relatives
Ruling on zakat when given after taharrī and recipient turns out to be ineligible

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on zakat paid after taharrī when the recipient is later discovered to be a close relative or a slave, and apply the hadith proof to similar cases.

Core Idea
Paying zakat after taharrī to one's father or son is valid; paying to one's slave or mukatab is invalid.

When a Muslim pays zakat, he must ensure the recipient is eligible. Sometimes, despite careful effort (taharrī), the true status of the recipient is later revealed. This lesson explains how the Hanafi school rules on such cases, especially when the recipient turns out to be a close relative or one's own slave. The ruling is supported by a well-known hadith from Sahih al-Bukhari.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its condition
The act is paying zakat after performing taharrī (making a sincere effort to find a deserving recipient). The condition is that the payer did not know the recipient's ineligibility at the time of payment.
2
Distinguish between valid and invalid cases
According to the text, if it turns out the recipient was the payer's father or son, the payment is valid. But if it was his slave or mukatab, the payment is invalid.
3
Locate the proof (dalil)
The proof is the hadith in Sahih al-Bukhari: 'For you is what you intended, O Zayd, and for you is what you took, O Ma'n' — spoken when Zayd gave zakat to his son Ma'n, and it was later known that the payment was valid.
4
Extract the wajh al-dalalah
The hadith shows that the Prophet (peace be upon him) validated Zayd's zakat, establishing that paying zakat to one's own son (or father) after taharrī is valid.
5
Define taharrī
The text clarifies that taharrī here means a strong likelihood based on effort, not full ijtihad. It is sufficient when there is doubt about the recipient's status.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو دفع بتحر فبان أنه غني أو هاشمي أو كافر وأبوه أو ابنه صح ولو عبده أو مكاتبه لا لحديث البخاري لك ما نويت يا زيد ولك ما أخذت يا معن» حين دفعها زيد إلى ولده معن. وليس المراد بالتحري الاجتهاد بل غلبة الظن بأنه مصرف بعد الشك في كونه
And if he pays after taharrī (making effort) and it becomes apparent that he was rich, Hashimi, or a disbeliever, and (it turns out) the recipient was his father or son, it is valid; but if it was his slave or mukatab, it is not valid. Due to the hadith of Bukhari: 'For you is what you intended, O Zayd, and for you is what you took, O Ma'n' when Zayd paid it to his son Ma'n. And the meaning of taharrī is not ijtihad but overwhelming likelihood that he is a recipient after doubt about his status.
Source pages p. 431

Commentary
This passage presents a ruling from the matn of Kanz al-Daqaiq. It states that if a person pays zakat after making an effort to find a deserving recipient (taharrī), and then it becomes clear that the recipient was actually wealthy, Hashimi, or a non-Muslim, but if the recipient is the payer's father or son, the payment is valid. However, if it was his slave or mukatab (a slave with a contract of manumission), it is not valid. The proof is a hadith from Bukhari where Zayd gave zakat to his son Ma'n, and the Prophet said that each gets what they intended. The term taharrī here means a strong likelihood, not full ijtihad.

Key Terms
التحري (al-taharrī): Making a sincere effort to determine the rightful recipient of zakat when the person's status is uncertain.
مكاتبه (mukātibuhu): A slave who has made a contract of manumission (kitaba) with the master.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a person pays zakat after taharrī and it later becomes clear that the recipient was his slave, what is the ruling?
A) The zakat is valid
B) The zakat is invalid and must be given again
C) It is makruh but valid
D) It is valid if the slave was poor

Key Takeaway
After taharrī, paying zakat to a father or son is valid, but paying to a slave or mukatab is invalid.

Absolute Prohibition of Zakat for Banī Hāshim and Ruling for Their Mawla
Ruling with refutation and exception clarification

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on receiving zakat for Banī Hāshim and for a mawla of a Hashimi, and explain why the ruling is not conditional on the availability of khums.

Core Idea
Zakat is absolutely prohibited for Banī Hāshim; a mawla of a Hashimi may receive it because the preventing factor does not apply.

In this passage, the commentator clarifies that the prohibition of zakat for Banī Hāshim is absolute, not tied to any time or person. He rejects narrations that would allow it if khums does not reach them. He then distinguishes the mawla (client) of a Hashimi, who is not included in the prohibition and may receive zakat.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling and its scope
The ruling is that zakat is prohibited for Banī Hāshim absolutely – not restricted by time or person. This means the prohibition stands even if their compensation (khums) is not delivered.
2
Locate the opposing narration
Abu 'Isma reported a narration from Imam Abu Hanifa allowing zakat for Banī Hāshim if khums is neglected. The passage rejects this narration and affirms the absolute prohibition.
3
Understand the rejected analogy
The opposing view analogizes: if the compensation (khums) does not reach them, they revert to the original entitlement (zakat). The commentator rejects this because the clear textual prohibition overrides such qiyas.
4
Analyze the exception for mawla
A mawla (client) of a rich Hashimi may receive zakat because the rich Hashimi is eligible but prevented by wealth, and that prevention does not extend to the mawla.
5
Examine the hadith about mawla
The hadith 'mawla of a people is from themselves' is not general; it only applies to the permissibility and prohibition of charity, not to all legal rulings (e.g., lineage, jizya).
Al-Asbijabi's commentary clarifies the scope.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أطلق الحكم في بني هاشم ولم يقيد بزمان ولا بشخص لإشارة إلى رد رواية أبي عصمة عن الإمام أنه يجوز الدفع إلى بني هاشم في في زمانه لأن عوضها وهو خمس الخمس لم يصل إليهم لإهمال الناس أمر الغنائم وإيصالها إلى مستحقها، وإذا لم يصل إليهم العوض عادوا إلى المعوض، وللإشارة إلى رد الرواية بأن الهاشمي يجوز له أن يدفع زكاته إلى هاشمي مثله لأن ظاهر الرواية المنع مطلقاً. وقيد بمولى الهاشمي لأن مولى الغني يجوز الدفع إليه لأن الغني أهل لها لكن الغني مانع ولا مانع في حق المولى. والحديث ليس على عمومه أعني مولى القوم من أنفسهم ولهذا قال الأسبيجابي في تفسيره؛ يعني في حل الصدقة وحرمتها وإلا فمولى القوم ليس منهم من جميع الوجوه، أنه ليس بكفؤ لهم وأن مولى المسلم إذا كان كافراً تؤخذ منه الجزية، وإن كان مولى التغلبي تؤخذ منه الجزية لا المضاعفة .اهـ. وفي آخر مبسوط الإمام السرخسي من كتاب الكسب وتكلم الناس في حق سائر الأنبياء عليهم الصلاة والسلام، أتجل لهم الصدقة أم لا؟ فمنهم من يقول ما كان يحل أخذ الصدقة لسائر الأنبياء عليهم أيضاً ولكن كانت تحل لقراباتهم، ثم إن الله تعالى أكرم نبينا بأن حرم الصدقة على قرابته إظهاراً لفضيلته، وقيل بل كانت الصدقة تحل لسائر الأنبياء وهذه خصوصية لنبينا عليه أفضل الصلاة والسلام.
He made the ruling absolute for Banī Hāshim, not restricted by time or person, to indicate rejection of the narration of Abu 'Isma from the Imam that it is permissible to give to Banī Hāshim in his time because their compensation, which is the fifth of the fifth, did not reach them due to people neglecting the matter of war booty and delivering it to its deserving recipients. And if the compensation does not reach them, they return to the original. And to indicate rejection of the narration that a Hashimi may pay his zakat to another Hashimi, because the apparent ruling is prohibition absolutely. And he restricted it regarding the mawla of a Hashimi because it is permissible to pay to a mawla of a rich person, because the rich person is eligible but prevented by wealth, and there is no prevention for the mawla. And the hadith is not general, meaning 'the mawla of a people is from themselves', and thus al-Asbijabi said in his commentary: it means in the permissibility and prohibition of charity, otherwise a mawla is not from them in all respects, he is not their equal, and if a mawla of a Muslim is a disbeliever, jizya is taken from him, and if he is a mawla of a Taghlibi, jizya is taken but not double. End. And in the end of al-Mabsut of Imam al-Sarakhsi from the book on earnings: People discussed regarding all prophets, peace be upon them, whether charity was permissible for them or not. Some say that taking charity was not permissible for previous prophets either, but it was permissible for their relatives. Then Allah honored our Prophet by prohibiting charity on his relatives to show his virtue. And it was said that charity was permissible for previous prophets, and it is a special characteristic of our Prophet, peace be upon him.
Source pages p. 431

Commentary
This passage addresses two points. First, the ruling on Banī Hāshim is absolute and not restricted to a particular time, rejecting a narration that would allow it in the absence of khums. It also discusses the mawla (client) of a Hashimi, stating that the mawla of a rich person may receive zakat because the rich person is eligible but prevented, while the mawla is not. The hadith 'the mawla of a people is from themselves' is not general here. Second, it discusses whether charity was permissible for previous prophets. There are two views: one says it was not permissible for them either, but only for their relatives; the other says it was permissible for them, and the prohibition for the Prophet's relatives is a special honor.

Key Terms
مولى الهاشمي (mawlā al-hāshimī): A non-Arab client (freed slave) of a Hashimi; such a mawla is allowed to receive zakat because the owner is eligible but prevented by wealth.
خمس الخمس (khums al-khums): The portion of war booty that belongs to the Prophet's family (the fifth of the fifth).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to this passage, can a mawla (client) of a rich Hashimi receive zakat?
A) He is prohibited like the Hashimi
B) He is allowed to receive zakat
C) He is only allowed if he is poor
D) He is allowed only if the Hashimi is poor

Key Takeaway
The prohibition of zakat for Banī Hāshim is absolute; a mawla of a Hashimi is not included in this prohibition.

Dispute Over Charity for Previous Prophets
Inter-madhhab dispute on a historical fiqh issue

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the two positions on whether charity was permissible for previous prophets and explain the Hanafi preferred view.

Core Idea
Whether charity was permissible for previous prophets is disputed; the Hanafi relied-upon view holds it was not, making the prohibition for our Prophet's relatives a unique honor.

This passage presents a dispute over the permissibility of charity for previous prophets. Some scholars held that it was not permissible for them, only for their relatives, while others said it was permissible. The text suggests the first view is preferred, emphasizing the special honor of our Prophet.

Guided Path
1
Identify the issue
The issue is whether taking charity (sadaqah) was permissible for prophets before Prophet Muhammad (peace be upon him).
2
State the first view
Some scholars say charity was not permissible for previous prophets themselves, but it was permissible for their relatives.
3
State the second view
Other scholars say charity was permissible for previous prophets; the prohibition for our Prophet's family is a unique honor.
4
Determine the practical consequence
The difference lies in the uniqueness of the prohibition for the Prophet's family. If the first view is correct, the prohibition is a special virtue; if the second, it is not unique.
5
Identify the preferred view
According to the commentator and the hadith 'haram alaykum awsakh al-nas' (forbidden to you is the filth of people), the first view is preferred: charity was not permissible for previous prophets.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أطلق الحكم في بني هاشم ولم يقيد بزمان ولا بشخص لإشارة إلى رد رواية أبي عصمة عن الإمام أنه يجوز الدفع إلى بني هاشم في في زمانه لأن عوضها وهو خمس الخمس لم يصل إليهم لإهمال الناس أمر الغنائم وإيصالها إلى مستحقها، وإذا لم يصل إليهم العوض عادوا إلى المعوض، وللإشارة إلى رد الرواية بأن الهاشمي يجوز له أن يدفع زكاته إلى هاشمي مثله لأن ظاهر الرواية المنع مطلقاً. وقيد بمولى الهاشمي لأن مولى الغني يجوز الدفع إليه لأن الغني أهل لها لكن الغني مانع ولا مانع في حق المولى. والحديث ليس على عمومه أعني مولى القوم من أنفسهم ولهذا قال الأسبيجابي في تفسيره؛ يعني في حل الصدقة وحرمتها وإلا فمولى القوم ليس منهم من جميع الوجوه، أنه ليس بكفؤ لهم وأن مولى المسلم إذا كان كافراً تؤخذ منه الجزية، وإن كان مولى التغلبي تؤخذ منه الجزية لا المضاعفة .اهـ. وفي آخر مبسوط الإمام السرخسي من كتاب الكسب وتكلم الناس في حق سائر الأنبياء عليهم الصلاة والسلام، أتجل لهم الصدقة أم لا؟ فمنهم من يقول ما كان يحل أخذ الصدقة لسائر الأنبياء عليهم أيضاً ولكن كانت تحل لقراباتهم، ثم إن الله تعالى أكرم نبينا بأن حرم الصدقة على قرابته إظهاراً لفضيلته، وقيل بل كانت الصدقة تحل لسائر الأنبياء وهذه خصوصية لنبينا عليه أفضل الصلاة والسلام.
He made the ruling absolute for Banī Hāshim, not restricted by time or person, to indicate rejection of the narration of Abu 'Isma from the Imam that it is permissible to give to Banī Hāshim in his time because their compensation, which is the fifth of the fifth, did not reach them due to people neglecting the matter of war booty and delivering it to its deserving recipients. And if the compensation does not reach them, they return to the original. And to indicate rejection of the narration that a Hashimi may pay his zakat to another Hashimi, because the apparent ruling is prohibition absolutely. And he restricted it regarding the mawla of a Hashimi because it is permissible to pay to a mawla of a rich person, because the rich person is eligible but prevented by wealth, and there is no prevention for the mawla. And the hadith is not general, meaning 'the mawla of a people is from themselves', and thus al-Asbijabi said in his commentary: it means in the permissibility and prohibition of charity, otherwise a mawla is not from them in all respects, he is not their equal, and if a mawla of a Muslim is a disbeliever, jizya is taken from him, and if he is a mawla of a Taghlibi, jizya is taken but not double. End. And in the end of al-Mabsut of Imam al-Sarakhsi from the book on earnings: People discussed regarding all prophets, peace be upon them, whether charity was permissible for them or not. Some say that taking charity was not permissible for previous prophets either, but it was permissible for their relatives. Then Allah honored our Prophet by prohibiting charity on his relatives to show his virtue. And it was said that charity was permissible for previous prophets, and it is a special characteristic of our Prophet, peace be upon him.
Source pages p. 431

Commentary
This passage addresses two points. First, the ruling on Banī Hāshim is absolute and not restricted to a particular time, rejecting a narration that would allow it in the absence of khums. It also discusses the mawla (client) of a Hashimi, stating that the mawla of a rich person may receive zakat because the rich person is eligible but prevented, while the mawla is not. The hadith 'the mawla of a people is from themselves' is not general here. Second, it discusses whether charity was permissible for previous prophets. There are two views: one says it was not permissible for them either, but only for their relatives; the other says it was permissible for them, and the prohibition for the Prophet's relatives is a special honor.

Key Terms
أوساخ الناس (awsākh al-nās): The filth of people – a metaphor for charity, implying it is unsuitable for the pure lineage of prophets.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to this passage, which view is preferred regarding charity for previous prophets?
A) Charity was permissible for them
B) Charity was not permissible for them
C) Charity was permissible for their relatives but not for them
D) Both views are equally valid

Key Takeaway
The preferred Hanafi view is that charity was not permissible for previous prophets; the prohibition for our Prophet's relatives is a unique honor.

Zakat — Almsgiving
intermediate

Paying Zakat Based on Ijtihad and Subsequent Discovery
Commentary on the ruling of ijtihad in zakat disbursement

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on Zakat paid with ijtihad when the recipient is later found ineligible, explain the proof from the hadith of Zayd, and apply the conditions for validity.

Core Idea
Ijtihad after doubt makes Zakat valid even if recipient is later found ineligible.

This lesson examines a crucial Hanafi principle: when a person pays Zakat after making an honest effort (taharrī) to find a valid recipient, and later discovers the recipient was actually wealthy, a Hashimi, or a kafir, the Zakat is still valid. The proof comes from the hadith of Zayd who gave Zakat to his son, and the Prophet said 'You have what you intended.' We will unpack the conditions, the proof, and the internal Hanafi disagreement about paying to a harbi.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling and its key elements
Read the passage to determine when Zakat with ijtihad is valid and when it is not. Note the condition of 'preponderant belief after doubt' and the exceptions (slave, mukatab).
2
Analyze the proof (dalil)
Examine the hadith of Zayd and Ma'n from Sahih al-Bukhari. Understand how the Prophet's statement validates the payment based on intention (niyyah) despite the recipient being a son (who is not normally a valid recipient).
3
Distinguish between valid and invalid ijtihad
The passage explains that if the payer had a preponderant belief (ghalabat al-zann) after doubt, the Zakat is valid. If he paid without any ijtihad or without a preponderant belief, it is invalid if the recipient is found ineligible.
4
Examine the internal Hanafi dispute
Note the difference of opinion regarding payment to a harbi (enemy combatant): the original narration (zahir al-riwayah) says it is valid, but Abu Yusuf and a narration from Abu Hanifah say it is not. Identify the authority weight and mufta bihi.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

قوله : (وفيه نظر الخ) قال الرملي : قد يقال وجوبه بالنذر العارض لا يعارض ا هـ. وكذا أجاب بعضهم بأن مراده لا إيجاب واجب بإيجاب الله تعالى .ا.هـ. وبالجملة فما ذكره المؤلف لا يدفع بحث المحقق إذ يبعد حمل كلامهم على الوقف المنذور قوله : وقيل بل كانت الصدقة تحل الخ) قال في النهر : والذي ينبغي اعتماده الأول لقوله في الحديث وحرم عليكم أوساخ الناس ولا شك أن الأنبياء عليهم الصلاة والسلام منزهون عن ذلك اهـ . وفي حواشي مسكين عن الحموي عن ابن بطال : اتفق الفقهاء على أن أزواجه عليه الصلاة والسلام لا يدخلن في الذين حرمت عليهم الصدقة قال : ثم قال الحموي : وفي المغني عن عائشة رضي الله تعالى عنها قالت: إنا آل محمد لا تحل لنا
الصدقة . قال : فهذا يدل على تحريمها عليهن قوله : (باجتهاد بدون ظن أي بأن اجتهد ولم يترجح عنده شيء وقوله «أو بغير اجتهاد أصلاً أي بعد الشك بدليل قوله الآتي «لأنه لو دفعها ولم يخطر بباله الخ». وقوله «أو بظن أنه بعد الشك ليس بمصرف الظاهر أن قوله «بعد الشك» من تصرف النساخ إذ لا موقع لذكره هنا، ومحله أن يذكر عقب قوله «أصلا» فتصير العبارة هكذا: أو بغير اجتهاد أصلاً بعد الشك أو بظن أنه ليس بمصرف الخ. قوله : ( ودفع المال إلى غير الفقير قربة الخ) قال في النهر كون الإعطاء لا يكون به عاصياً مطلقاً فقد ممنوع ، صرح الأسبيجابي بأنه إذا غلب على ظنه غناه حرم عليه الدفع ا.هـ. وفيه أنه لا يخلو إما أن يراد بالغني في كلام الأسبيجابي ما هو المتبادر منه وهو أن يكون مالك نصاب أولا بأن كان يملك قوت يومه فقط، فإن كان الأول فالدافع إليه تكون هبة وهي جائزة، وإن كان الثاني كما حمله عليه في النهر آخر الباب فلا يتوجه المنع به لأنه مصرف والكلام فيمن ظنه غير مصرف فالدفع إليه يكون هبة كما يأتي آخر الباب، وهي مندوبة وقبولها سنة على أن كلام الأسبيجابي الظاهر منه أن المراد به دفع الزكاة وأن المراد بالغني المعتبر، ووجه الحرمة حينئذ عدم سقوط الزكاة عنه بهذا الدفع فإذا اجتزأ به يكون مانعاً للزكاة، والمراد بقولهم في الفرق ودفع المال إلى غير الفقير قربة غير الزكاة كما لا يخفى فأنى يتوجه المنع قوله : (وأطلق الكافر الخ ) قال في كفاية البيهقي : دفع إلى حربي خطأ ثم تبين جاز على رواية الأصل، وروى أبو يوسف عن أبي حنيفة أنه لا يجوز وهو قوله ا هـ . قال الأقطع وقال أبو يوسف : لا يجوز
Chapter of Zakat: Section on Disbursement. He said: If my father makes a vow that I should stop this house... The verifier himself explained in the book of Waqf that and raised a question: How does the vow become binding when it is not of the same type as an obligatory act? And he answered: It is obligatory upon the Imam to establish a mosque from the public treasury for Muslims, and if there is nothing in the treasury, then upon the Muslims. In Fatawa al-Zahiriyyah from the chapter on Zakat, section on vows: A man lost something and said, 'If I find it, I obligate myself to stop this land for the wayfarers.' He found it, so he must fulfill it. Then if he stops his land for those whom it is permissible... Is it not that he may give Zakat to them from relatives and strangers? Permissible. End. And he made the ruling absolute regarding Banu Hashim without restricting by time or person, to indicate the rejection of the narration of Abu 'Ismah from al-Imam that it is permissible to give to Banu Hashim in his time because their compensation, which is one-fifth of the khums, did not reach them due to people's negligence concerning spoils and delivering them to their entitled recipients. And if the compensation does not reach them, they revert to the compensated. And to indicate the rejection of the narration that the Hashimi may give his Zakat to a fellow Hashimi because the apparent narration forbids absolutely. And he restricted it to the mawla of the Hashimi because giving to the mawla of the wealthy is permissible because the wealthy is eligible for it but wealth is a preventer, and there is no preventer regarding the mawla. And the hadith is not general—meaning the mawla of a people are among them—hence al-Asbajabi said in his commentary: meaning regarding the permissibility and prohibition of charity; otherwise, the mawla of a people are not among them in all respects, such that he is not a peer to them, and if the mawla of a Muslim is a kafir, jizyah is taken from him, and if he is the mawla of a Taghlibi, jizyah is taken from him but not double. End. And at the end of al-Sarakhsi's Mabsut from the chapter on Earning, and people spoke about the status of other prophets (peace be upon them): Is charity permissible for them or not? Some say that taking charity was not permissible for other prophets either, but it was permissible for their relatives. Then Allah honored our Prophet by prohibiting charity upon his relatives as a manifestation of his excellence. It was also said that charity was permissible for other prophets and this is a specificity of our Prophet (peace be upon him). His statement: (And if he pays based on ijtihad and it becomes clear that he is wealthy, or a Hashimi, or a kafir, and his father or his son, it is valid; and if his slave or his mukatab, not) due to the hadith of Bukhari: 'You have what you intended, O Zayd, and you have what you took, O Ma'n,' when Zayd gave it to his son Ma'n. And by ijtihad is meant not mere exertion but predominance of opinion that he is a valid recipient after doubt about him being so. His statement: (There is a consideration in it, etc.) al-Ramli said: It may be said that its obligation by an incidental vow is not contradictory. End. Similarly, some answered that his intent is that no obligation by Allah's command is opposed. End. In summary, what the author mentioned does not refute the verifier's investigation, for it is unlikely to interpret their words as referring to a vowed waqf. His statement: (It was said that charity was permissible, etc.) In al-Nahr: What should be relied upon is the first, due to the hadith: 'And the filth of people is forbidden for you,' and there is no doubt that prophets are pure from that. End. In the glosses of Miskin from al-Hamawi from Ibn Battal: The jurists agreed that his wives (peace be upon him) are not included among those upon whom charity is prohibited. He said: Then al-Hamawi said: In al-Mughni from 'A'ishah (may Allah be pleased with her): 'We, the family of Muhammad, charity is not permissible for us.' He said: This indicates its prohibition upon them. His statement: (With ijtihad without preponderance) meaning he exerted effort but nothing became preponderant with him. And his statement: 'Or without any ijtihad at all' meaning after doubt, as evidenced by his later statement: 'Because if he paid it and it did not cross his mind, etc.' And his statement: 'Or with the presumption that after doubt he is not a valid recipient' — it seems that the phrase 'after doubt' is an insertion by the copyists, as it has no place here; its place is after the word 'at all', so the expression becomes: 'Or without any ijtihad at all after doubt, or with the presumption that he is not a valid recipient, etc.' His statement: (And paying money to a non-poor person is a charitable act, etc.) In al-Nahr: The claim that the giver is not sinful at all is disputed; al-Asbajabi explicitly stated that if he predominantly thinks the recipient is wealthy, it is forbidden for him to give. End. There is a discussion: Either by 'wealthy' in al-Asbajabi's statement is meant the obvious meaning—that he owns a nisab—or not, such that he only possesses his daily sustenance. If the first, then giving to him is a gift and permissible; if the second, as interpreted in al-Nahr at the end of the chapter, then the prohibition is not applicable because he is a valid recipient, and the discussion concerns one whom he presumes not to be a valid recipient, so giving to him is a gift as will come at the end of the chapter, and it is recommended, and accepting it is sunnah. However, al-Asbajabi's apparent statement is that he means paying Zakat and that the relevant meaning of 'wealthy' is the considered one, and the reason for prohibition here is that Zakat is not discharged by this payment; if he relies on it, he becomes a preventer of Zakat. And their statement in the distinction 'Paying money to a non-poor person is a charitable act' refers to non-Zakat charity, as is not hidden. So how can prohibition be directed? His statement: (And he made the kafir absolute, etc.) In Kifayat al-Bayhaqi: He paid to a Harbi by mistake, then it became clear; it is valid according to the original narration, and Abu Yusuf reported from Abu Hanifah that it is not valid, and that is his (Abu Hanifah's) statement. End. Al-Aqta' said: Abu Yusuf said: It is not valid.
Source pages p. 431

Commentary
This is an expansion (sharh) on the ruling from Kanz al-Daqa'iq regarding paying Zakat based on ijtihad (taharrī) where the recipient later turns out to be ineligible (wealthy, Hashimi, or kafir). The commentator explains that the ruling is divided: if the payer exerted ijtihad (genuine effort to determine eligibility) and later discovers the recipient was actually ineligible, the Zakat is still valid. This is based on the hadith recorded by Bukhari: when Zayd gave Zakat to his son Ma'n, the Prophet said, 'You have what you intended, O Zayd, and you have what you took, O Ma'n.' This indicates that the intention behind the payment suffices, even if the recipient turns out not to be a valid recipient. However, the condition is that the ijtihad must produce a preponderant belief (ghalabat al-zann) that the recipient is eligible, after initially being in doubt. If one pays without any ijtihad or without any preponderant belief, then the Zakat is not valid if the recipient is found to be ineligible. The commentator then draws a key distinction between Zakat and prayer: if one prays with ijtihad towards a direction he believes is not the qibla, the prayer is invalid even if it later turns out to be correct, because intentionally facing a non-qibla direction is an act of disobedience which cannot transform into obedience. In contrast, paying money to a non-poor person is itself a charitable act (qurbah) that can be rewarded, so the mistaken payment is still valid based on the intention. Further, the passage discusses the case where the payer did not think about the recipient's status at all (no ijtihad, no preponderance); the default is that the payment is valid unless it becomes clear the recipient was ineligible, because the default presumption is that the Zakat was given to its proper place. Once the payer has given it, he cannot reclaim the money even if the recipient turns out to be ineligible; the payment becomes a voluntary charity (tatowwu'). There is a discussion on whether the recipient can lawfully keep the money; some say he should give it in charity due to its taint (khubth), others say he should return it. Finally, the passage addresses the case of paying to a kafir: the ruling is absolute, covering dhimmis and harbis. However, there is a dispute: according to the original narration (zahir al-riwayah), if one mistakenly pays to a harbi and it later becomes clear, the Zakat is valid. But Abu Yusuf reported from Abu Hanifah that it is not valid, and this is also Abu Yusuf's own view. The commentator favors the first view but notes the disagreement.

Key Terms
التحري (al-taharrī): Ijtihad to determine the correct recipient of Zakat after doubt, resulting in a preponderant belief (ghalabat al-zann).
مصرف (masrif): The category of people eligible to receive Zakat, as defined by the Quran (the eight asnaf).
الغني (al-ghaniyy): One who owns a nisab (minimum amount of wealth subject to Zakat) or has sufficient wealth, making him ineligible to receive Zakat.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
When is Zakat invalid due to subsequent discovery that the recipient was ineligible?
A) When the payer had a preponderant belief of eligibility after doubt.
B) When the payer paid without any ijtihad or preponderant belief.
C) When the payer's father or son receives the Zakat.
D) When the recipient is a Hashimi.

Key Takeaway
Zakat paid with genuine ijtihad (taharrī) after doubt is valid even if the recipient is later found ineligible, based on the hadith 'You have what you intended'.

Zakat — Almsgiving
intermediate
10 min
Giving Zakat with Mistaken Belief About Recipient's Eligibility
Ruling_with_proof on mistaken belief in zakat disbursement

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on giving zakat with mistaken belief about the recipient's eligibility, and explain the distinction from the case of prayer.

Core Idea
Mistaken presumption of ineligibility does not invalidate zakat if the recipient turns out eligible — valid qurba cannot become invalidated by error.

When a person gives zakat, they must ensure it reaches an eligible recipient. But what if they later discover their assumption was wrong? This passage from al-Bahr al-Raiq clarifies the ruling based on whether the mistake was about the recipient's eligibility at the time of giving. It also compares the case with praying towards a mistaken qibla, revealing a key legal principle: acts of worship differ in how errors affect their validity.

Guided Path
1
Identify the key scenarios
Read the text carefully for the three cases: (1) paying with ijtihad but without presumption, (2) without ijtihad at all, (3) with a presumption that the recipient is not eligible. Note the outcome for each.
Focus on the ruling when the recipient later turns out to be eligible vs. ineligible.
2
Distinguish between the two outcomes
Understand why paying to a presumed ineligible who turns out eligible is valid, while paying to a presumed eligible who turns out ineligible is invalid. The key is that giving to a non-eligible person is still a charitable act (qurba), whereas praying to the wrong qibla is disobedience.
The text provides the rationale: 'الفرق' (distinction).
3
Analyze the analogy with prayer
Compare the zakat case with the prayer case: one prays with ijtihad towards a direction they think is not qibla, but it turns out to be qibla. The prayer is invalid. Why? Because praying to a non-qibla is sinful and sin cannot transform into obedience.
Apply the principle: valid qurba vs. disobedience.
4
Apply to the case of a harbi
The text extends the ruling to non-Muslim recipients. There is a dispute within the Hanafi school about giving to a harbi (enemy non-Muslim). Identify the two narrations from Abu Hanifa and the later preferred view.
The relied-upon position is impermissibility, citing consensus.
5
Evaluate the intra-madhhab dispute
Consider the practical consequence (thamarah): if a Hanafi gives zakat to a harbi mistakenly, the mufta bihi view says it is invalid and must be repeated. The opposing view says it is valid.
Understand the authority of consensus and why the later Hanafis adopted the prohibition.

The chapter of the disbursement. It becomes clear that between this is wasteful. We say this because if he pays with ijtihad without presumption, or without ijtihad at all, or with the presumption that after doubt it is not a valid recipient, then it turns out the obstacle exists, it does not suffice. Likewise, if nothing becomes clear, it is invalid until it becomes a valid disbursement. And if he pays to someone he presumes is not a valid recipient, then it turns out he is a valid recipient, it suffices. The difference between this and one who prays with ijtihad towards a direction he presumes is not the qibla, where the prayer does not suffice even if it turns out to be the qibla, rather the Imam said he fears disbelief for him, is that obligatory prayer in other than the qibla is disobedience, and disobedience does not turn into obedience, while giving money to other than the poor is a charitable act for which he is rewarded. We restricted it to being after doubt because if he pays them and it does not occur to his mind whether it is a valid disbursement or not, then it is permissible unless it becomes clear that it is not a valid disbursement, because the apparent assumption is that he gave the charity to its proper place since he intended zakat at the time of payment, and the apparent assumption is not invalidated except by certainty, so that even if he later doubts it and nothing appears to him, he is not required to repeat it because the first apparent assumption is not invalidated by doubt. He may not reclaim what he paid if it becomes clear that it is not a valid disbursement and it occurred as a voluntary gift. Thus in al-Bada'i. The scholars differed on whether it is permissible for the poor recipient to enjoy it. According to the view that it is not permissible, some said he should give it in charity because of its impurity, and some said he should return it to the giver. Thus in Mi'raj al-Diraya. He included the disbeliever absolutely, so it includes the dhimmi and the harbi. Both were clearly mentioned in al-Mughtabi. In al-Muhit: If it appears that he is a harbi, there are two narrations, and the distinction in one of them is that the quality of being a charitable act was not found at all. The correct view is prohibition, for he said in Ghayat al-Bayan, citing al-Tuhfah: They agreed that if it appears that he is a harbi, even if a musta'min, it is not permissible. Likewise in Mi'raj al-Diraya: Sadaqah. He said: This indicates its prohibition for them. His saying: (With ijtihad without presumption) meaning that he exercised ijtihad and nothing was preponderant for him. His saying: (Or without ijtihad at all) meaning after doubt, as indicated by his following words: 'Because if he paid and it did not occur to his mind...' His saying: (Or with the presumption that after doubt it is not a valid recipient) the apparent meaning is that the words 'after doubt' are from the copyists' interference, for there is no place for it here. Its proper place is after 'at all', so the expression becomes: Or without ijtihad at all after doubt, or with the presumption that it is not a valid recipient... His saying: (And giving money to other than the poor is a charitable act...) He said in al-Nahr: The claim that the giving is not sinful at all is denied. al-Asbijabi stated: If he is predominantly of the opinion that he is rich, it is prohibited for him to pay. End quote. There is in it that it is not empty: either by 'rich' in al-Asbijabi's words is meant what is commonly understood, that he possesses a nisab, or that he possesses only a day's food. If the first, then the giver gives a gift, which is permissible. If the second, as al-Nahr interpreted at the end of the chapter, then the prohibition does not apply because he is a recipient. The discussion is about one whom he presumes is not a recipient, so the payment is a gift, as will come at the end of the chapter, and it is recommended, and its acceptance is sunnah. However, al-Asbijabi's apparent words mean that the intended payment is zakat and that the intended 'rich' is the one considered for zakat. The reason for prohibition then is that the zakat obligation does not fall by this payment, so if he contents himself with it, he is preventing the zakat. The meaning of their statement in the distinction: 'Giving money to other than the poor is a charitable act' refers to other than zakat, as is not hidden. So how can the prohibition be directed? His saying: (And he absolutely included the disbeliever...) He said in Kifayat al-Bayhaqi: He paid to a harbi mistakenly, then it became clear. It is permissible according to the original narration. Abu Yusuf narrated from Abu Hanifa that it is not permissible, which is his opinion. End quote. al-Aqta' said: And Abu Yusuf said: it is not permissible.
Source pages p. 432

Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Raiq is a commentary on Kanz al-Daqa'iq, discussing the rulings on giving zakat when the giver makes a mistake about the recipient's eligibility. The passage type is a ruling_with_proof, as it provides rational distinctions and fiqh comparisons. It opens by stating that if a person gives zakat after exercising ijtihad (effort to determine eligibility) but without a clear presumption, or without any ijtihad at all, or with a presumption that the recipient is NOT eligible (i.e., not poor) while still doubting, and then it turns out the recipient was indeed ineligible, the zakat does not count and must be repeated. The same applies if nothing becomes clear. However, if the person gives to someone he presumes is NOT eligible, and then it turns out the recipient WAS eligible, the zakat IS valid. The author distinguishes this from the case of prayer: if someone prays towards a direction he believes is NOT the qibla, the prayer is invalid even if it later turns out it was the qibla, because intentionally praying to a wrong qibla is disobedience and cannot become obedience. But giving money to a non-poor person is still a charitable act (qurba) that earns reward, so the mistake does not invalidate the act entirely. The author then restricts the discussion to cases after doubt (shakk). If the giver pays without thinking about eligibility at all, the default is validity unless ineligibility becomes certain. The recipient cannot be forced to return the money if it turns out he was ineligible; it becomes a voluntary gift. The author also discusses the difference of opinion among scholars on whether the recipient may benefit from such a payment if it turns out invalid; some say he must give it in charity, others return it. The passage then addresses giving to non-Muslims. The word 'kaafir' in the ruling includes both dhimmi (protected non-Muslim) and harbi (enemy non-Muslim). There are two narrations regarding giving to a harbi: one allows it, one prohibits. The correct view in the Hanafi school is prohibition, and there is claimed consensus that even a musta'min (non-Muslim with safe conduct) is not a valid recipient. The author then provides clarifications on the wording of the original text and reconciles different statements.

Key Terms
مصرف (masrif): The valid recipient of zakat, i.e., the categories mentioned in Quran (poor, needy, etc.)
اجتهاد (ijtihad): The giver's effort to determine whether a recipient is eligible for zakat
حبري (harbi): A non-Muslim who is in a state of war with Muslims and not under Muslim protection

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a person gives zakat to someone he presumes is NOT a valid recipient (e.g., not poor), but it later becomes clear that the recipient was actually poor, what is the ruling?
A) The zakat does not count and must be repeated.
B) The zakat counts and is valid.
C) The zakat counts only if the giver had no doubt.
D) The zakat is invalid because the giver intended charity, not zakat.

Key Takeaway
Giving zakat to a person you mistakenly think is ineligible becomes valid if they are actually eligible, because the act remains a charitable deed.

Zakat — Almsgiving

The Distinction Between Haqq Allah and Haqq al-'Ibad: Zakat vs. Will and Its Analogical Extensions
Analogical extension of haqq distinction to executor's liability.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the distinction between haqq Allah and haqq al-'ibad, apply it to cases of mistaken payment in zakat and will, and evaluate the qiyas of the executor buying waqf property.

Core Idea
Haqq Allah favors intention; haqq al-'ibad demands exact fulfillment — a fundamental distinction in Hanafi fiqh.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Raiq explores a key distinction in Hanafi law: rights of Allah (huquq Allah) versus rights of individuals (huquq al-'ibad). The author uses the difference in treatment of mistaken payments in zakat and will to illustrate, then extends the reasoning via qiyas to a contemporary problem involving an executor buying waqf property. Understanding this distinction is essential for applying the correct rulings in cases of human error.

Guided Path
1
Identify the key distinction
The passage begins by contrasting zakat (haqq Allah) with will (haqq al-'ibad). In haqq Allah, intention (wus') is considered; in haqq al-'ibad, the actual reality must be fulfilled. Identify the authorities: the author cites Mi'raj al-Diraya and consensus on the will case.
Note the phrase: 'لأن الزكاة حق الله تعالى فاعتبر فيها الوسع والوصية حق العباد فاعتبر فيها الحقيقة'.
2
Extract the original ruling and the qiyas
The original ruling: if an executor gives will to supposed poor who turn out rich, he is liable by consensus. The qiyas: if an executor buys a house intending to make waqf, pays the price, and it turns out to be another's waqf, he is liable by analogy. Identify the asl, far', and illa in the qiyas panel.
The qiyas is based on the idea that the executor did not achieve the commanded act.
3
Analyze the objection to the qiyas
Al-Ramli objects that in the far', the executor did not violate his command (he was told to buy a house), unlike the asl. The passage supports this with a narration from Nawadir Hisham regarding buying a slave for freeing. Understand the distinction: istihqaq (claim) vs. being free; buying a waqf is like istihqaq, so no liability.
The distinction is that waqf property can sometimes be sold, so the purchase is not a clear violation.
4
Connect to other analogous cases
The passage brings in the cases of mixed pure/impure vessels and clothes, and a judge's ijtihad overruled by a clear text. In each, the person failed to perform the commanded act because knowledge was possible. Note the refinement: for vessels, necessity may not apply, leading to different rulings between vessels and clothes.
This shows the principle at work across different fiqh chapters.
5
Identify the practical outcome (thamarah al-khilaf)
The practical consequence: if the qiyas is applied, the executor must personally compensate; if the distinction is accepted, he is not liable. The text seems to prefer the latter view. Also note the rulings on selling waqf property under various conditions, which provide context for why buying waqf is not a clear violation.
The mufta bihi for istibdal is Abu Yusuf's opinion.

أما الثياب النجسة إذا اختلطت بالطاهرة فإنه يتحرى مطلقاً ولو كانت النجسة أكثر أم مساوية، وتبعه في فتح القدير، وقد أخذاه من مبسوط السرخسي من كتاب التحري وفرق بينهما بأن الضرورة لا تتحقق في الأواني لأن وهو أحد قولي الشافعي، وقوله الآخر مثل قول أبي حنيفة. قال في مشكلات خواهر زاده: قوله «ثم ظهر أنه غني . أو هاشمي أو كافر أي ذمي لأن الإجماع منعقد أنه لو كان مستأمناً أو خربياً فإنه تجب الإعادة ا هـ. ونص فلا ضمان على الوصي، وإن ظهر أنها حرة فالوصي ضامن اهـ. وأيضاً دار الوقف تقبل البيع في الجملة حتى فرقوا ضم الحر إلى العبد وبين ضم الوقف إلى الملك فسرى البطلان في الأول دون الثاني. قال الشارح في الفاسد It is restricted to zakat because if he bequeaths one-third of his wealth to the poor, and the executor gives them, then it becomes clear they are rich, it is not valid and the executor is liable by consensus, because zakat is a right of Allah so intention (wus') is considered, while will is a right of people so reality is considered. Do you not see that if a sleeping person destroys something, he is liable but does not sin. Thus in Mi'raj al-Diraya. And by analogy, the executor who buys a house to endow it, and pays the price, then it appears it is a waqf of someone else and the price is lost, then the executor is liable. This is an occurrence in our time. And because if pure vessels mix with impure ones, or clothes likewise, and the pure predominate, and he performs tayammum based on investigation, then it appears he was wrong, he must repeat the prayer. Or if a judge passes judgment by ijtihad, then a text to the contrary appears, his judgment is void. This is what Abu Yusuf analogized to the case of the book. The distinction for them is that knowledge of the pure garment, pure water, and the text is possible, so he did not perform the commanded act. We restricted it to the case where the pure predominate because if the impure predominate or they are equal, he does not investigate but performs tayammum. Thus in al-Mi'raj. In al-Nihayah, this ruling is made specific to vessels. As for impure clothes mixed with pure ones, he investigates absolutely, even if the impure are more or equal. al-Fath al-Qadir followed him. They took it from al-Sarakhsi's al-Mabsut from the chapter on investigation. The distinction between them is that necessity is not realized in vessels because... And it is one of al-Shafi'i's two opinions; his other opinion is like Abu Hanifa's. He said in Mushkilat Khuwarzadeh: His saying 'then it becomes clear he is rich, or Hashimi, or disbeliever' i.e. dhimmi, because there is consensus that if he were a musta'min or a harbi, repetition is obligatory. End quote. In al-Mukhtar, the permissibility of payment when it appears he is a harbi is explicitly stated. Its absolute wording in al-Kanz 'or a disbeliever' without restricting to dhimmi indicates permissibility. Thus in Sharh al-Kanz by 'Allamah Ibn al-Shubli, the teacher of the author of al-Bahr. His saying: (This is an occurrence in our time). al-Ramli said: There may be a distinction between the two cases: In the case from al-Mi'raj, the executor actually violated his duty because he was commanded to give to the poor and gave to the rich. In the occurrence, there was no actual violation because the command was to buy a house, and its being a waqf does not constitute violation, like the case of istihqaq. Evidence for this is in al-Tatarakhaniyyah from Nawadir Hisham: A man left three thousand dirhams and bequeathed to a man to buy a slave for him for one thousand dirhams and free him. The executor bought a slave for one thousand and freed him, then the slave was claimed by someone else. The executor is not liable. But if it appeared that the slave was free, the executor is liable. End quote. Also, a waqf house is generally subject to sale. They even distinguished between joining a free person to a slave and joining a waqf to private ownership: the defect spreads in the former but not the latter. The commentator said in the invalid section, in the issue of joining a waqf to ownership, the distinction between it and joining a free person to a slave: The waqf, after the court's order, although binding by consensus, it is subject to sale after its binding, either with the condition of exchange (istibdal) which is valid according to Abu Yusuf's opinion that is mufta bihi, or due to weakness of its revenue as is the two companions' view, or due to usurpation without possibility of recovery, then the supervisor may sell it as in Fatawa Qadikhan, or by the judgment of a Hanbali judge permitting its sale, because according to him selling a waqf is permissible. Sale. al-Bahr al-Raiq vol. 2 page 28.
Source pages p. 433

Commentary
This passage continues from the previous, now comparing the ruling of zakat with that of a will (wasiyyah) and other fiqh issues. The passage type is a ruling_with_proof, as it uses qiyas to draw distinctions. The main point is that in zakat, because it is a right of Allah (haqq Allah), the giver's intention (wus') is given weight, so if he intends to give zakat but mistakenly gives to a non-eligible recipient, the act may still be considered a voluntary gift. In contrast, a will (wasiyyah) is a right of humans (haqq al-'ibad), so the actual reality matters. If an executor gives to people he thinks are poor but they turn out rich, the executor is liable and must compensate from his own wealth. This is by consensus. The author then provides an analogy: if an executor buys a house intending to make it a waqf, pays the price, then it turns out the house was already a waqf for someone else, the executor is liable for the lost price. This is described as a contemporary occurrence. Next, a comparison is made with the case of mixing pure and impure vessels or clothes. If a person performs taharah (purification) by using water or tayammum based on investigation, and it turns out he was wrong, he must repeat the prayer. This is analogous to the zakat case because the person acted on a mistaken presumption. However, the author notes that there is a distinction: in the case of mixing, the knowledge of purity is possible, so the person is considered to have failed to perform the commanded act. The author then mentions a specific ruling from al-Nihayah that the investigation (taharrin) for mixed pure and impure items applies only to vessels, not to clothes. For clothes, one may investigate absolutely even if the impure predominate. This is the view of al-Nihayah and Fath al-Qadir, taken from al-Sarakhsi's al-Mabsut. The distinction is that necessity does not arise in vessels (because one can avoid using vessels), whereas for clothes, there is necessity to wear them. The passage then returns to the earlier point about giving zakat to a presumed disbeliever, clarifying that the consensus is that if the recipient turns out to be a musta'min or harbi, the zakat must be repeated. However, al-Mukhtar explicitly allows it when it appears he is a harbi, and the absolute wording in al-Kanz indicates permissibility. This is pointed out by Ibn al-Shubli. The author then addresses a comment by al-Ramli, who distinguishes between the executor giving to the rich (actual violation) and the executor buying a waqf (no actual violation). This is supported by a narration from Nawadir Hisham. Finally, the passage discusses the permissibility of selling waqf property under certain conditions, such as with a condition of exchange (istibdal) according to Abu Yusuf's opinion, or due to weak revenue, or due to usurpation, or by a Hanbali judge's ruling. This is a related fiqh discussion relevant to the occurrence mentioned.

Key Terms
حق الله (haqq Allah): A right that belongs to Allah, where intention (niyyah) is given weight and the act may be accepted based on the doer's best effort.
حق العباد (haqq al-'ibad): A right belonging to individuals, where actual reality must be fulfilled; the doer is liable if he fails to deliver correctly.
استبدال (istibdal): The process of selling a waqf property and replacing it with another property of similar value.
ضمان (damān): Liability, especially financial compensation. In this lesson, the executor must compensate from his own wealth if he misdirects the bequest.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the difference between zakat and will regarding the case when the recipient turns out to be rich?
A) In both, the payment is invalid and must be repeated.
B) In zakat, it may count as a voluntary gift; in will, the executor is liable.
C) In zakat, the executor is liable; in will, it counts as voluntary.
D) Both are valid if the giver had good intention.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, the distinction between haqq Allah (where intention suffices) and haqq al-'ibad (where exact fulfillment is required) is crucial for determining liability in cases of mistaken payment.
