Transferring Zakat: General Rule and Exceptions
Expands ruling on transferring zakat with exceptions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on transferring zakat and identify the six exceptions where transfer is not disliked.

Core Idea
Transferring zakat from its district is disliked, but six exceptions remove the dislike.

This passage expands on the ruling in Kanz al-Daqa'iq that transferring zakat is disliked. It explains the primary evidence from the hadith of Mu'adh and then enumerates six situations where the dislike is lifted. Understanding these exceptions is crucial for proper zakat distribution according to the Hanafi school.

Guided Path
1
State the general ruling
Identify the base ruling: transferring zakat from the district of collection is makruh tanzih (lightly disliked).
The ruling is from Kanz al-Daqa'iq.
2
Analyze the primary evidence
Look at the hadith of Mu'adh: 'Take it from their rich and return it to their poor.' Understand how the Hanafi school interprets the pronoun 'their' as referring to Muslims in general, not just the local poor.
This interpretation allows for transfer under certain conditions.
3
Enumerate the six exceptions
From the text, list the six cases where transfer is not disliked: 1) to a relative, 2) to a more needy person in another district, 3) out of piety to a poor person elsewhere, 4) from Dar al-Harb to Dar al-Islam, 5) accelerated zakat before the hawl, 6) accelerated zakat to a non-more-needy debtor (or as derived from the text).
The commentator explicitly says six exceptions; verify each one.
4
Understand the rationale behind each exception
For each exception, consider the underlying maslaha (benefit): combining charity with family ties, prioritizing greater need, following the practice of Mu'adh, preferring the poor of Dar al-Islam, or facilitating early payment.
This shows that Hanafi fiqh is not rigid but considers circumstances.
5
Apply the ruling to a modern scenario
Take a concrete case, e.g., sending zakat from a city to a relative in another city, and determine the ruling using the exceptions.
This reinforces the practical application of the principles.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله (المصنف وكره نقلها (الخ قال الرملي قال الزيلعي : فأما كراهة النقل لغير هذين فلقوله عليه الصلاة والسلام لمعاذ حين بعثه إلى اليمن اعلمهم أن عليهم صدقة تؤخذ من أغنيائهم ترد في فقرائهم (۱) من غير قيد بالمكان وأما حديث معاذ المشهور «خذها من أغنيائهم وردها في فقرائهم» فلا ينفي الصحة لأن الضمير راجع إلى فقراء المسلمين لا إلى أهل اليمن، أو لأنه ورد لبيان أنه عليه الصلاة والسلام لا طمع له في الصدقات، ولأنه صح عنه أنه كان يقول لأهل اليمن ائتوني بخميس أو لبيس - وهما الصغار من الثياب ـ آخذه منكم في الصدقة» مكان الشعير والذرة أهون عليكم وخير لأصحاب رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم. فإن كان في زمنه فهو تقرير، وإن كان في زمن أبي بكر فذاك إجماع لسكوتهم عنه، وعدم الكراهة في نقلها للقريب للجمع بين أجري الصدقة والصلة وللأحوج لأن المقصود منها سد خلة المحتاج، فمن كان أحوج كان أولى وليس عدم الكراهة منحصراً في هاتين لأنه لو نقلها إلى فقير في بلد آخر أورع وأصلح كما فعل معاذ رضي الله عنه لا يكره، ولهذا قيل : التصدق على العالم الفقير أفضل. كذا في المعراج ولا يكره نقلها من دار الحرب إلى فقراء دار الإسلام ولهذا ذكر في نوادر المبسوط : رجل مكث في دار الحرب سنين فعليه زكاة ماله الذي خلف ههنا ومال استفاده في دار الحرب لكن تصرف زكاة الكل إلى فقراء المسلمين الذين في دار الإسلام لأن فقراءهم أفضل من فقراء دار الحرب .اهـ. وكذا لا يكره نقل الزكاة المعجلة مطلقاً ولهذا قال الخلاصة : لا يكره أن ينقل زكاة ماله المعجلة قبل الحول لفقير غير أحوج ومديون اهـ. فاستثنى على هذا ستة.
His statement (the author: 'and it is disliked to transfer it [zakat]' etc.) — al-Ramli said: al-Zayla'i said: As for the dislike of transferring for other than these two, it is due to his statement (peace be upon him) to Mu'adh when he sent him to Yemen: 'Tell them that upon them is charity taken from their rich and returned to their poor' without qualification by place. As for the famous hadith of Mu'adh: 'Take it from their rich and return it to their poor,' it does not negate validity because the pronoun refers to the poor of the Muslims, not the people of Yemen; or because it came to clarify that he (peace be upon him) had no desire for charity; and because it is authentically reported that he used to say to the people of Yemen: 'Bring me a khamees or lubays — which are small garments — I will take it from you as charity' instead of barley and corn, easier for you and better for the Companions of the Messenger of Allah. If that was in his time, it was an endorsement; if in the time of Abu Bakr, then it was consensus due to their silence. And there is no dislike in transferring it to a relative to combine the rewards of charity and family ties, or to a more needy person because the purpose is to fill the need of the needy, and whoever is more needy is more deserving. Not disliking is not limited to these two because if he transfers it to a poor person in another country out of piety and betterment, as Mu'adh did, it is not disliked. Hence it is said: giving to a poor scholar is better. Similarly, it is not disliked to transfer it from Dar al-Harb to the poor of Dar al-Islam. Hence it is mentioned in Nawadir al-Mabsut: 'A man stayed in Dar al-Harb for years; he owes zakat on his wealth left here and wealth acquired in Dar al-Harb, but he must spend the zakat of all to the poor Muslims who are in Dar al-Islam because their poor are better than the poor of Dar al-Harb.' Similarly, it is not disliked to transfer accelerated zakat unconditionally. Hence al-Khulasa said: 'It is not disliked to transfer his accelerated zakat before the hawl to a poor person who is not more needy and is in debt.' So he made six exceptions.
Source pages p. 436

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on a ruling in Kanz al-Daqa'iq stating that transferring zakat from its place of collection is disliked. The commentator clarifies the exceptions and the underlying legal reasoning. The primary evidence is the Prophet's instruction to Mu'adh to take zakat from the rich of Yemen and give it to their poor, implying that distribution should be local. However, the hadith does not absolutely forbid transfer; the pronoun 'their' in 'their poor' refers generally to Muslims, so the ruling is not confined to Yemen. The commentator resolves the apparent conflict with the famous narrated instruction 'take it from their rich and return it to their poor' by explaining that the latter hadith was specific to the context of Mu'adh's mission and does not negate the validity of transfer. Rather, it was to clarify that the Prophet had no personal interest in the funds. The discussion then addresses exceptions: transferring to relatives is not disliked because it combines charity and upholding family ties; transferring to a more needy person is better because the goal of zakat is to fulfill need; transferring to a poor person in another country out of piety is not disliked, as Mu'adh himself did; transferring from Dar al-Harb to Dar al-Islam is allowed because the poor of Dar al-Islam are given preference due to their situation; and transferring accelerated zakat before the hawl to a non-more-needy person in debt is also allowed. The commentator notes that six exceptions are derived from these considerations.

Key Terms
كراهة النقل (karahat al-naql): Dislike of transferring zakat from the district of collection to another district without a valid cause.
دار الحرب (dar al-harb): Territory under non-Muslim rule where Islamic law is not enforced.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following is NOT an exception to the dislike of transferring zakat according to this passage?
A) Transferring to a relative
B) Transferring to a more needy person in the same city
C) Transferring from Dar al-Harb to Dar al-Islam
D) Transferring accelerated zakat to a debtor who is not more needy

Key Takeaway
Transferring zakat is generally disliked, but Hanafi fiqh allows it for specific beneficial purposes such as kinship, greater need, or transferring from hostile territory.

Prohibition of Begging When You Have Daily Sustenance
Explains the prohibition of asking for charity if one has enough food for the day, with exceptions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on begging when one has daily sustenance, explain the evidence from the hadith, and identify exceptions for the warrior and student of knowledge.

Core Idea
If you have enough food for today, asking for charity is haram; giving to you is a gift, not sadaqah.

This lesson explores a key fiqh ruling from Kanz al-Daqa'iq: one who has daily sustenance should not ask for charity. The commentator provides the prophetic definition of 'free of need' and discusses exceptions for those occupied with jihad or seeking knowledge. A subtle point: giving to such a beggar is considered a gift, not sadaqah, and thus not sinful.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and ruling
Determine what act is being ruled upon and its legal status. The act is asking (sual) for daily sustenance (qut yawmih). The ruling is haram when the asker already has sufficient food for the day.
2
Understand the evidence
Analyze the hadith from al-Tahawi. The Prophet defined 'free of need' (ghina) as having enough to feed one's family for the evening and morning. This defines the condition for prohibition.
The hadith is ahad but carries weight; it is used as primary evidence.
3
Apply the exceptions
The text lists exceptions: the warrior (ghazi) and, by analogy, the student of knowledge. They may ask even if they have daily sustenance because their occupation prevents earning. Also, the strong earner who lacks daily sustenance is prohibited because he can earn.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله (ولا يسأل من له قوت يومه) أي لا يحل سؤال قوت يومه لمن له قوت يومه الحديث. الطحاوي «من سأل الناس عن ظهر غنى فإنه يستكثر من جمر جهنم . قلت : يا رسول الله وما ظهر غنى؟ قال : أن يعلم أن عند أهله ما يغدّيهم وما يعشيهم قيدنا بسؤال القوت لأن سؤال الكسوة المحتاج إليها لا يكره ، وقيدنا بالسؤال لأن الأخذ لمن ملك أقل من نصاب جائز بلا سؤال كما قدمناه ، وقيد بمن له القوت لأن السؤال لمن لاقوت يومه له جائز ولا يرد عليه القوي المكتسب فإنه لا يحل سؤال القوت له إذا لم يكن له قوت يومه لأنه قادر بصحته واكتسابه على قوت اليوم فكأنه مالك له. واستثنى من ذلك في غاية البيان الغازي فإن طلب الصدقة جائز له وإن كان قوياً مكتسباً لاشتغاله بالجهاد عن الكسب ..هـ. وينبغي أن يلحق به طالب العلم لاشتغاله . الكسب بالعلم ولهذا قالوا : إن نفقته على أبيه وإن كان صحيحاً مكتسباً كما لو كان زمناً. وإذا حرم السؤال عليه إذا ملك قوت يومه فهل يحرم الإعطاء له إذا علم حاله؟ قال الشيخ أكمل الدين في شرح المشارق : وأما الدفع إلى مثل ذلك السائل عالماً بحاله فحكمه في القياس أن يأثم بذلك لأنه إعانة على الحرام لكنه يجعل هبة وبالهبة للغني أو لمن لا يكون محتاجاً إليه لا يكون آثماً اهـ . ويلزم عليه أن الصدقة على من ملك قوت يومه فقط تكون هبة حتى يثبت فيها أحكام الهبة من صحة الرجوع فإنهم قالوا : الصدقة على الغني هبة فله الرجوع بخلافها على الفقير وهو بعيد . فإن الظاهر أن مرادهم بالغني من ملك نصاباً لكن يمكن دفع القياس المذكور بأن الدفع ليس إعانة على الحرام لأن الحرمة في الابتداء إنما . بالسؤال وهو متقدم على الدفع ولا يكون الدفع إعانة إلا لو هي كان الأخذ هو المحرم فقط فليتأمل والله تعالى أعلم .
His statement (the author: 'And he who has his daily sustenance should not ask') means: It is not permissible for one who has his daily sustenance to ask for his daily sustenance. The hadith: al-Tahawi: 'Whoever asks people while being free of need will be gathering embers of Hell.' I said: 'O Messenger of Allah, what is being free of need?' He said: 'He knows that with his family he has what will feed them for the evening and the morning.' We restricted it to asking for sustenance because asking for needed clothing is not disliked. And we restricted it to asking because taking (without asking) is permissible for one who owns less than a nisab, as we mentioned. And we restricted it to one who has sustenance because asking is permissible for one who does not have daily sustenance. And the strong earner is not included in this permissibility because he is capable through his health and earning of obtaining daily sustenance, so it is as if he owns it. An exception is made in Ghayat al-Bayan for the warrior: it is permissible for him to ask for charity even if he is strong and earning, because he is occupied with jihad. It is appropriate to include the student of knowledge, as he is occupied with learning. Hence they said: his maintenance is due from his father even if he is healthy and earning, just as if he were disabled. And if asking is prohibited for one who owns daily sustenance, is it prohibited to give to such a person if one knows his condition? Shaykh Akmal al-Din said in Sharh al-Mashariq: 'As for paying to such a beggar knowing his condition, the ruling in qiyas is that he sins because it is assistance in sin. However, it is considered a gift, and by giving a gift to the rich or to one who does not need it, he does not sin.' This implies that sadaqah to one who only has daily sustenance would be considered a gift, with the consequence that the rules of gift apply, including the right of revocation, for they said: 'Sadaqah to the rich is a gift, so he may revoke it, unlike sadaqah to the poor.' This is far-fetched, for it is apparent that by 'rich' they mean one who owns a nisab. But one may rebut the aforementioned qiyas by saying that the payment is not assistance in sin because the sin lies in begging, which precedes the payment, and the payment is not assistance unless the taking itself is the prohibited act. So reflect. And Allah knows best.
Source pages p. 436

Commentary
This passage is a legal exposition of the matn line 'ولا يسأل من له قوت يومه' from Kanz al-Daqa'iq, which states that one who has sufficient food for a day should not ask for charity. The commentator provides the evidence from a hadith reported by al-Tahawi: the Prophet defined 'free of need' as having enough to feed his family for a day. The commentator refines the ruling by specifying that the prohibition is limited to asking for food; asking for clothing is not disliked if needed. Moreover, the prohibition is on asking, not on taking; taking without asking is permissible for those who own less than nisab. The prohibition applies only to one who has daily sustenance; it is permissible for one who lacks it to ask. The strong earner is treated as if he has daily sustenance because he can earn it. Exceptions are made for the warrior and, by analogy, for the student of knowledge, as they are occupied with important activities. A subtle issue arises: if asking is prohibited for such a person, is giving to him also prohibited? The commentator reports a view that giving would be a gift rather than sadaqah, thus avoiding sin, but this leads to implications about revocation. The discussion concludes by refuting the qiyas that giving is assistance in sin, since the sin is in the asking, not the giving.

Key Terms
قوت يومه (qut yawmih): Sufficient food for the day for oneself and one's family, according to the hadith interpretation.
صدقة على الغني (sadaqah 'ala al-ghani): Charity given to a rich person; considered a gift with right of revocation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the ruling for a strong earner who currently has no daily sustenance?
A) He is prohibited from asking because he is capable of earning
B) He is allowed to ask because he lacks daily sustenance
C) He is allowed to ask only for clothing
D) He is prohibited from asking even if he has daily sustenance

Key Takeaway
Begging is haram for one who has daily sustenance; exceptions are the warrior and student of knowledge.

Introduction to Sadaqat al-Fitr
Introduces the chapter on Sadaqat al-Fitr, defining terms and explaining its placement.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the definition of Sadaqat al-Fitr, explain why its chapter is placed between zakat and fasting, and identify the linguistic basis for the term 'sadaqah'.

Core Idea
Sadaqat al-Fitr is a wealth-based worship tied to both zakat and fasting; its name reflects sincerity.

This lesson introduces the chapter on Sadaqat al-Fitr from Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq. The author explains why this obligation is discussed between zakat and fasting: it is a financial worship like zakat, yet its obligation is triggered by completing the fast. You will also learn the linguistic meaning of 'sadaqah' and why the term 'fitr' is an Islamic technical term. Understanding these foundations will help you appreciate the unique character of this obligation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the topic and its placement
The passage opens with 'Chapter on Sadaqat al-Fitr'. Note that the author places it between the chapters on zakat and fasting. Ask yourself: what is the author signalling by this arrangement?
Placement in fiqh works often reflects conceptual relationships.
2
Analyze the rationale for placement
The text states two connections: to zakat because it is a wealth-based worship, and to fasting because its condition of obligation is breaking the fast after fasting. Understand that Sadaqat al-Fitr shares features with both chapters.
This dual connection justifies its interstitial position.
3
Examine the definition of sadaqah
The author defines sadaqah as a gift intended for divine reward, deriving from 'sidq' (truthfulness). Reflect on how the name indicates the sincerity of the giver's desire for reward, analogous to 'sadaq' (dower) in marriage.
Etymology reinforces the spiritual purpose of the act.
4
Recognize 'Fitr' as an Islamic term
The passage ends by noting that 'Fitr' is an Islamic technical term. Recognize that it refers specifically to the breaking of the fast at the end of Ramadan, giving the charity a defined temporal context.
This distinguishes it from other forms of charity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب صدقة الفطر لما كان لها مناسبة بالزكاة لكونها عبادة مالية، وبالصوم لأن شرط وجوبها الفطر بعد الصوم، ذكرها بينهما والصدقة العطية التي يراد بها المثوبة عند الله تعالى، وسميت بها لأنها تظهر صدق رغبة الرجل في تلك المثوبة كالصداق يظهر به صدق رغبة الزوج في المرأة. والفطر لفظ إسلامي)
Chapter on Sadaqat al-Fitr. Since it has a connection to zakat because it is a wealth-based worship, and to fasting because its condition of obligation is breaking the fast after fasting, he mentioned it between them. Sadaqah is a gift by which reward from Allah is intended, and it is called sadaqah because it manifests the truth of a person's desire for that reward, just as sadaq (dower) manifests the truth of the husband's desire for the woman. And 'Fitr' is an Islamic term.
Source pages p. 437

Commentary
This passage is the beginning of the chapter on Sadaqat al-Fitr. The author explains the rationale for placing this chapter between the chapters on zakat and fasting: it shares characteristics with both. Like zakat, it is a financial obligation; like fasting, its obligation is connected to the completion of Ramadan (the 'fitr' or breaking of the fast). The term sadaqah is defined as a gift given with the intention of divine reward, and its name derives from 'sidq' (truthfulness), as it demonstrates the sincerity of the giver. The term 'fitr' is noted to be an Islamic technical term, referring to the breaking of the fast at the end of Ramadan. This brief introduction sets the stage for the detailed rulings that follow in the chapter.

Key Terms
صدقة الفطر (sadaqat al-fitr): Charity given at the end of Ramadan, obligatory on every Muslim who has means beyond his basic needs.
الفطر (al-fitr): The breaking of the fast; here it refers to the occasion of Eid al-Fitr.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

C) Because it is compulsory for those who fast
D) Because it was revealed in a separate verse between zakat and fasting

Key Takeaway
Sadaqat al-Fitr is a unique financial obligation tied to the completion of Ramadan, named to reflect sincerity and placed between zakat and fasting due to its dual nature.

Asking for Charity with Sufficiency for the Day
Determining when asking for charity is prohibited based on hadith

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on asking for charity when one has sufficient provision for the day, identify the exceptions (warrior and student of knowledge), and explain the debate on whether giving to such a person is sinful.

Core Idea
Asking for charity when you have enough for the day is haram, but giving to such a person may not be sinful if considered a gift.

This passage expands on a famous hadith reported by al-Tahawi about the prohibition of asking for charity while having sufficiency for the day. The author explains the conditions that trigger the prohibition, the exceptions for those occupied with jihad or seeking knowledge, and then delves into a debated question: does the giver also sin? We will follow the author's reasoning, which combines textual evidence (dalil) and analogy (qiyas), to reach the preferred Hanafi position.

Guided Path
1
Identify the primary ruling from the hadith
Read the hadith: 'Whoever asks people while having sufficiency for the day asks for a piece of hellfire.' The apparent threat indicates prohibition (haram).
The hadith is ahad, so the ruling is zanni in thubut.
2
Determine the conditions that trigger the prohibition
The author restricts the hadith: (a) asking for food (not clothing), (b) asking (not taking without asking), (c) having provision for the day (qut). If any condition is missing, the prohibition may not apply.
Having qut means knowing one's family has enough for morning and evening meals.
3
List the exceptions to the prohibition
The author mentions the warrior (ghazi) as an exception because he is occupied with jihad, and analogizes the student of knowledge. Even if they are physically able to earn, they may ask because their occupation prevents earning.
This is based on necessity and public interest.
4
Analyze the debate on the giver's sin
Shaykh Akmal al-Din says that in qiyas, giving to such a person is assistance in sin (haram), but it is considered a gift, and giving a gift to a rich person is not sinful. The author finds this problematic because it would turn charity into a gift with revocation rights.
The author then offers a rebuttal: the sin is in the asking, not the giving, so giving is not aiding.
5
Evaluate the author's rebuttal and the preferred view
The author suggests that the prohibition is on asking, and giving does not aid that particular sin. He also notes a marginal comment that 'assistance' refers to causing future asking. The preferred view is that giving is not sinful.
This shows the Hanafi use of istihsan (juristic preference) over strict qiyas.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الطحاوي «من سأل الناس عن ظهر غنى فإنه يستكثر من جمر جهنم . قلت : يا رسول الله وما ظهر غنى؟ قال : أن يعلم أن عند أهله ما يغدّيهم وما يعشيهم قيدنا بسؤال القوت لأن سؤال الكسوة المحتاج إليها لا يكره ، وقيدنا بالسؤال لأن الأخذ لمن ملك أقل من نصاب جائز بلا سؤال كما قدمناه ، وقيد بمن له القوت لأن السؤال لمن لاقوت يومه له جائز ولا يرد عليه القوي المكتسب فإنه لا يحل سؤال القوت له إذا لم يكن له قوت يومه لأنه قادر بصحته واكتسابه على قوت اليوم فكأنه مالك له. واستثنى من ذلك في غاية البيان الغازي فإن طلب الصدقة جائز له وإن كان قوياً مكتسباً لاشتغاله بالجهاد عن الكسب ..هـ. وينبغي أن يلحق به طالب العلم لاشتغاله . الكسب بالعلم ولهذا قالوا : إن نفقته على أبيه وإن كان صحيحاً مكتسباً كما لو كان زمناً. وإذا حرم السؤال عليه إذا ملك قوت يومه فهل يحرم الإعطاء له إذا علم حاله؟ قال الشيخ أكمل الدين في شرح المشارق : وأما الدفع إلى مثل ذلك السائل عالماً بحاله فحكمه في القياس أن يأثم بذلك لأنه إعانة على الحرام لكنه يجعل هبة وبالهبة للغني أو لمن لا يكون محتاجاً إليه لا يكون آثماً اهـ . ويلزم عليه أن الصدقة على من ملك قوت يومه فقط تكون هبة حتى يثبت فيها أحكام الهبة من صحة الرجوع فإنهم قالوا : الصدقة على الغني هبة فله الرجوع بخلافها على الفقير وهو بعيد . فإن الظاهر أن مرادهم بالغني من ملك نصاباً لكن يمكن دفع القياس المذكور بأن الدفع ليس إعانة على الحرام لأن الحرمة في الابتداء إنما . بالسؤال وهو متقدم على الدفع ولا يكون الدفع إعانة إلا لو هي كان الأخذ هو المحرم فقط فليتأمل والله تعالى أعلم .
صرح :
قوله : (لكن يمكن دفع القياس المذكور الخ الظاهر أن المراد بالإعانة على السؤال أنه يكون سبباً لسؤاله بعد ذلك لا لهذا السؤال المخصوص، ثم رأيت العلامة المقدسي اعترضه بمثل ذلك .
Al-Tahawi reports: 'Whoever asks people while having sufficiency for the day, then he asks for a piece of hellfire.' I said: O Messenger of Allah, what is sufficiency for the day? He said: 'That he knows that his family has what will feed them for the morning and what will feed them for the evening.' We restricted it to asking for food because asking for clothing that one needs is not disliked. And we restricted it to asking because taking for one who owns less than a nisab is permissible without asking, as we have presented. And we restricted it to one who has provision because asking is permissible for one who has no provision for his day. And the strong earner is not included in this; indeed, it is not permissible for him to ask for food if he does not have provision for his day, because he is able with his health and earning to have provision for the day, so it is as if he owns it. An exception is made in Ghayat al-Bayan for the warrior, for seeking charity is permissible for him even if he is strong and earning, because he is occupied with jihad from earning. And it is fitting that the student of knowledge be joined to him because he is occupied with earning knowledge; and for this reason they said: his maintenance is upon his father even if he is healthy and earning, just as if he were disabled. And when asking is prohibited for him when he owns provision for his day, is giving to him prohibited when one knows his situation? Shaykh Akmal al-Din said in Sharh al-Mashariq: 'As for giving to such a questioner while knowing his situation, its ruling in qiyas is that one sins thereby because it is assistance in sin, but it is made a gift, and with a gift to a rich person or one who is not in need of it, one does not sin.' And it follows from this that charity to one who only owns provision for his day becomes a gift such that the rulings of gifts are established regarding it, including the validity of revocation, for they said: charity to the rich is a gift, so he may revoke it, unlike charity to the poor, and that is far-fetched. For the apparent meaning is that by 'rich' they mean one who owns a nisab, but it is possible to repel the mentioned qiyas by saying that the giving is not assistance in sin because the prohibition is initially only due to the asking, which precedes the giving, and the giving is not assistance unless the taking itself were the only prohibited act. So reflect, and Allah knows best.
He said:
His statement: 'But it is possible to repel the mentioned qiyas...' The apparent meaning is that 'assistance in asking' means that it becomes a cause for his asking afterwards, not for this particular asking. Then I saw al-Maqdisi objected to it with something similar.
Source pages p. 437

Commentary
This passage is an expansion on the hadith about a person who asks from people while having sufficiency for the day. The author first quotes the hadith from al-Tahawi: 'Whoever asks people while having sufficiency for the day asks for a piece of hellfire...' Then he explains the conditions: it is restricted to asking for food (qut) because asking for clothing that one needs is not disliked; restricted to asking because taking without asking is permissible for one who owns less than nisab; restricted to one who has the provision for the day because asking is permissible for one who has no provision. Then he discusses exceptions: a warrior (ghazi) on jihad and analogously a student of knowledge, and then discusses whether giving to such a person is prohibited. He cites Akmal al-Din's view from Sharh al-Mashariq that giving is theoretically sinful as aiding in sin, but is considered a gift, and giving a gift to a rich person is not sinful. The author then raises a consequence: that sadaqah to one who has only a day's provision would be a gift, allowing revocation, which is far-fetched because the term 'rich' (ghani) usually means one who owns nisab. He then offers a possible rebuttal that giving is not aiding in sin because the sin is in asking, which precedes giving, and giving is not aiding unless taking is the only sin. Finally, a marginal note clarifies that the 'aid' refers to causing future asking, not this particular instance.

Key Terms
ظهر غنى (zahr ghina): Having sufficient provision for the day, i.e., knowing that one's family has food for the morning and evening meals.
القوت (al-qut): The food necessary for survival; here it is the daily sustenance.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, who is an exception to the prohibition of asking for charity even if they have the physical ability to earn?
A) A wealthy merchant
B) A warrior engaged in jihad
C) A disabled person
D) A traveler without provisions

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school prohibits asking for charity when one has sufficient daily provision, but permits giving to such a person, based on a distinction between the sin of asking and the permissibility of giving.
